_Thought for 28s_

Here is the next push.

I think the sharpest thing we can now prove is a clean **conditional exclusion theorem on a good patch** that meshes the two strands together:

- the cancellation-chart blow-up picture from the other chats, and
- the involutive / quotient-visible compression from the residual-corner attack.

The result is not a full unconditional closure, but it isolates the remaining burden very tightly.

---

## 1. The right local theorem now

Work on a compact good patch
\[
J\Subset \{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M:=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

Use the collision-cancellation chart
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\lambda=-\frac{a_2}{a_1},
\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1,
\]
or, better,
\[
\omega=\frac{\lambda-1}{\lambda+1}.
\]
After polar blow-up
\[
(\mu,\delta)=\rho(\cos\theta,\sin\theta),
\]
the first-order projective source direction is
\[
\Theta_{m,\theta}=\cos\theta\,D_m+\sin\theta\,D_m'.
\]

Now assume the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses admit the blow-up asymptotics
\[
\mathcal C_3(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,C(m,\theta)+o(\rho),
\qquad
\mathcal C_5(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,K(m,\theta)+o(\rho),
\]
uniformly on compact subsets, with the one-pair linearizations
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m).
\]

Then:

### Proposition
On \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), the equations
\[
C(m,\theta)=0,\qquad K(m,\theta)=0
\]
have no simultaneous solution.

### Proof
Since \(c(m)\neq 0\), the cubic equation determines a unique projective direction:
\[
(\cos\theta:\sin\theta)=(c'(m):-c(m)).
\]
Substituting into \(K\) gives
\[
K(m,\theta_*(m))
=
c'(m)A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)-c(m)(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)
=
-\,M(m),
\]
up to the harmless projective normalization factor. Since \(M(m)\neq 0\) on \(J\), this cannot vanish.

So cubic and quintic cannot vanish simultaneously. ∎

That is the core local determinant obstruction.

---

## 2. Transversality sharpened

This can be improved to a robust gap theorem.

At the cubic-killing direction,
\[
\partial_\theta C(m,\theta)
=
-c(m)\sin\theta+c'(m)\cos\theta.
\]
At the unique root \(C(m,\theta_*(m))=0\),
\[
|\partial_\theta C(m,\theta_*(m))|
=
\sqrt{c(m)^2+c'(m)^2}.
\]
On a compact \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0\}\), this is bounded below by a positive constant.

Likewise
\[
|K(m,\theta_*(m))|
\asymp |M(m)|
\]
is bounded below on \(J\Subset\{M\neq 0\}\).

Therefore there is a uniform gap:
there exist constants \(\eta,\varepsilon>0\) such that on \(J\),
if
\[
|C(m,\theta)|<\eta,
\]
then
\[
|K(m,\theta)|>\varepsilon.
\]

So under the uniform blow-up asymptotics above, for all sufficiently small \(\rho\),
\[
\mathcal C_3(m,\rho,\theta)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathcal C_5(m,\rho,\theta)\neq 0.
\]

This yields:

### Conditional good-patch exclusion theorem
If the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses linearize on the cancellation blow-up to the one-pair functionals \(C\) and \(K\) above, then there are **no close cancelling bad two-point cores** on any compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
\]

This is, I think, the sharpest local theorem currently in reach.

---

## 3. How this meshes with the residual \(K_v=0\) corner

This local theorem dovetails well with the residual-corner work.

From the residual attack we already had:

- on
  \[
  \{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\},
  \]
  the branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal;
- \(Q(t)=\widehat Q(t^2)\);
- every parity-normalized finite transport jet is quotient-visible;
- on the quartic-sextic rung, the finite transport state has local rank \(1\);
- any swap-even analytic local defect on the cancellation chart factors as
  \[
  E=\delta^2\,\mathcal H(m,\kappa,\delta^2),
  \qquad
  \kappa=\frac{2\omega}{\delta},
  \]
  and along the involutive branch,
  \[
  E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\widetilde H(t^2),
  \]
  or on the quartic-sextic rung,
  \[
  E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\mathcal G(Q(t)).
  \]

So the local good-patch blow-up theorem and the residual-corner parity theorem are killing different things:

- the blow-up theorem kills the **linear projective source-direction lane** on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\);
- the involutive/quotient theorem kills hidden finite-order local reset and pushes any surviving local defect into a **quadratic quotient-visible law**.

Put differently: the first theorem says “the tangent-direction family already fails on good patches,” while the second says “even if something survives past that, it must enter only through a quotient-visible quadratic-or-higher package datum.”

That is a clean division of labor.

---

## 4. New structural finding

I think the strongest new structural conclusion from combining these is:

> On a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), any still-live two-point obstruction cannot come from first-order cancellation blow-up geometry at all.

Because if the corrected witnesses linearize functorially, the unique cubic-killing direction is already quintic-nonzero. So there is no first-order local bad branch there.

That means:

- septic is **not** the first local obstruction on good patches;
- the first genuinely live obstruction, if any, must come either from failure of blow-up functoriality itself, or from more global/package-level coincidence data.

That is a useful clarification.

---

## 5. Negative finding

This also gives a sharper negative result against a tempting but wrong strategy.

It is not enough to say “the residual corner is quotient-visible and parity-even, therefore we are done.” That only shows any analytic local defect is at least quadratic in the cancellation variables. It does **not** identify the coefficient, and by itself does not prove vanishing.

The good-patch blow-up theorem shows where the real next burden lies:

- either prove the corrected witnesses really do linearize to \(C\) and \(K\),
- or, if they do not, identify the first package-level datum that causes the failure.

So the remaining difficulty is no longer raw local geometry. It is the **functoriality of the corrected construction under cancellation blow-up**.

---

## 6. Best current frontier

I think the frontier now splits very cleanly into three theorem targets.

### (a) Best local theorem target
Prove uniform cancellation-blow-up asymptotics
\[
\mathcal C_3=\rho\,C+o(\rho),\qquad
\mathcal C_5=\rho\,K+o(\rho)
\]
on compact good patches.

If this lands, close cancelling bad two-point cores are locally excluded on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
\]

### (b) Best residual-corner theorem target
Prove the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects are analytic swap-even functions of the collision chart and vanish on the collision locus.

If this lands, the exact fixed-shear residual corner is excluded by
\[
E_{12}^{(3)},E_{12}^{(5)}=O((h_1-h_2)^2).
\]

### (c) Best stronger/global theorem target
Prove collision-functoriality / same reduced image germ at coincidence for the corrected block triple.

If this lands, the honest order-7 obstruction
\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\in \mathfrak f/\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]
collapses formally.

---

## 7. Bottom line

The strongest honest summary from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{On }J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\text{ the first-order cancellation blow-up lane is dead if the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses linearize functorially.}
}
\]

Combined with the residual-corner quotient-visible compression, this means the remaining two-point burden is now very specifically package-level:

- prove blow-up functoriality of the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses on the cancellation chart;
- or prove collision-functoriality / same reduced image germ at coincidence;
- or isolate the first genuinely relational quotient-septic obstruction that survives after all lower structure is compressed away.

That is the sharpest math attack I see right now.

I think the next clean push is to fuse the **good-patch cancellation blow-up theorem** with the **residual \(K_v=0\) quotient-visible theorem** into a single local dichotomy. That gives a sharper frontier than either piece alone.

Here is the statement.

Assume we are on a compact good patch
\[
J\Subset \{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M:=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
and suppose a close bad \(2\)-point branch exists. Then, after the work from the other chats and the existing draft reductions, there are only two local ways it can try to survive:

1. it lies in the ordinary cancellation blow-up regime and is governed at first order by the projective source direction
\[
\Theta_{m,\theta}=\cos\theta\,D_m+\sin\theta\,D_m',
\]
or

\[
\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\},
\]
where the branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal and the local defect package is forced into quotient-visible even structure. turn12file1

The new point is that these two lanes can be attacked together as follows.

If the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses satisfy the cancellation blow-up asymptotics
\[
\mathcal C_3(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,C(m,\theta)+o(\rho),\qquad
\mathcal C_5(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,K(m,\theta)+o(\rho),
\]
with
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m),
\]
then on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\) there is a uniform local exclusion of the non-fixed-shear cancellation lane, because the unique cubic-killing direction is already quintic-nonzero:
\[
C(m,\theta)=0 \Longrightarrow K(m,\theta)\neq 0.
\]
Indeed, as before,
\[
(\cos\theta:\sin\theta)=(c'(m):-c(m))
\]
and then
\[
K(m,\theta_*(m))\asymp -M(m)\neq 0.
\]
So the first-order cancellation lane is dead on a good patch if that blow-up functoriality holds.

once the actual cubic/quintic defects are even under the involution and vanish on coincident atoms, one gets

\]
hence
\[
E_{12}^{(3)},E_{12}^{(5)}=O((h_1-h_2)^2),
\]
and the defect-closeness corollary excludes the branch. In the stronger form developed in the attack, any analytic swap-invariant local scalar in the cancellation variables and descended transport state is automatically quadratic in the involutive branch parameter, so ordinary analytic local scalar obstructions cannot keep that corner alive either. 

So the combined theorem target is really this:

> **Conditional local dichotomy exclusion theorem.**  
> On a compact good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), assume:
> 1. the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses linearize on the cancellation blow-up to the one-pair functionals \(C\) and \(K\); and
> 2. on the residual exact fixed-shear corner, the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects belong to the analytic swap-invariant local class already isolated by the involutive reduction.
>
> Then no close bad two-point branch can persist on \(J\).

The proof is just the dichotomy above: the non-fixed-shear cancellation lane dies by the \(C/K/M\) determinant obstruction, while the fixed-shear residual lane dies by evenness-plus-diagonal-vanishing and the good-patch closeness theorem.

That is already a useful structural synthesis, because it says the only genuinely remaining burden on a good patch is to prove two functoriality statements:

- blow-up functoriality for the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses away from the exact fixed-shear corner;
- analytic swap-invariant package behavior on the exact fixed-shear corner.

Everything else local has been squeezed out.

There is also a sharper negative finding hidden in this synthesis.

If a close bad two-point branch still survives on a good patch after all current reductions, then at least one of the following must fail:

- the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses do **not** linearize to the one-pair blow-up functionals \(C\) and \(K\);
- or the actual residual-corner defect package is **not** an ordinary analytic swap-invariant local scalar in the cancellation / amplitude chart;
- or the obstruction is not local at all, but truly package-level and global, such as same reduced image germ / quotient-septic / provenance-sensitive data.

That is much sharper than “something remains open.”

A second push I think is worth recording is this: the good-patch determinant obstruction shows that **\(M\)** is now the exact local separator between lower-order local exclusion and higher-order coincidence geometry. On
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
quintic already kills the unique cubic-cancelling direction, so septic should not be the first local obstruction. Therefore the order-\(7\) quotient class
\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\in \mathfrak f/\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]
should now be viewed as living on the complementary burden: exceptional points where \(M=0\), or global coincidence / same-image-germ / collision-functoriality questions. That gives a very clean division of labor between quintic and septic. turn12file1

So my updated handback is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{On a good patch }J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\text{ every local two-point lane is conditionally dead except for failure of functoriality itself.}
}
\]

Concretely, the remaining attack should now focus on proving one of two theorem schemata, because either one would be decisive locally:

1. **Cancellation blow-up functoriality theorem**
\[
\mathcal C_3=\rho\,C+o(\rho),\qquad \mathcal C_5=\rho\,K+o(\rho)
\]
uniformly on compact good patches;

2. **Residual-corner analytic swap-invariance theorem**
show the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects on
\[
\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}
\]
are analytic swap-invariant local scalars vanishing on collision.

If both land, the local \(2\)-point problem on good patches is essentially done, and the only serious remaining burden is the genuinely global / quotient-septic / same-image-germ package problem.

\Theta_{m,\theta}=\cos\theta\,D_m+\sin\theta\,D_m',
\]
while on the residual exact fixed-shear side, the natural local variables are the involutive quotient variables
\[
u=t^2,\qquad Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\]
together with the swap chart
\[
(\nu,\delta),\qquad \nu=\log\Lambda,\quad \Lambda=-a_2/a_1,
\]
where the involution is
\[
(\nu,\delta)\mapsto(-\nu,-\delta).
\]

So if the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses are well behaved, they should have **compatible first-order models** in these two charts. The attack now says:

- in the cancellation blow-up chart, the first-order model should be linear in the projective direction and given by the one-pair functionals \(C\) and \(K\);
- in the involutive swap chart, every analytic swap-invariant scalar is automatically a function of the quadratic invariants
\[
\nu^2,\qquad \nu\delta,\qquad \delta^2,
\]
so there is no linear term at all.

That means there is only one way these two pictures can both be true:

the linear blow-up model must vanish precisely on the fixed-shear involutive sublane. In other words, the exact fixed-shear corner must be the locus where the projective blow-up law degenerates from linear order to quadratic order.

This gives a very sharp theorem target.

---

## 1. Compatibility theorem target

Let \(\mathcal C_3,\mathcal C_5\) denote the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses near a close bad two-point core.

Assume:

1. away from the exact fixed-shear involutive corner, they admit blow-up asymptotics
\[
\mathcal C_3(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,C(m,\theta)+o(\rho),
\qquad
\mathcal C_5(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,K(m,\theta)+o(\rho),
\]
with
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m);
\]

2. on the exact fixed-shear involutive corner they are analytic swap-invariant local scalars in the \((\nu,\delta)\) chart and vanish on collision.

Then the only possible failure of a uniform first-order blow-up law occurs along sequences whose projective directions converge to the **fixed-shear degeneration direction**, i.e. the direction where the first-order blow-up coefficient itself vanishes and the branch falls into the involutive quadratic regime.

So the problem is no longer “prove blow-up asymptotics everywhere.” It is:

> identify the first-order degeneration locus of the blow-up model, and show it is exactly the already-classified fixed-shear corner.

That is a narrower target.

---

## 2. What the degeneration locus must look like

Suppose the corrected cubic witness has blow-up model \(C(m,\theta)\). On a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0\}\), the cubic-killing direction is unique:
\[
(\cos\theta:\sin\theta)=(c'(m):-c(m)).
\]
So if a close bad branch exists and is governed by a first-order blow-up law, it is forced into that unique direction.

Now the good-patch determinant theorem says that on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad M=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
the quintic model does not vanish there:
\[
K(m,\theta_*(m))\neq 0.
\]
Therefore the only way the branch can survive locally on a good patch is if the assumed first-order blow-up law itself **breaks down** at the putative bad sequence.

But the involutive residual-corner analysis tells us exactly how such a breakdown can happen in a controlled way: on the exact fixed-shear corner, swap parity kills the entire linear layer, and the witnesses start at quadratic order in the collision variables.

So the linear blow-up law can fail only by collapsing into the involutive swap-even quadratic law.

This is the new local dichotomy:

### Local dichotomy
On a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), a close bad branch must satisfy exactly one of the following:

1. **first-order regime:** the corrected witnesses have linear blow-up asymptotics, hence are excluded by the \(C/K/M\) determinant argument;

2. **degenerate regime:** the linear blow-up layer disappears, and the branch falls into the exact fixed-shear involutive corner, where every analytic swap-invariant scalar starts at quadratic order.

So the remaining local problem is not arbitrary. It is the problem of showing that **every degeneration of the linear blow-up law is already captured by the fixed-shear involutive classification**.

That is the sharpest new structural finding from this round.

---

## 3. Why this is useful

This matters because it reduces the local theorem target from a global statement about all cancellation blow-up directions to a statement about the **degeneration set of first-order blow-up**.

Instead of proving:
\[
\mathcal C_3=\rho C+o(\rho),\qquad \mathcal C_5=\rho K+o(\rho)
\quad\text{uniformly everywhere,}
\]
it would be enough to prove:

- wherever the first-order model fails to control the branch, the sequence is forced into
\[
\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\},
\]
and then the residual-corner quadratic theory applies.

That is a much more believable theorem.

---

## 4. A concrete theorem schema

Here is the cleanest version.

> **Conditional degeneration-capture theorem.**  
> Let \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). Suppose a close bad two-point sequence exists on \(J\). Then either:
> 
> - after cancellation blow-up, the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses have nondegenerate first-order limits \(C,K\), in which case the sequence is excluded by \(C=0\Rightarrow K\neq 0\);
> 
> or
> 
> - the first-order blow-up coefficients degenerate, and the sequence is forced into the exact fixed-shear residual corner \(\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}\), where every analytic swap-invariant local witness is quadratic in the branch parameter and hence falls under the residual-corner closeness theorem.

If one could prove this theorem, the local \(2\)-point problem on good patches would be essentially finished.

---

## 5. Negative finding

This also tells us where not to keep pushing.

It is probably not productive to search for yet another scalar contradiction inside the residual corner itself. That corner is already too compressed: quotient-diagonal, rank-one finite transport state, no hidden finite-order reset, analytic swap-invariant local scalars automatically quadratic.

The real local missing theorem is instead a **capture theorem for failure of first-order blow-up functoriality**:
if the blow-up linearization fails, the branch must already be in the fixed-shear involutive corner.

That is more precise than anything I had before.

---

## 6. Best next attack

So the next thing I would attack is:

### Capture-of-degeneration theorem
Show that any close bad two-point sequence on a good patch for which the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses do not admit the expected nondegenerate first-order blow-up law must satisfy
\[
S\to 0,\qquad K_v\to 0,
\]
hence is captured by the residual exact fixed-shear corner.

This would unify the two live local routes into one theorem and leave only the genuinely global / quotient-septic / same-image-germ burden.

That feels like the sharpest current local target.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:47:46)

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next push. I think the cleanest advance is to make the “capture-of-degeneration” idea more precise and isolate what would actually have to be proved.

The right statement is not yet a theorem, but it is now narrow enough to be a serious target.

---

Let a close bad two-point sequence on a good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}
\]
be given, with cancellation-chart variables
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\lambda=-\frac{a_2}{a_1},\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1.
\]
After blow-up, write
\[
(\mu,\delta)=\rho(\cos\theta,\sin\theta),
\qquad
\Theta_{m,\theta}=\cos\theta\,D_m+\sin\theta\,D_m'.
\]
The other chats have already isolated the first-order one-pair models
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m),
\]
and shown that on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\),
\[
C=0\Longrightarrow K\neq 0.
\]
So any bad branch can only survive if the expected first-order blow-up law itself degenerates. turn12file1

The next attack is to understand what “degenerates” can really mean.

## 1. A concrete degeneration functional

Define the normalized first-order cancellation source
\[
\Xi:=\frac{D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}}{\sqrt{\mu^2+\delta^2}}.
\]
At the formal level,
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu D_m-\delta D_m'+O((|\mu|+|\delta|)^2),
\]
so after dividing by \(\rho=\sqrt{\mu^2+\delta^2}\),
\[
\Xi
=
-\cos\theta\,D_m-\sin\theta\,D_m'+O(\rho).
\]
Thus \(\Xi\) converges projectively to \(-\Theta_{m,\theta}\) whenever the first-order blow-up is nondegenerate. That is exactly the cancellation-chart model in a normalized form. 

Now the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses evaluated on this normalized direction should have leading limits
\[
\mathcal C_3/\rho \to C(m,\theta),\qquad
\mathcal C_5/\rho \to K(m,\theta).
\]
So the failure of first-order blow-up functoriality is equivalent to the failure of the normalized source direction \(\Xi\) to control the corrected witnesses at first order.

That suggests a first theorem target:

> **Normalized-source control theorem.**  
> Away from the exact fixed-shear degeneration locus, the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses are continuous to first order in the normalized cancellation source \(\Xi\).

If that theorem held, the good-patch determinant argument would close the nondegenerate lane immediately.

## 2. What must happen if normalized-source control fails

Suppose normalized-source control fails along a bad sequence. Then there is no stable nonzero first-order limit carried by \(\Xi\). In practical terms, the corrected witnesses are seeing something smaller than the formal \(\rho\)-scale.

But from the residual-corner attack, we now know exactly what happens when the linear scale disappears: the branch becomes involutive / quotient-diagonal, the finite transport state descends to \(u=t^2\), and every analytic swap-invariant local scalar starts at quadratic order in the cancellation variables. In particular, on that corner the linear layer is parity-killed. 

So the only plausible way first-order blow-up control can fail is that the sequence is approaching the unique place where linear order is not the right scale anymore, namely the exact fixed-shear corner
\[
\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}.
\]

This leads to the refined capture statement:

> **Refined capture conjecture.**  
> If a close bad two-point sequence on a good patch does not admit a nondegenerate first-order blow-up model governed by \(\Xi\), then necessarily
> \[
> S\to 0,\qquad K_v\to 0.
> \]
> Hence the sequence is captured by the residual exact fixed-shear corner.

That is better than the vague “either blow-up works or residual corner” dichotomy. It says the **only** place first-order scale can collapse is where both geometric degeneracies occur.

## 3. Why \(S\) and \(K_v\) are the right capture markers

This is where the two streams of work really mesh.

From the overlap geometry, close exact mixed two-point bad branches can only accumulate on
\[
\{S=0\}\cup\{K_v=0\},
\]
and this is already sharpened into three lanes:
\[
(K_v=0,\ S\neq 0)\ \text{mixed low-order,}
\]
\[
(S=0,\ K_v\neq 0)\ \text{transfers to nonmixed 4-point,}
\]
\[
(S=0,\ K_v=0)\ \text{residual exact fixed-shear corner.}
\]
So \(S\) and \(K_v\) are not arbitrary functions; they are exactly the local markers of which geometric lane one is in. 

That means the desired capture theorem can be split into two narrower claims:

1. if first-order blow-up degenerates, then \(K_v\to 0\);
2. if first-order blow-up degenerates and \(K_v\to 0\), then either \(S\not\to 0\) and one is already in the mixed low-order obstruction lane, or else \(S\to 0\) and one is in the residual corner.

The second part is almost just bookkeeping using the existing lane split. The hard part is the first: showing that collapse of the \(\rho\)-scale forces \(K_v\to 0\).

## 4. A plausible route to \(K_v\to 0\)

Here is the most concrete mathematical route I see.

The cancellation blow-up direction is built from the first derivative of the source family:
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu D_m-\delta D_m'+\cdots
\]
So if the first-order law genuinely survives, the local two-atom branch is controlled by the span
\[
\operatorname{span}\{D_m,D_m'\}.
\]
If that first-order law collapses, the first derivative direction is no longer separating the two atoms at the level seen by the corrected witnesses.

But the fixed-shear quantity \(K_v\) is exactly measuring the failure of the first transport/shear direction to stay transverse in the local overlap geometry. That is why the residual corner is \(K_v=0\): the first-order shear has frozen out there. This is already implicit in the exact fixed-shear name and the ladder package. 

So the conjectural mechanism is:

- nondegenerate first-order cancellation blow-up needs nonzero shear transversality;
- loss of first-order control therefore forces shear transversality to collapse;
- hence \(K_v\to 0\).

I do not yet have a formal proof of that implication from the current draft ingredients alone, but structurally it is now the right local theorem.

## 5. What this buys if proved

If one proves
\[
\text{failure of first-order blow-up}\Longrightarrow K_v\to 0,
\]
then together with the already-established lane split, every close bad sequence on a good patch falls into exactly one of two cases:

### Case A: first-order blow-up survives
Then
\[
\mathcal C_3/\rho\to C,\qquad \mathcal C_5/\rho\to K,
\]
and on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\),
\[
C=0\Longrightarrow K\neq 0.
\]
So the branch is excluded.

### Case B: first-order blow-up collapses
Then \(K_v\to 0\), so the sequence is forced into the fixed-shear side. If also \(S\to 0\), one lands in the residual involutive corner, where analytic swap-invariant local defects are quadratic and the residual-corner closeness theorem applies. If \(S\not\to 0\), one is in the already-classified mixed low-order lane. 

So proving that single implication would essentially finish the local good-patch story.

## 6. Strongest current structural finding

The strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

> The only serious local missing theorem is no longer a contradiction theorem inside some already-known lane. It is a **scale-capture theorem**:
> \[
> \text{if the linear cancellation blow-up scale fails, the branch is forced into fixed-shear geometry.}
> \]

That is much sharper than the earlier formulation.

It also explains the division of labor:

- \(C,K,M\) govern the nondegenerate linear cancellation scale;
- \(K_v\) detects collapse of that scale into fixed-shear behavior;
- \(S\) then distinguishes whether one is in the residual corner or already transferred into another lane.

## 7. Negative finding

This also tells us what not to attack anymore.

It does not look useful to search for another ad hoc scalar contradiction on the nondegenerate blow-up side: the determinant obstruction already identified the unique cubic-killing direction and showed quintic blocks it on good patches.

It also does not look useful to squeeze more internal parity facts out of the residual corner alone: that corner is already maximally compressed in the current package.

The real missing theorem is the **bridge between scales**:
from failure of the linear cancellation model to \(K_v=0\).

## 8. Best next theorem target

So the next theorem I would explicitly attack is:

> **Linear-scale collapse implies fixed-shear theorem.**  
> Let \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). Suppose a close bad two-point sequence on \(J\) does not admit a nondegenerate first-order cancellation blow-up law in the normalized source direction \(\Xi\). Then
> \[
> K_v\to 0.
> \]

A slightly stronger version would be:

> **Degeneration capture theorem.**  
> If the normalized first-order cancellation source fails to control the corrected witnesses, then
> \[
> S\to 0,\qquad K_v\to 0.
> \]

The weaker version is probably the right first target.

That is the sharpest math attack I see now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:49:10)

I’m pushing directly on the bridge theorem now: whether collapse of the linear cancellation scale can be converted into a concrete vanishing statement that forces the branch into the fixed-shear lane, rather than just a heuristic “something degenerated.”

_Thought for 8s_

I think the next real move is to sharpen the bridge theorem from a slogan into an algebraic criterion.

The right target is no longer just

\[
\text{“failure of linear blow-up”} \Rightarrow K_v\to 0.
\]

It is this more concrete statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if the normalized cancellation source becomes asymptotically invisible to all first-order corrected witnesses, then the branch tangent lies in the fixed-shear kernel.}
}
\]

That is closer to something one could actually prove from the draft’s geometry.

---

## 1. Normalize the cancellation tangent properly

On the cancellation chart,
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1,\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,
\]
we have the source expansion
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu D_m-\delta D_m'+O((|\mu|+|\delta|)^2).
\]
Write
\[
\rho:=\sqrt{\mu^2+\delta^2},
\qquad
(\mu,\delta)=\rho(\cos\theta,\sin\theta),
\]
and define the normalized first-order source direction
\[
\Xi:=\frac{D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}}{\rho}.
\]
Then
\[
\Xi=-\cos\theta\,D_m-\sin\theta\,D_m'+O(\rho).
\]

So the first-order cancellation geometry lives in the 2-plane
\[
\mathcal P_m:=\operatorname{span}\{D_m,D_m'\}.
\]

The corrected first-order blow-up law is exactly the statement that the corrected witnesses, after dividing by \(\rho\), converge to linear functionals on this plane:
\[
\mathcal C_3/\rho \to C(m,\theta),\qquad
\mathcal C_5/\rho \to K(m,\theta),
\]
with
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m).
\]
On a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), these two functionals already exclude any nondegenerate bad branch, because
\[
C=0 \Longrightarrow K\neq 0.
\]

So the only live possibility is: the normalized source direction \(\Xi\) exists, but the corrected witnesses do **not** see it at first order.

That is the exact failure mode we need to capture.

---

## 2. What “invisible to first order” should mean

A mathematically clean failure notion is:

there exists a close bad sequence with \(\rho_n\to 0\) such that
\[
\frac{\mathcal C_3^{(n)}}{\rho_n}\to 0,
\qquad
\frac{\mathcal C_5^{(n)}}{\rho_n}\to 0,
\]
after passing to a subsequence and expressing everything in the cancellation chart.

Equivalently, the normalized tangent \(\Xi_n\) is asymptotically annihilated by the corrected cubic and quintic first-order witnesses.

Since on the formal tangent plane \(\mathcal P_m\), those first-order witnesses are represented by
\[
L_3(\alpha D_m+\beta D_m')=\alpha\,c(m)+\beta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
L_5(\alpha D_m+\beta D_m')=\alpha\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\beta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m),
\]
this says the projected tangent direction has moved into the common near-kernel of the first-order corrected functionals.

But on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), there is no exact common kernel in \(\mathcal P_m\). That is precisely what \(M\neq 0\) means. So if both corrected first-order responses vanish asymptotically, the tangent cannot remain nondegenerate inside \(\mathcal P_m\).

Therefore the only plausible interpretation is that the actual branch tangent is ceasing to be represented faithfully by the source plane \(\mathcal P_m\) at first order. In other words, the first-order source plane is losing transversality to the branch geometry.

That is exactly where \(K_v\) should enter.

---

## 3. The bridge lemma we really want

The right missing theorem is something like this.

### Bridge Lemma (target form)
Let \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), and let a close bad two-point sequence be given with normalized cancellation tangents \(\Xi_n\). Suppose
\[
\frac{\mathcal C_3^{(n)}}{\rho_n}\to 0,
\qquad
\frac{\mathcal C_5^{(n)}}{\rho_n}\to 0.
\]
Then
\[
K_v(h_n)\to 0.
\]

Interpretation: if the corrected cubic/quintic package cannot detect the normalized cancellation tangent at linear order, then the tangent direction has fallen into the fixed-shear kernel.

This is much sharper than the earlier heuristic “linear-scale collapse implies fixed-shear.”

---

## 4. Why this is plausible from the existing structure

There are really only two first-order tangent pictures left in the combined handback.

The first is the nondegenerate cancellation picture, where the branch is modeled at first order by
\[
\Xi\sim -\cos\theta\,D_m-\sin\theta\,D_m'
\]
and the corrected witnesses see that tangent through the one-pair functionals \(C\) and \(K\). 

The second is the exact fixed-shear residual picture, where the branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal, \(Q(t)=\widehat Q(t^2)\), every analytic swap-invariant scalar starts at quadratic order, and the entire linear layer is parity-killed. 

There is not supposed to be a third first-order tangent regime.

So if the linear cancellation model disappears, the only available replacement in the current geometry is the fixed-shear one. That is why the bridge lemma is plausible.

More concretely, \(K_v=0\) is not just another vanishing condition; it is the defining marker that the shear/transverse linear direction has frozen out. So it is exactly the condition one expects to characterize the failure of first-order cancellation control.

---

## 5. A stronger algebraic reformulation

There is an even sharper way to package it.

Let \(\mathscr T_n\) denote the actual first-order tangent line of the close bad branch in the relevant reduced configuration space, and let \(\mathcal P_{m_n}\) denote the model source plane spanned by \(\{D_{m_n},D_{m_n}'\}\).

Then the cancellation blow-up theorem says, morally:

\[
\mathscr T_n \text{ is represented in } \mathcal P_{m_n} \text{ with nonzero first-order weight}.
\]

Failure of blow-up functoriality means that representation degenerates.

So the bridge theorem can be rephrased as:

### Tangent-plane degeneration theorem (target form)
If \(\mathscr T_n\) cannot be represented at first order by a nonzero direction in \(\mathcal P_{m_n}\), then
\[
K_v(h_n)\to 0.
\]

This says \(K_v\) is the obstruction to first-order representability of the branch tangent in the cancellation source plane.

That is a very natural statement geometrically.

---

## 6. What this would buy immediately

If this bridge theorem were proved, then on a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), every close bad sequence falls into exactly one of two cases.

### Case 1: nondegenerate first-order cancellation tangent
Then
\[
\mathcal C_3/\rho\to C,\qquad
\mathcal C_5/\rho\to K,
\]
and since
\[
C=0\Longrightarrow K\neq 0
\]
on \(J\), the branch is excluded. 

### Case 2: first-order cancellation tangent degenerates
Then by the bridge theorem,
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]
Now the existing lane split takes over:

- if also \(S\to 0\), the branch is captured by the residual exact fixed-shear corner;
- if \(S\not\to 0\), it is already in the mixed low-order lane.

So the whole local problem reduces to the already-classified lanes.

That would essentially finish the local good-patch story.

---

## 7. A new structural finding

I think the strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

> The local missing theorem is best viewed as a **tangent representability theorem**, not merely a witness asymptotics theorem.

That is, the real question is not just whether the corrected witnesses have a limit after dividing by \(\rho\). It is whether the actual bad-branch tangent is faithfully represented at first order by the cancellation source plane
\[
\operatorname{span}\{D_m,D_m'\}.
\]
If yes, the \(C/K/M\) determinant kills it.
If no, then the tangent has already collapsed into fixed-shear geometry.

That feels like the right conceptual formulation.

---

## 8. Negative finding

This also clarifies what is probably stale.

It is probably no longer the best framing to say “we need a uniform blow-up asymptotics theorem everywhere.” That is stronger than necessary and hides the real issue.

The real issue is only the failure locus of first-order representability. If one can prove that every such failure is captured by \(K_v=0\), then the local theorem is done even without a perfectly uniform global blow-up theorem.

So the target should now be weakened and sharpened at the same time:

not global asymptotics everywhere, but **capture of the failure-of-first-order-representability locus**.

---

## 9. Best next theorem target

The next theorem I would explicitly attack is:

### First-order representability capture theorem
Let \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). Suppose a close bad two-point sequence on \(J\) is such that the branch tangent is not represented at first order by any nonzero normalized cancellation source direction in
\[
\operatorname{span}\{D_m,D_m'\}.
\]
Then
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

A slightly stronger version would add
\[
S\to 0
\]
under the same hypothesis, but I think \(K_v\to 0\) is the right first target.

That is the sharpest current bridge theorem I can see.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:51:13)

I’m pushing on the new bridge theorem from the tangent-plane side now. The goal is to turn “loss of first-order cancellation control” into a concrete codimension statement, rather than leaving it as a descriptive failure mode.

_Thought for 9s_

Here is the next push.

I think the right move now is to make the bridge theorem more algebraic by separating two different kinds of “failure of first-order blow-up,” and showing that only one of them is actually possible on a good patch.

The key distinction is:

1. **directional failure**: the normalized cancellation direction in
\[
\mathcal P_m:=\operatorname{span}\{D_m,D_m'\}
\]
has a well-defined nonzero limit, but the corrected witnesses do not converge to the expected one-pair functionals;

2. **plane-collapse failure**: the bad-branch tangent is no longer represented at first order by any nonzero direction in \(\mathcal P_m\).

The first one is much harder to believe, and I think we can now say why.

---

## 1. Directional failure is heavily constrained

Fix a good patch
\[
J\Subset \{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M:=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]
Suppose we have a close bad sequence with cancellation variables
\[
(\mu_n,\delta_n)=\rho_n(\cos\theta_n,\sin\theta_n),
\qquad \rho_n\to 0,
\]
and suppose the normalized source directions
\[
\Xi_n:=\frac{D_{h_{1,n}}-\lambda_n D_{h_{2,n}}}{\rho_n}
\]
converge projectively to a nonzero direction in \(\mathcal P_{m_*}\). Then formally
\[
\Xi_n=-\cos\theta_n\,D_{m_n}-\sin\theta_n\,D_{m_n}'+O(\rho_n).
\]

So once the direction \((\cos\theta_n:\sin\theta_n)\) converges, the only first-order candidates for the corrected cubic/quintic responses are the linear functionals
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m).
\]

Now on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), the pair \((C,K)\) has no common zero. More strongly, the map
\[
(\cos\theta,\sin\theta)\longmapsto (C(m,\theta),K(m,\theta))
\]
has rank \(2\) as a linear map from the projective source plane to \(\mathbf C^2\), because its determinant is exactly \(M(m)\), up to the obvious normalization. So on a good patch, the first-order corrected response to a nonzero direction in \(\mathcal P_m\) cannot vanish in both channels.

That means:

> if the normalized cancellation direction converges to a genuine nonzero direction in \(\mathcal P_m\), then a bad branch can only survive if the corrected construction fails to be first-order continuous on that direction in a very specific way.

So the remaining issue is not “maybe there is some mysterious zero direction.” There is none. The only question is whether the corrected construction could misread a nonzero first-order source direction.

That is already a useful narrowing.

---

## 2. Why plane-collapse is the natural remaining failure mode

The more natural failure mode is that the tangent is no longer represented at first order by a nonzero vector in \(\mathcal P_m\) at all. In that case, after dividing by \(\rho_n\), the effective tangent seen by the corrected witnesses collapses.

This matches the residual-corner picture exactly. On the exact fixed-shear involutive corner, every analytic swap-invariant scalar starts at quadratic order in the cancellation variables, so the entire linear layer is absent. That is precisely what “plane-collapse” should look like from the cancellation side. 

So the bridge theorem should really be phrased as:

### Bridge Theorem, refined target
On a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), every close bad sequence must satisfy one of the following:

- either its tangent is represented at first order by a nonzero direction in \(\mathcal P_m\), in which case the \(C/K/M\) determinant excludes it;
- or its first-order representation in \(\mathcal P_m\) collapses, in which case
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

This is better than the older phrasing “failure of linear blow-up implies \(K_v\to 0\),” because it isolates the geometric content of the failure: loss of first-order representability in the cancellation source plane.

---

## 3. An algebraic witness for plane-collapse

There is also a concrete quantity to monitor.

Write the first-order source coefficients as
\[
v_n:=(\mu_n,\delta_n)\in \mathbf C^2.
\]
The cancellation plane \(\mathcal P_{m_n}\) is generated by \((D_{m_n},D_{m_n}')\), and the first-order corrected responses are given by the \(2\times 2\) matrix
\[
\mathsf A(m_n):=
\begin{pmatrix}
c(m_n) & c'(m_n)\\[4pt]
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m_n) & (A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m_n)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the formal first-order response vector is
\[
\mathsf A(m_n)\,v_n.
\]
Since
\[
\det \mathsf A(m)=M(m)\neq 0
\]
on the good patch, the matrix is uniformly invertible there. Therefore
\[
\|\mathsf A(m_n)v_n\|\asymp \|v_n\|=\rho_n.
\]

This is a real mathematical gain: on a good patch, the formal first-order corrected response has a uniform lower bound proportional to the size of the cancellation tangent.

So if the actual corrected witnesses are smaller than \(O(\rho_n)\), the discrepancy cannot come from bad conditioning of the first-order model. The first-order model itself is uniformly nondegenerate.

That means any bad sequence with
\[
\mathcal C_3=o(\rho_n),\qquad \mathcal C_5=o(\rho_n)
\]
must be failing **before** the determinant stage: the actual construction is no longer seeing the branch through the first-order source plane at all.

This is exactly the algebraic content we wanted.

---

## 4. What this says about \(K_v\)

Now the interpretation becomes sharper.

Since \(\mathsf A(m)\) is uniformly invertible on the good patch, there is no internal linear-algebra reason for first-order cancellation responses to disappear. So disappearance of the \(O(\rho)\) scale must mean that the true geometric branch tangent is no longer governed by the first-order source variation
\[
-\mu D_m-\delta D_m'.
\]
But the only currently available mechanism for that is fixed-shear collapse, i.e.
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

So we can sharpen the bridge target to:

### Uniform invertibility bridge principle
On \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), if the actual corrected cubic/quintic witnesses are \(o(\rho)\) along a close bad sequence, then the sequence must leave the first-order cancellation plane regime; equivalently, it must enter the fixed-shear degeneration regime, so
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

This is still not a proof, but it is much tighter than before because the alternative “maybe the one-pair linear map itself had a near-kernel” is now gone.

---

## 5. Stronger local dichotomy

So the local picture now becomes:

### Local dichotomy on a good patch
Let \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). Any close bad two-point sequence satisfies exactly one of the following:

**Noncollapsed tangent.**
The branch has a nonzero first-order representation in the cancellation source plane. Then by invertibility of \(\mathsf A(m)\), the formal cubic/quintic responses are of size \(\asymp \rho\), and the \(C/K/M\) determinant excludes simultaneous vanishing.

**Collapsed tangent.**
The actual corrected witnesses are \(o(\rho)\), so first-order representability in the cancellation plane fails. Then the sequence is forced into fixed-shear degeneration, hence
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

Once \(K_v\to 0\), the existing lane split takes over: either \(S\to 0\) and we are in the residual exact fixed-shear corner, or \(S\not\to 0\) and the sequence is already in the mixed low-order lane. 

That is the cleanest local organization I have so far.

---

## 6. New structural finding

The strongest new structural finding from this round is:

> On a good patch, the pair \((c,A_5^{\mathfrak f})\) and its derivative give a uniformly invertible first-order detector of cancellation tangents.

Equivalently, \(M\neq 0\) means that the cancellation tangent plane is uniformly visible to cubic and quintic order. So any disappearance of the \(O(\rho)\) scale is not a linear-algebra accident — it is a genuine geometric collapse into the fixed-shear regime.

That is a more precise role for \(M\) than we had before.

---

## 7. Negative finding

This also kills another possible stale line of thought.

We do **not** need to wonder anymore whether there might be some mysterious nonzero cancellation direction on a good patch that is “invisible” to both cubic and quintic at first order. There is no such direction because \(M\neq 0\) gives uniform invertibility of the detector matrix.

So the remaining missing theorem is not about finding a better first-order detector. We already have one. The missing theorem is about proving that if the actual witnesses are smaller than this detector predicts, then the branch has indeed left the first-order cancellation regime and entered fixed-shear collapse.

That is a much narrower task.

---

## 8. Best next theorem target

So the next theorem I would attack is:

### First-order detector theorem
Let \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). Suppose a close bad two-point sequence on \(J\) satisfies
\[
\mathcal C_3=o(\rho),\qquad \mathcal C_5=o(\rho)
\]
in the cancellation chart. Then
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

This is the sharpest current bridge theorem I can see. If it lands, the local good-patch story is almost finished.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:53:01)

_Thought for 12s_

Here is the next push. I think the best way forward is to turn the bridge problem into a **first-derivative identification problem** for the corrected construction.

That makes the remaining gap much more concrete.

---

Let
\[
\mathcal W(h_1,h_2,\lambda)
:=
\bigl(\mathcal C_3(h_1,h_2,\lambda),\mathcal C_5(h_1,h_2,\lambda)\bigr)
\]
denote the corrected cubic/quintic witness vector near the cancellation locus
\[
h_1=h_2=m,\qquad \lambda=1.
\]
In the cancellation chart
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1,
\]
we are studying the expansion of
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
\]
near \((\mu,\delta)=(0,0)\).

The other chats already isolated the formal first-order source variation
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu D_m-\delta D_m'+O((|\mu|+|\delta|)^2),
\]
and the corresponding one-pair linear functionals
\[
L_3(\alpha D_m+\beta D_m')=\alpha\,c(m)+\beta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
L_5(\alpha D_m+\beta D_m')=\alpha\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\beta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m).
\]
So the formal first-order detector matrix is
\[
\mathsf A(m):=
\begin{pmatrix}
c(m) & c'(m)\\[4pt]
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m) & (A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det \mathsf A(m)=M(m).
\]
On a good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
this matrix is uniformly invertible. turn12file1

So the real remaining question is:

> is the first derivative of the **actual corrected witness map** \(\mathcal W\) at the cancellation locus equal to \(\mathsf A(m)\)?

If yes, the bridge theorem is basically done.

## 1. Exact theorem target

The right local theorem is:

### First-derivative identification theorem
For each \(m\) with \(c(m)\neq 0\), the corrected witness map satisfies
\[
D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)
=
\mathsf A(m),
\]
i.e.
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\mathsf A(m)\binom{\mu}{\delta}
+
o\!\bigl(\sqrt{|\mu|^2+|\delta|^2}\bigr).
\]

Written componentwise,
\[
\mathcal C_3(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\mu\,c(m)+\delta\,c'(m)+o(\rho),
\]
\[
\mathcal C_5(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\mu\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\delta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)+o(\rho),
\qquad
\rho=\sqrt{\mu^2+\delta^2}.
\]

This is just the rigorous Fréchet-differentiable version of the blow-up linearization theorem.

If this holds, then on a good patch
\[
\|\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)\|
\asymp \rho
\]
for all sufficiently small \(\rho\), because \(\mathsf A(m)\) is uniformly invertible there.

So simultaneous vanishing of \(\mathcal C_3,\mathcal C_5\) is impossible for sufficiently small \(\rho\).

That would directly exclude close cancelling bad cores on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
\]

So one clean path is simply:

\[
\text{identify }D\mathcal W(m,0,0)=\mathsf A(m).
\]

---

## 2. Why this is better than the earlier bridge slogan

Earlier I was framing the missing theorem as

\[
\mathcal C_3=o(\rho),\ \mathcal C_5=o(\rho)\Longrightarrow K_v\to 0.
\]

That is still true as a good target, but it is now clearly **secondary**.

If the first derivative is identified and nondegenerate, then on a good patch the \(o(\rho)\) regime cannot happen at all unless the branch has already exited the ordinary cancellation chart. In other words, the bridge theorem becomes a corollary of first-derivative identification plus the existing lane split.

So the sharper logic is:

1. prove first derivative identification;
2. conclude nondegenerate linear detection on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\);
3. therefore any surviving bad sequence must leave the first-order cancellation regime;
4. hence it is captured by the already-known geometric degeneracy lanes, in particular \(K_v=0\).

That is a cleaner attack.

---

## 3. What would make first-derivative identification fail?

This is where the attack gets informative even if we cannot yet prove the theorem.

If
\[
D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)\neq \mathsf A(m),
\]
then the failure has to come from one of only a few sources.

First, the corrected construction may not be differentiable through the cancellation locus in the ordinary chart. That would mean the local obstruction is already non-analytic / blow-up-dependent, exactly the exotic possibility the other chats were circling.

Second, the corrected construction may be differentiable, but its derivative may not be source-functorial: some package-level renormalization or correction term may alter the first derivative away from the naive one-pair detector.

Third, the first derivative may exist but be defined only after quotienting by some gauge, in which case the right first-order object is not \(\mathsf A(m)\) itself but its class modulo lower-order freedoms.

That last possibility is where the order-7 quotient story lives; but on the good-patch cubic/quintic problem, the natural expectation is still that the first derivative should literally be \(\mathsf A(m)\).

So this attack isolates the precise frontier:

> either the corrected witness map is genuinely first-order source-functorial at the cancellation locus, or the first obstruction begins already at the level of differentiability / derivative identification there.

That is much sharper than “something package-level remains.”

---

## 4. A useful weaker theorem

There is also a weaker version that would still be enough.

### Weak detector theorem
There exists a \(2\times 2\) matrix \(\mathsf B(m)\), continuous in \(m\), such that
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\mathsf B(m)\binom{\mu}{\delta}+o(\rho),
\]
and
\[
\det \mathsf B(m)\neq 0
\]
on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\).

This would still kill close bad cores locally, even without proving \(\mathsf B=\mathsf A\).

So there are really two nested targets:

- strong target: identify \(\mathsf B=\mathsf A\);
- weaker but still decisive target: prove \(\mathsf B\) exists and is invertible on good patches.

That is a helpful fallback.

---

## 5. How this meshes with the residual-corner work

This also clarifies the relation to the exact fixed-shear corner.

The residual-corner attack already says: once one is on
\[
\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\},
\]
the branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal, finite transport state is quotient-visible, and analytic swap-invariant local scalars start at quadratic order. 

So the local story is now:

- **ordinary cancellation regime:** first derivative of \(\mathcal W\) should be nonzero and detected by \(\mathsf A(m)\);
- **fixed-shear residual regime:** linear order disappears, and only quadratic swap-even terms remain.

That means the true local dichotomy is not merely geometric. It is a dichotomy of **Taylor order**:

- non-fixed-shear bad branches must be first-order detected;
- fixed-shear bad branches begin only at second order.

This is a cleaner formulation than the earlier bridge language.

---

## 6. Strongest new structural finding

I think the strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

> the local good-patch problem has been reduced to identifying the first derivative of the corrected cubic/quintic witness map at the cancellation locus.

If that derivative exists and is nondegenerate, the whole non-fixed-shear local lane is dead.

So the missing theorem is no longer “some asymptotic exists” in a vague sense. It is literally a first-order Taylor theorem for the corrected witness map.

That is about as sharp a formulation as we can hope for.

---

## 7. Negative finding

This also tells us something stale.

It is probably no longer optimal to keep phrasing the next step as “prove blow-up functoriality on the cancellation chart.” That is true, but it is a bit too broad.

The sharp local target is narrower:

\[
\boxed{
\text{compute }D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0).
}
\]

If the derivative is the expected detector matrix, we win locally on good patches.
If the derivative does not exist or differs from the expected one, then that failure itself is the first genuinely package-level obstruction.

That is a much cleaner fork.

---

## 8. Best next attack

So the next theorem I would attack is:

### Cancellation-locus first derivative theorem
For the corrected cubic/quintic witness map \(\mathcal W\),
\[
D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
c(m) & c'(m)\\[4pt]
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m) & (A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

And the fallback theorem is:

### Cancellation-locus invertible derivative theorem
The derivative \(D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)\) exists and is invertible on every compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
\]

If either one lands, the local non-fixed-shear two-point problem on good patches is essentially finished.

That is the sharpest current math attack I see.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:55:16)

I’m pushing on the sharpest remaining local question now: whether the first derivative of the corrected witness map at the cancellation locus is forced by the already-known one-pair calculus, or whether that derivative itself is the first genuinely package-level obstruction.

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next push.

I think the strongest advance now is to isolate a **jet-theoretic alternative** for the corrected witness map at the cancellation locus. This sharpens the previous “compute the first derivative” target into a theorem schema with a genuine fork.

Write the corrected cubic/quintic witness vector as
\[
\mathcal W(h_1,h_2,\lambda)
:=
\bigl(\mathcal C_3(h_1,h_2,\lambda),\mathcal C_5(h_1,h_2,\lambda)\bigr),
\]
and pass to the cancellation chart
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1.
\]
So we study
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
\]
near
\[
(\mu,\delta)=(0,0),
\]
which is the collision-cancellation locus.

From the formal source expansion we already have
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu D_m-\delta D_m'+O((|\mu|+|\delta|)^2),
\]
and therefore the naive first-order detector matrix
\[
\mathsf A(m)=
\begin{pmatrix}
c(m) & c'(m)\\[4pt]
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m) & (A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
On a good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
this matrix is uniformly invertible. So if
\[
D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)=\mathsf A(m),
\]
the non-fixed-shear local problem is over on \(J\). That much is already clear.

The new point is that even without proving equality to \(\mathsf A\), one can now identify the only two possible first-jet behaviors compatible with everything else we know.

## 1. First-jet alternative at the cancellation locus

The corrected map \(\mathcal W\) near \((\mu,\delta)=(0,0)\) has only two plausible first-jet types.

### Type I: nondegenerate linear jet
There exists a \(2\times 2\) matrix \(\mathsf B(m)\) such that
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\mathsf B(m)\binom{\mu}{\delta}
+
o\!\bigl(\sqrt{|\mu|^2+|\delta|^2}\bigr),
\]
with
\[
\det \mathsf B(m)\neq 0
\]
on \(J\).

Then
\[
\|\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)\|\asymp \sqrt{|\mu|^2+|\delta|^2},
\]
so the corrected cubic/quintic witnesses cannot vanish simultaneously for sufficiently small \((\mu,\delta)\neq (0,0)\). This excludes close cancelling bad cores on the good patch.

### Type II: vanishing linear jet
The first derivative vanishes:
\[
D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)=0.
\]
Then
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)=o(\rho),
\qquad
\rho=\sqrt{|\mu|^2+|\delta|^2}.
\]
So the branch is invisible at first order to the cancellation tangent plane. In the current geometric picture, the only plausible place for that to happen is the exact fixed-shear involutive corner, where the linear layer is parity-killed and one enters the quadratic swap-even regime. In other words, Type II should force
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

This gives a cleaner bridge statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Either the cancellation-locus first jet is nondegenerate and kills the branch, or the first jet vanishes and the sequence is forced into fixed-shear geometry.}
}
\]

That is sharper than the earlier wording “failure of blow-up implies \(K_v\to 0\).”

## 2. Why there is no meaningful third option on a good patch

The only apparent third possibility would be:

### Type III: singular but nonzero linear jet
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\mathsf B(m)\binom{\mu}{\delta}
+
o(\rho),
\qquad
\det \mathsf B(m)=0,
\qquad
\mathsf B(m)\neq 0.
\]

But on a good patch this is already highly constrained.

Indeed, if the corrected first jet is source-functorial in the ordinary sense, then \(\mathsf B=\mathsf A\), and Type III is impossible because \(\det\mathsf A=M\neq 0\).

So Type III can occur only if the first derivative exists but is already a genuinely package-level deformation of the naive source detector. That is not impossible in principle, but it is now a very narrow obstruction: the first package-level obstruction would already have to appear at the level of the **linear jet**, not later.

That is an important negative finding. It means the local problem has been compressed to the following fork:

- either first-order source-functoriality survives, in which case the good-patch determinant kills the branch;
- or the first genuinely new obstruction is already a deformation or collapse of the cancellation-locus linear jet.

There is no longer room for a mysterious hidden first-order direction inside the naive source plane.

## 3. Quadratic regime as the only stable degeneration

Now combine this with the residual-corner attack.

On the exact fixed-shear involutive corner, after passing to the swap chart
\[
\nu=\log\Lambda,\qquad \Lambda=-a_2/a_1,
\]
the involution acts by
\[
(\nu,\delta)\mapsto(-\nu,-\delta),
\]
and any analytic swap-invariant local scalar factors through
\[
\nu^2,\qquad \nu\delta,\qquad \delta^2.
\]
So the entire linear layer vanishes there. The first nontrivial terms are quadratic. This is exactly the Type II behavior above. So the residual-corner theory supplies the unique currently known stable model for vanishing first jet.

That suggests the following stronger conjectural alternative:

### Conjectural local jet dichotomy
On a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), any close bad sequence satisfies exactly one of the following.

1. The corrected witness map has nondegenerate linear jet at the cancellation locus, hence the branch is excluded.

2. The corrected witness map has vanishing linear jet, and the sequence is captured by the exact fixed-shear involutive corner, where the witnesses begin at quadratic order.

That is a much cleaner local theorem target than a vague blow-up statement.

## 4. A useful intermediate theorem

There is an intermediate theorem that would already be enough and is probably easier than full derivative identification.

### Intermediate theorem target
Show that at each \(m\in J\), either

- \(D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)\) is invertible, or
- \(D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)=0\).

In other words, rule out the singular-but-nonzero linear jet.

If this were proved, then on the good patch the local problem would collapse to the clean dichotomy above. Since the residual-corner package already models the zero-jet case, and the determinant argument kills the invertible case, the only remaining work would be to show zero first jet indeed implies \(K_v=0\).

That is a significantly narrower burden than identifying the exact derivative matrix.

## 5. How to attack the singular-but-nonzero case

There is also a concrete way to think about Type III.

A rank-one first jet would mean that, to first order, the corrected cubic and quintic witnesses are proportional:
\[
\mathcal C_5(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\alpha(m)\,\mathcal C_3(m,\mu,\delta)+o(\rho).
\]
So the first-order corrected image of the cancellation tangent plane would collapse to a line in \(\mathbf C^2\).

But the naive source-functorial image of that plane does not collapse on \(J\), since \(\det\mathsf A=M\neq 0\). Therefore a rank-one first jet would itself be the first genuinely new package-level obstruction. It would amount to a first-order relation between corrected cubic and quintic responses that is not present in the one-pair calculus.

That is mathematically useful because it tells us exactly what to test:

> does the corrected construction impose a new first-order proportionality between cubic and quintic responses at the cancellation locus?

If the answer is no, then Type III is dead.

## 6. Strongest new structural finding

I think the strongest new structural finding from this round is:

> the cancellation-locus local problem is now reducible to the classification of the **linear jet** of the corrected witness map.

There are only three logical jet types:

- invertible;
- singular nonzero;
- zero.

The invertible type kills the branch locally on good patches.
The zero type is exactly the quadratic/fixed-shear regime.
So the only genuinely mysterious possibility left is the singular nonzero jet, i.e. a new first-order package-level proportionality between corrected cubic and quintic responses.

That is a much sharper picture than before.

## 7. Negative finding

This also says something useful about what is probably stale.

It is probably no longer the best use of effort to keep discussing “global blow-up functoriality” in broad terms. The real local question is much smaller:

\[
\boxed{
\text{What is the rank of }D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)\text{ on }J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\,?
}
\]

If it is rank \(2\), we win locally.
If it is rank \(0\), we are in the fixed-shear quadratic lane.
Only rank \(1\) is genuinely new.

That feels like the right mathematical compression.

## 8. Best next theorem target

So the next theorem I would explicitly attack is:

### Linear-jet rank theorem
On every compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
the corrected witness map at the cancellation locus satisfies
\[
\operatorname{rank} D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)\in\{0,2\}.
\]

The stronger version is:

### Exact first-derivative theorem
\[
D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)=
\begin{pmatrix}
c(m) & c'(m)\\[4pt]
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m) & (A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

The weaker rank theorem would already be a major advance, because it reduces the whole local good-patch problem to the already-understood quadratic fixed-shear lane versus the already-killed nondegenerate linear lane.

That is the sharpest current math attack I see.

Let

be the corrected cubic/quintic witness map in the cancellation chart

\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad

\]
near the collision-cancellation locus \((\mu,\delta)=(0,0)\). The formal first-order source expansion is
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu D_m-\delta D_m'+O((|\mu|+|\delta|)^2),

so the naive first-order detector is the matrix
\[
\mathsf A(m)=
\begin{pmatrix}

\qquad

\]
On a good patch
\[

this is uniformly invertible, so the ordinary first-order source plane is already fully seen by cubic and quintic. There is no hidden nonzero direction in \(\operatorname{span}\{D_m,D_m'\}\) that both channels miss. turn13file4turn13file5

So if the actual corrected first jet
\[
\mathsf B(m):=D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)

exists, then there are only three possibilities: rank \(2\), rank \(1\), or rank \(0\).

Rank \(2\) is the good case: then

\qquad \rho=\sqrt{|\mu|^2+|\delta|^2},

with \(\det \mathsf B(m)\neq 0\), so \(\|\mathcal W\|\asymp \rho\), and close cancelling bad cores are excluded locally.

Rank \(0\) is the fixed-shear-type case: the whole linear layer vanishes, so one is already in the quadratic regime that the residual-corner analysis understands. That is exactly the involutive / swap-even picture where ordinary analytic local scalars start at quadratic order in the cancellation variables. turn13file15

So the only genuinely mysterious case is rank \(1\).

But rank \(1\) now has a very concrete meaning: it says that, to first order,
\[
\mathcal C_5(m,\mu,\delta)=\alpha(m)\,\mathcal C_3(m,\mu,\delta)+o(\rho)
\]
for some scalar \(\alpha(m)\). In other words, the corrected cubic and quintic responses become **first-order proportional** at the cancellation locus, even though the naive one-pair detector matrix \(\mathsf A(m)\) is invertible there. So rank \(1\) is not “some vague remaining difficulty.” It is the first genuinely package-level local obstruction: a new first-order relation between corrected cubic and quintic that is absent from the source-level one-pair calculus. turn13file4

That gives a sharper theorem target than before:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Rule out rank }1\text{ for }D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0)\text{ on }J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
}
\]

If that lands, then the local cancellation problem splits cleanly into the already-good rank \(2\) regime and the already-understood rank \(0\) quadratic fixed-shear regime.

So the best current local dichotomy is:

- **rank \(2\)**: nondegenerate first-order cancellation law, killed by the \(C/K/M\) determinant argument;
- **rank \(0\)**: no linear layer, captured by the involutive fixed-shear quadratic regime;
- **rank \(1\)**: the only genuinely new local obstruction, equivalent to a first-order proportionality of corrected cubic and quintic responses.

That is, I think, the sharpest compression so far.

It also clarifies the next attack. The natural thing to try is not a broad “prove blow-up functoriality everywhere” statement, but the narrower claim:

> the corrected witness map cannot have a singular nonzero first jet at the cancellation locus on a good patch.

Equivalently: the first corrected jet is either the ordinary source-detected one or it vanishes entirely.

That feels much more attackable.

A second useful consequence is conceptual. The residual-corner work and the cancellation-chart work now fit together as a statement about **Taylor order**:

- non-fixed-shear local branches should be detected at first order;
- fixed-shear branches begin only at second order;
- hence rank \(1\) would be the only “intermediate” local regime.

So if rank \(1\) can be excluded, the local good-patch problem is essentially organized completely.

My honest read is: I do **not** yet have a proof that rank \(1\) is impossible from the present draft ingredients alone. But the attack has now reduced the local missing theorem to that one issue, and made clear what it means mathematically. turn13file12turn13file15

## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:58:24)

_Thought for 19s_

Here is the next push.

I think the best way to advance now is to treat the rank-\(1\) jet as a **codimension-one intermediate regime** and show what it would force. That makes the remaining local obstruction much more rigid than “maybe the derivative is singular.”

Let
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\bigl(\mathcal C_3(m,\mu,\delta),\mathcal C_5(m,\mu,\delta)\bigr)
\]
be the corrected cubic/quintic witness map in the cancellation chart
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1,
\]
near \((\mu,\delta)=(0,0)\). Let
\[
\mathsf B(m):=D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0).
\]

We already know the naive source-level detector is
\[
\mathsf A(m)=
\begin{pmatrix}
c(m) & c'(m)\\[4pt]
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m) & (A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det \mathsf A(m)=M(m),
\]
and on a good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}
\]
this is uniformly invertible. So source-level first-order geometry is fully visible there. turn12file1

The only unresolved local jet type is now:

\[
\operatorname{rank}\mathsf B(m)=1.
\]

The key observation is that this already forces a very specific structure.

## 1. Rank \(1\) gives a unique first-order bad tangent

If \(\operatorname{rank}\mathsf B(m)=1\), then
\[
\ker \mathsf B(m)
\]
is a unique projective line in the cancellation tangent plane with coordinates \((\mu,\delta)\).

So any exact bad branch through the cancellation locus must have tangent direction
\[
(\mu,\delta)=\rho\,(\mu_*(m),\delta_*(m))+o(\rho)
\]
with
\[
\mathsf B(m)\binom{\mu_*(m)}{\delta_*(m)}=0.
\]

That is already stronger than the earlier story. Rank \(1\) does not leave a family of possible first-order bad directions. It leaves exactly **one** projective tangent direction at each \(m\).

So if rank \(1\) occurs on a good patch, the local bad branch is forced into a canonically selected tangent line.

That is the first new structural gain.

## 2. Along the kernel direction, the witnesses are automatically quadratic

Take a tangent direction \(v_*(m)=(\mu_*(m),\delta_*(m))\in\ker \mathsf B(m)\setminus\{0\}\). Along a curve
\[
(\mu,\delta)=\rho\,v_*(m)+o(\rho),
\]
we get
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)=O(\rho^2).
\]

So rank \(1\) automatically produces a **quadratic regime along one distinguished tangent direction**, even though the full linear jet is not zero.

This is important because it means rank \(1\) is not an ordinary first-order regime. It is already half-collapsed toward the fixed-shear quadratic picture.

That is the second structural gain.

## 3. Rank \(1\) is an “intermediate Taylor-order” regime

This now sharpens the local Taylor-order picture to three possibilities:

- rank \(2\): every nonzero tangent is seen at order \(1\);
- rank \(1\): exactly one tangent line is invisible at order \(1\), and along that line the witnesses begin at order \(2\);
- rank \(0\): every tangent is invisible at order \(1\), i.e. the full fixed-shear quadratic regime.

So rank \(1\) is literally the unique intermediate Taylor-order regime between ordinary cancellation geometry and exact fixed-shear involutive geometry.

That makes it much more geometric than before.

## 4. Why rank \(1\) should be unstable on a good patch

Now compare this to the source-level picture.

At source level, the unique cubic-killing direction is determined by
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m)=0,
\]
namely
\[
(\cos\theta:\sin\theta)=(c'(m):-c(m)).
\]
And on the good patch,
\[
K(m,\theta_*(m))\asymp -M(m)\neq 0.
\]
So the source-level one-pair calculus says:

> there is exactly one distinguished first-order cancellation direction, and along that direction quintic is definitely nonzero.

By contrast, rank \(1\) says:

> there is exactly one distinguished first-order cancellation direction, and along that direction both corrected cubic and quintic vanish to first order.

So rank \(1\) can only happen if the corrected construction replaces the source-level distinguished direction by a different one, or else keeps the same direction but creates a new first-order proportionality that kills quintic there.

Either way, rank \(1\) is not a small perturbation of the source-level model. It is the first place where the corrected package would have to introduce a genuinely new first-order relation not present in \((c,A_5^{\mathfrak f})\).

That is the strongest negative finding from this round.

## 5. A sharper theorem target: exclude drifting kernel lines

Because rank \(1\) gives a unique kernel line, the natural thing to attack is whether that kernel line can drift independently of the source-level cubic-killing line.

Let
\[
\ell_B(m):=\mathbf P(\ker \mathsf B(m))\subset \mathbf P^1
\]
when \(\operatorname{rank}\mathsf B(m)=1\), and let
\[
\ell_C(m)
\]
be the source-level cubic-killing line
\[
(\mu:\delta)=(c'(m):-c(m)).
\]

Then there are only two subcases.

### Subcase A: \(\ell_B(m)\neq \ell_C(m)\)
Then the actual corrected first-order bad direction is different from the source-level cubic-killing direction. That means the corrected cubic first derivative is already not source-functorial at first order.

### Subcase B: \(\ell_B(m)=\ell_C(m)\)
Then along the source-level cubic-killing direction, the corrected quintic derivative must also vanish. But source-level quintic there is
\[
-\,M(m)\neq 0.
\]
So this forces a first-order deformation of the quintic derivative itself.

Thus either way, rank \(1\) means that at least one of the corrected first derivatives
\[
D\mathcal C_3,\qquad D\mathcal C_5
\]
fails to match the source-level one-pair derivative on the good patch.

So rank \(1\) is equivalent to a **first-order failure of source functoriality**.

That is much sharper than saying “rank \(1\) is the remaining case.”

## 6. Best reformulation of the missing theorem

I think the right theorem target is now:

> **No intermediate first-order regime theorem.**  
> On a compact good patch
> \[
> J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
> \]
> the corrected witness map at the cancellation locus has no rank-\(1\) first jet.

Equivalently:

> the corrected first jet is either source-functorial and nondegenerate, or vanishes entirely.

That is the cleanest local theorem statement I see now.

If proved, the local good-patch picture would collapse to:

- rank \(2\): excluded by the \(C/K/M\) determinant;
- rank \(0\): captured by the quadratic fixed-shear lane already analyzed.

And then the only remaining work would be to prove that rank \(0\) indeed forces entry into the \(K_v=0\) corner, which is exactly the bridge theorem we were already circling.

## 7. Strongest new structural finding

The strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Rank }1\text{ is not just “another case.” It is exactly the first-order failure-of-source-functoriality regime.}
}
\]

That is useful because it turns the remaining local ambiguity into a very specific obstruction class:

- not hidden directions in the source plane;
- not bad conditioning of the \(C/K\) detector;
- but a genuine package-level deformation of the cancellation-locus first jet.

That is a much narrower target.

## 8. Honest status

I do **not** yet have a proof from the current draft ingredients that rank \(1\) is impossible.

But I think the attack has now reduced the local missing theorem to two very sharp statements:

1. **No rank-\(1\) first jet on good patches.**
2. **Rank \(0\) first jet implies \(K_v\to 0\).**

If both land, the local two-point problem on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}
\]
is essentially organized completely.

That is the sharpest current math attack I can see.
