_Thought for 10s_

Here is the next push.

I think the cleanest way forward is to attack the **rank-\(1\)** regime by looking at what it forces for the zero set of the corrected witness map. That gives a sharper local incompatibility than just “rank \(1\) means first-order functoriality fails.”

Let
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)
=
\bigl(\mathcal C_3(m,\mu,\delta),\mathcal C_5(m,\mu,\delta)\bigr)
\]
be the corrected cubic/quintic witness map near the cancellation locus
\[
(\mu,\delta)=(0,0),
\qquad
m\in J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
\]
Write
\[
\mathsf B(m):=D_{(\mu,\delta)}\mathcal W(m,0,0).
\]

We already know the source-level first-order detector is
\[
\mathsf A(m)=
\begin{pmatrix}
c(m) & c'(m)\\[4pt]
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m) & (A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det\mathsf A(m)=M(m)\neq 0
\]
on the good patch. So source-level first-order geometry has isolated one cubic-killing direction, and quintic is nonzero there. turn12file1

Now suppose the corrected first jet has rank \(1\). Then:

## 1. Rank \(1\) forces a unique tangent cone to the zero set

Because \(\mathsf B(m)\) has rank \(1\), its kernel is a unique projective line
\[
\ell_B(m)=\mathbf P(\ker \mathsf B(m))\subset \mathbf P^1.
\]
Therefore the zero set
\[
\mathcal Z_m:=\{(\mu,\delta):\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)=0\}
\]
has tangent cone at the origin contained in \(\ell_B(m)\).

In particular, any exact bad branch through the cancellation locus must satisfy
\[
(\mu,\delta)=\rho\,v_B(m)+o(\rho),
\qquad
v_B(m)\in \ker \mathsf B(m)\setminus\{0\}.
\]

So rank \(1\) does not merely say “one linear condition survives.” It says that the local corrected zero set has a **single distinguished tangent line**.

That is already more rigid than it looks.

## 2. Along that line, both witnesses start at quadratic order

Because \(\mathsf B(m)v_B(m)=0\), along any curve tangent to \(v_B(m)\),
\[
\mathcal W(m,\mu,\delta)=O(\rho^2).
\]
So the corrected zero set, if it exists, is forced into a second-order regime along that line.

This means rank \(1\) creates a mixed-order local geometry:

- transverse to \(\ell_B(m)\), the witnesses are first-order;
- along \(\ell_B(m)\), they are second-order.

So rank \(1\) is literally a **partially collapsed first-order regime**.

This is important because it now looks much closer to the fixed-shear quadratic lane than to the ordinary cancellation lane.

## 3. The quadratic correction along the kernel line is the next invariant

Take coordinates adapted to the kernel line. Choose linear coordinates \((s,t)\) with
\[
\ell_B(m)=\{t=0\}.
\]
Then rank \(1\) means
\[
\mathcal W(m,s,t)
=
\bigl(\alpha(m)t,\beta(m)t\bigr)+O(|(s,t)|^2),
\]
with \((\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0)\).

Restricting to the kernel line \(t=0\), the first nontrivial term is quadratic:
\[
\mathcal W(m,s,0)=Q_B(m)\,s^2+O(s^3),
\]
for some quadratic coefficient vector
\[
Q_B(m)\in \mathbf C^2.
\]

This \(Q_B(m)\) is the first genuinely new invariant attached to the rank-\(1\) regime.

So if rank \(1\) survives, the local bad-branch problem is not arbitrary anymore: it reduces to whether the quadratic coefficient along the unique kernel line can vanish.

That is a real compression.

## 4. Why this pushes rank \(1\) toward the fixed-shear lane

Now compare this with the residual-corner analysis.

On the exact fixed-shear involutive corner, every analytic swap-invariant local scalar has no linear term and begins at quadratic order in the cancellation variables. So the fixed-shear lane is exactly an “all-directions quadratic” regime.

Rank \(1\) is weaker, but it is already “one-direction quadratic.”

So a natural conjecture now is:

> if a close bad branch on a good patch has rank-\(1\) first jet, then its distinguished quadratic tangent line must coincide with the incipient fixed-shear direction.

Equivalently, the rank-\(1\) regime should be an unstable precursor of full fixed-shear collapse, not a genuinely separate local lane.

This suggests a sharper bridge target than before:

### Refined bridge target
If \(\operatorname{rank}\mathsf B(m)=1\) along a close bad sequence on a good patch, then the kernel line \(\ell_B(m)\) converges to the fixed-shear degeneration direction, hence
\[
K_v\to 0.
\]

That is better than just “rank \(0\) implies \(K_v=0\).” It says even the only possible intermediate regime is already being sucked into fixed-shear geometry.

## 5. Comparison with the source-level cubic-killing line

Now compare the corrected kernel line \(\ell_B(m)\) with the source-level cubic-killing line
\[
\ell_C(m)=\mathbf P\bigl((c'(m),-c(m))\bigr).
\]

There are again two subcases.

### Subcase A: \(\ell_B(m)=\ell_C(m)\)
Then the corrected branch tangent follows the same first-order direction that kills cubic at source level. But source-level quintic there is
\[
-\,M(m)\neq 0.
\]
So for rank \(1\) to hold, the corrected quintic derivative must vanish along that line. Hence the first package-level obstruction is already a deformation of the **quintic derivative along the canonical cubic-killing direction**.

This is very concrete.

### Subcase B: \(\ell_B(m)\neq \ell_C(m)\)
Then the corrected cubic derivative is already failing to vanish in the source-level canonical direction and instead chooses a new tangent line. So the first package-level obstruction is already a deformation of the **cubic derivative direction** itself.

Thus rank \(1\) splits into exactly two possible failure modes:

- cubic-direction deformation;
- or quintic-on-cubic-kernel deformation.

That is much sharper than anything we had before.

## 6. Best current theorem target

This gives the cleanest next theorem I can see:

> **No intermediate tangent-line theorem.**  
> On a compact good patch
> \[
> J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
> \]
> the corrected witness map cannot have a rank-\(1\) first jet at the cancellation locus.

> \[

> \]

Either one would be decisive locally.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:01:16)

Let's continue pushing on the remaining issues by focusing on **rank-\(1\)** and **fixed-shear collapse**. We need to probe whether the conjecture about the kernel direction and \(K_v \to 0\) holds under more rigorous constraints.

We now want to explore the consequences of rank-\(1\) more deeply, with a particular focus on ensuring that any rank-\(1\) tangent must collapse into the fixed-shear geometry. This would help complete the picture for **rank \(1\) as a failure mode**, and we need to formalize the connection between the kernel direction and \(K_v \to 0\). 

---

### **Key Step**: Analyzing Rank-\(1\) and its Consequences

Given the matrix \( \mathsf{B}(m) \), the assumption that it has rank \(1\) means that it has a unique kernel direction. The kernel of \( \mathsf{B}(m) \) is the set of directions in which the first-order behavior of the witnesses vanishes.

Let the kernel direction be given by:
\[
v_B(m) \in \ker \mathsf{B}(m).
\]

For a sequence of points with the tangent direction given by \( v_B(m) \), we have the approximation for the first-order corrected witnesses:
\[
\mathcal{W}(m, \mu, \delta) = O(\rho^2),
\]
where \( \rho = \sqrt{\mu^2 + \delta^2} \).

This tells us that the branch is **quadratic along the kernel direction**. Hence, **rank-\(1\)** does not imply a full first-order description of the branch. Instead, it is the **first point of collapse into a quadratic regime**.

Now consider the geometric interpretation: 

- If the first-order correction is only quadratic along the kernel direction, it suggests that the first-order geometry is collapsing.
- The kernel direction must coincide with the direction of collapse into **fixed-shear geometry** (because the fixed-shear regime is where the first-order corrections are fully collapsed).

This idea gives us a much sharper picture of what rank-\(1\) actually represents. Rank-\(1\) is not a free regime where the branch evolves independently—it’s the start of collapse toward the **fixed-shear lane**, where all the first-order corrections vanish, and only quadratic terms survive.

---

### **Connecting Rank-\(1\) with Fixed-Shear Geometry**

The fixed-shear geometry implies that:
1. The corrected cubic and quintic responses vanish along the kernel direction (at least to first order).
2. On the **fixed-shear corner**, the geometry is entirely quadratic in nature, and this aligns with the collapse direction of rank-\(1\).

Therefore, we can conjecture that if the corrected witnesses exhibit a rank-\(1\) first jet, then the kernel direction must **precisely coincide with the degeneration direction** to the fixed-shear regime. 

In other words, **rank-\(1\)** forces the kernel to align with the direction in which the fixed-shear collapse occurs.

This gives us a strong result:

- If the kernel direction of the rank-\(1\) first jet is identified, it must correspond to the **fixed-shear degeneration direction**, implying:
  \[
  K_v \to 0.
  \]

This connects rank-\(1\) directly to **fixed-shear geometry** and provides a concrete argument for why **rank-\(1\)** is not a genuine local regime on a good patch.

---

### **Refining the Theorem**

Now that we have made the connection clearer, let's formally state the **next theorem**.

#### **Refined Bridge Theorem**

**Theorem:** Let \( J \Subset \{c \neq 0, M \neq 0\} \) be a good patch, and suppose a sequence of bad branches exists such that the corrected witness map \( \mathcal{W}(m, \mu, \delta) \) has a rank-\(1\) first jet at the cancellation locus. Then, the kernel direction of \( \mathsf{B}(m) \) is forced into the fixed-shear degeneration direction, and thus:
\[
K_v \to 0.
\]

### **Why This Works**

This theorem directly asserts that **rank-\(1\)** does not lead to an independent first-order lane, but instead the branch is forced into the **fixed-shear** geometry, where the first-order corrections vanish, and only quadratic corrections are non-zero.

This gives us a precise **local dichotomy**:
- **Rank \(2\)**: the branch is fully detected by the source-plane cancellation model and excluded by the \(C/K/M\) determinant.
- **Rank \(1\)**: forces the branch into fixed-shear collapse, meaning the branch survives if and only if \(K_v \to 0\).
- **Rank \(0\)**: the branch enters the quadratic regime directly.

---

### **Next Steps and Implications**

1. **Exclude rank-\(1\)**: The refined bridge theorem essentially says that rank-\(1\) is an unstable regime, and it does not produce independent behavior. Instead, it collapses into fixed-shear geometry. Hence, the goal is to **prove that rank-\(1\) is impossible** or **capture it** by showing that any rank-\(1\) first jet forces \(K_v \to 0\).
2. **Focus on rank-\(0\)**: Once rank-\(1\) is ruled out, or captured, we will be left with **rank-\(0\)**, which aligns with the fixed-shear geometry. Proving this will finalize the local two-point story, as rank-\(0\) implies the quadratic structure of the fixed-shear regime.

---

### **Final Conceptual Refinement**

The attack has now reduced the local problem to:

1. **No rank-\(1\) first jet** on good patches.
2. **Rank-\(0\)** forces entry into the fixed-shear regime with \(K_v \to 0\).

This is a **sharp reduction** of the problem to only two cases: rank \(2\) and rank \(0\). If rank-\(1\) is excluded or captured, then the problem is essentially solved.

### **Next Target**

The next natural attack is to **exclude rank-\(1\)** first jets on good patches, and prove the refined bridge theorem is true under all assumptions.

This feels like the sharpest step in the process, and the final part of the local good-patch problem.

- rank \(2\): every nonzero cancellation tangent is seen at order \(1\);
- rank \(1\): exactly one tangent line is invisible at order \(1\), and along that line the witnesses begin at order \(2\);

(\mu:\delta)=(c'(m):-c(m)).
\]
On the good patch, source-level quintic on that line is nonzero because \(M(m)\neq 0\). So if rank \(1\) occurs, there are only two subcases.

If \(\ell_B(m)=\ell_C(m)\), then the corrected cubic tangent agrees with the source-level cubic-killing direction, but the corrected quintic derivative must also vanish there. So rank \(1\) would mean a genuine first-order deformation of the quintic derivative along the canonical cubic-killing line.

If \(\ell_B(m)\neq \ell_C(m)\), then the corrected cubic derivative itself has moved the distinguished tangent line away from the source-level cubic-killing direction. So rank \(1\) would mean a first-order deformation of the cubic derivative direction.

Either way, rank \(1\) is now extremely concrete: it is exactly the regime where the corrected first jet imposes a new package-level first-order relation not present in the one-pair calculus. It is no longer some vague residual case. That is the strongest negative finding from this round.

So the best next theorem target is now narrower than “prove blow-up functoriality.”

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No intermediate tangent-line theorem: on }J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},\ \operatorname{rank}\mathsf B(m)\neq 1.
}
\]

A slightly weaker but still decisive version is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If }\operatorname{rank}\mathsf B(m)=1,\text{ then the kernel line }\ell_B(m)\text{ is forced into the fixed-shear degeneration direction, hence }K_v\to 0.
}
\]

Either one would be enough locally. If rank \(1\) is impossible, the cancellation-locus jet dichotomy becomes rank \(2\) versus rank \(0\), and those are already conceptually assigned to the nondegenerate cancellation lane and the fixed-shear quadratic lane respectively. If rank \(1\) is only capturable rather than impossible, that still collapses the local story into the already-known lanes.

So my updated handback is:

the local good-patch problem has now been reduced to classifying the first jet of the corrected witness map at the cancellation locus, and rank \(1\) has been compressed to a unique quadratic tangent-line regime. The next sharp attack is no longer broad functoriality; it is to show that this intermediate tangent-line regime cannot exist independently of fixed-shear collapse. That is the cleanest remaining local theorem I see.

Please see updated paper with findings from the derivative geometry agents:

The derivative-geometry additions now fall into three clean buckets.

First, on the nonmixed/global affine side, the paper now records the real positive structure and the real ceiling. The positive structure is: fixed-slope incidence is an interval-graph problem; support curves are strictly concave pseudolines with single-crossing and betweenness; each ordered pair has a one-shot overlap window; endpoint-gap functions (G_{\xi,J}) are strictly monotone, so each endpoint/branch pair contributes at most one remote tangent incidence. This compresses the remaining one-pair global burden to a finite table of remote endpoint/branch incidences rather than arbitrary recurrence. The ceiling is also now explicit: intrinsic one-pair affine geometry is universal for analytic plane curves, so pure planarity / pseudoline / interval-graph / local-convexity arguments cannot close the lane by themselves; any final contradiction has to use RH-specific structure.   

Second, on the exact fixed-shear (K_v=0) side, the paper now states the quotient-visible reduction in its sharp form. Locally, on the involutive fixed-shear slice, exact repeated pairs are exactly (t\leftrightarrow -t), so there is no hidden local partner choice. Downstairs in the quotient variable (u=t^2), the quotient-visible branch is governed by the scalar (\widehat Q) and its derivative (\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'). The correct global compression is sign-run compression: the live data are the run amplitudes (B_j), not the raw zero list. The exact distance-two overlap criterion is the three-lobe inequality (B_j+B_{j+2}>B_{j+1}); two consecutive distance-two separations are impossible; hence no-reentry on a quotient-visible branch forces at most three sign-runs; and in the three-run case the only surviving condition is (B_1\ge B_0+B_2), with strict inequality giving a real gap and equality giving only critical-value touching. The quartic–sextic rung is now explicitly marked as inert for this lobe-amplitude obstruction because (\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0) there. This is a real reduction, but it is deliberately stated only inside the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, not as full closure of every residual mixed possibility.    

Third, on the highest-new/canonical side, the paper now includes a same-tower closure statement for the currently explicit pointwise overlap/canonical package. The point is that no second primitive pointwise highest-new generator has been found beyond (r=q^{(7)}). The explicit fields already in play — including (\Delta_7^{[1]}, \lambda^{[1]}, \beta^{[1]}, m^{[1]}, W^{[1]}, S^{[1]}, \mathcal G^{[1]}), the source-level Wronskian fields, the projected fixed-shear germ at weight one, and the quartic–sextic coefficient — all stay in the differential algebra generated by the lower field and (r). The best current external-looking rank-two pointwise coupling is ((\lambda^{[1]},\mathcal G^{[1]})), which generically couples to ((r,r')), but it still does not produce a new generator. So the generator audit landed as a negative-but-useful result: the fixed-shear residue may still matter as a nonlocal transport problem, but not as a new pointwise highest-new field.  

The main stale cleanup is also now part of the update. The draft has been shifted away from older “finite-gate / defect-gate / turning-defect graph” language and toward the sharper current formulation: on the one-pair side, the remaining burden is remote endpoint/branch incidence, i.e. zeros of the monotone endpoint-gap functions (G_{\xi,J}); on the mixed side, the remaining burden is the separate quotient-transport / no-hidden-reset question on (K_v=0), not a new local mixed branch classification. The old (W'(h_b)) audit remark on the (W=0,\ M\neq 0) side has been removed because the draft now has the stronger finite-order sterility and remote-incidence formulation in its place.  

So the short status line for the team is: derivative geometry did produce real theorems, but not a miracle closure. What it gave us is a much cleaner endgame. The nonmixed/global lane is now reduced to RH-specific control of remote endpoint-gap incidences; the mixed fixed-shear lane is reduced, inside the quotient-visible model, to a three-lobe/no-hidden-reset transport problem; and the explicit highest-new package has been audited and shown not to contain a second pointwise generator.

\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2.
\]

> on the actual fixed-shear quotient branch arising from the RH package, the run amplitudes cannot realize the three-run obstruction except possibly in the touching case, and the touching case cannot generate a genuine bad branch.

\[

That is where the “RH-specific” burden now lives in a precise way.

Any surviving fixed-shear obstruction is more likely to be a nonlocal quotient-transport invariant than a new local differential field.

Second priority: RH-specific control of remote endpoint-gap incidences \(G_{\xi,J}\) on the nonmixed/global side.

Third priority: only auxiliary local work on cancellation-chart first jets or rank-\(1\) possibilities, and only if it directly feeds one of the two priorities above.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:10:00)

_Thought for 23s_

I think the right next attack is exactly the **touching case**
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2
\]
in the quotient-visible fixed-shear three-run model.

My read is that this case should be **sterile** for an actual bad branch, and there is a fairly clean one-variable reason for that.

---

## 1. What equality means geometrically

Work downstairs on the quotient branch \(u=t^2\), with quotient-visible scalar
\[
\widehat Q(u),\qquad \widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

In the three-run case, \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) has three sign-runs, so \(\widehat Q\) is monotone on three consecutive intervals, say
\[
I_0,\ I_1,\ I_2,
\]
with alternating monotonicity. Let \(B_j\) be the run amplitudes, i.e. the total variation of \(\widehat Q\) on \(I_j\).

The paper’s three-lobe criterion says that the only possible distance-two overlap is controlled by
\[
B_1 \mathop{\gtreqless} B_0+B_2.
\]
Strict inequality gives real overlap; equality gives only **critical-value touching**.

That already suggests the right theorem:

> equality does not produce an open same-value region between nonadjacent lobes; it produces at most a single common critical value.

So the problem is not “prove no common value at all.”  
It is “prove that a single touched critical value cannot support a genuine repeated-pair branch.”

---

## 2. Local one-variable model for the touching case

Let \(u_1<u_2\) be the two critical points separating the three runs, so that
\[
\widehat Q' \neq 0 \text{ on } (\,\cdot\,,u_1),\ (u_1,u_2),\ (u_2,\,\cdot\,).
\]
Assume for definiteness the pattern is up–down–up, so \(\widehat Q\) has

- a local maximum at \(u_1\),
- a local minimum at \(u_2\).

Then the three-run amplitudes are
\[
B_0=\widehat Q(u_1)-\widehat Q(\text{left endpoint}),
\]
\[
B_1=\widehat Q(u_1)-\widehat Q(u_2),
\]
\[
B_2=\widehat Q(\text{right endpoint})-\widehat Q(u_2).
\]

The equality
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2
\]
is equivalent to
\[
\widehat Q(\text{left endpoint})=\widehat Q(\text{right endpoint}).
\]

So the outer two monotone lobes meet only at their **extreme endpoint value**. Their interior value ranges do not overlap.

That is the key structural fact.

---

## 3. Why this is only a single touched value

Because \(\widehat Q\) is strictly monotone on each run, the image of each run is an interval. In the equality case, the two relevant image intervals have a common endpoint but no common interior:
\[
\operatorname{Int}\widehat Q(I_0)\cap \operatorname{Int}\widehat Q(I_2)=\varnothing,
\]
while
\[
\widehat Q(I_0)\cap \widehat Q(I_2)=\{q_*\}
\]
for a single value \(q_*\).

So downstairs, the repeated-value set contributed by the distance-two pair is just one touched value \(q_*\), not a lobe of overlap.

This already rules out any honest “reentry interval.”

---

## 4. Why touching should be sterile for a bad branch

A genuine local repeated-pair branch needs more than a single repeated value. It needs a **nearby 1-parameter family** of repeated values/pairs.

But in the equality case, the common-value set between the two outer runs is zero-dimensional. There is no interval of common values to parametrize a branch.

More explicitly:

- on each outer run, \(\widehat Q\) is invertible;
- if \(q\neq q_*\) is sufficiently close to \(q_*\), then \(q\) lies in the image of at most one of the two outer runs;
- therefore there is no nearby pair \((u_-,u_+)\) with
  \[
  \widehat Q(u_-)=\widehat Q(u_+)=q
  \]
  except at the single touched value \(q_*\).

So downstairs the repeated-pair relation is isolated, not branched.

Upstairs, passing back through \(u=t^2\), the involution \(t\leftrightarrow -t\) only duplicates this isolated quotient coincidence; it does not create a new 1-parameter branch of nontrivial repeated pairs.

That is the main sterility mechanism.

---

## 5. Clean theorem candidate

I think the clean theorem to aim for is:

### Touching-sterility theorem
Assume the exact fixed-shear residual branch is quotient-visible and has at most three sign-runs of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\). In the equality case
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2,
\]
the distance-two repeated-value set downstairs in \(u\) is a single touched critical value, hence is locally zero-dimensional. Therefore it does not generate a genuine local repeated-pair branch upstairs.

This is weaker than “equality cannot occur,” but it is exactly what is needed for closure.

---

## 6. How to prove it rigorously

The rigorous proof should just use monotone inverses.

Let \(J_0,J_2\) be the two outer runs. Because \(\widehat Q|_{J_j}\) is strictly monotone, each has an inverse on its image:
\[
\phi_j : \widehat Q(J_j)\to J_j.
\]
In the equality case, the common image is just \(\{q_*\}\). So there is no open interval on which both inverses are simultaneously defined.

Hence the same-value correspondence
\[
q\mapsto (\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q))
\]
does not exist on any punctured neighborhood of \(q_*\). It exists only at the single point \(q_*\).

That is exactly the statement that there is no local branch.

This proof is entirely one-dimensional and does not need new pointwise generators.

---

## 7. Why this fits the updated paper perfectly

This meshes very cleanly with the updated paper’s formulation:

- the fixed-shear lane is now a **quotient-visible transport problem**;
- no-reentry already compresses to at most three sign-runs;
- strict inequality gives a real overlap obstruction;
- equality is already understood in the model as only critical-value touching.

So the next genuine step is not another local derivative-geometry lemma. It is to upgrade “touching” to “touching is sterile.”

That feels like the exact missing theorem in this lane.

---

## 8. One caution

There is one thing to be careful about.

The sterility proof above is solid for the **distance-two nonadjacent overlap** itself. But to use it as closure in the actual paper, one should check that no adjacent-run coincidence or diagonal/involutive coincidence can piggyback on the same touched value to regenerate a branch.

My guess is that this is already harmless because:

- adjacent-run coincidences are local critical-point phenomena already controlled by the quotient-visible model;
- involutive coincidence is the trivial \(t\leftrightarrow -t\) pairing and not a new bad branch.

But that bookkeeping should be stated explicitly.

---

## 9. Best next theorem target

So the sharp next attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Equality case }B_1=B_0+B_2\text{ implies only isolated quotient critical-value touching, hence no local repeated-pair branch.}
}
\]

If that lands, then the three-run fixed-shear quotient-visible lane is effectively closed:

- strict inequality would be the only real overlap case;
- equality is sterile;
- quartic–sextic is already inert because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\).

That is the sharpest current math attack I see in light of the updated paper.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:12:29)

_Thought for 8s_

I can push the touching case one step further.

The right refinement is: in the equality case
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2,
\]
the distance-two coincidence is not just set-theoretically isolated downstairs. It is also **first-order transverse as a value-space contact**, so there is no hidden tangent family that could reopen it upstairs.

That gives a cleaner sterility statement.

---

Work downstairs on the quotient branch \(u=t^2\), with
\[
\widehat Q'(u)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u),
\]
and suppose we are in the three-run case. Let \(J_0,J_1,J_2\) be the three monotone runs of \(\widehat Q\), and let \(q_*\) be the unique common touched value between the two outer runs in the equality case.

Because \(\widehat Q\) is strictly monotone on \(J_0\) and \(J_2\), each restriction has a local inverse on its image:
\[
\phi_0:\widehat Q(J_0)\to J_0,\qquad
\phi_2:\widehat Q(J_2)\to J_2.
\]
In the equality case, the common image is only
\[
\widehat Q(J_0)\cap \widehat Q(J_2)=\{q_*\}.
\]
So there is no open interval on which both inverses coexist.

That already showed set-theoretic sterility. The extra step is to encode the repeated-pair relation as a zero set in value space.

Define
\[
F(q):=\phi_0(q)-\phi_2(q)
\]
whenever both inverses exist. In the strict-overlap case, \(F\) is defined on an interval of \(q\)-values. In the touching case, it is not: the common domain collapses to a single point \(q_*\). So there is literally no one-variable family of same-value pairs to differentiate.

This means the obstruction is stronger than “a branch fails to continue smoothly.” There is no ambient interval in the value variable to support continuation at all.

That already kills any downstairs branch in the strongest possible way.

Now let me translate that into a more geometric local normal form near the touching value.

Suppose \(u_0\in \overline{J_0}\) and \(u_2\in \overline{J_2}\) satisfy
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2)=q_*.
\]
Since these are endpoints of monotone runs, they are critical endpoints for the distance-two comparison. Write local expansions
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_*+a_0 (u-u_0)^{m_0}+O((u-u_0)^{m_0+1}),
\]
\[
\widehat Q(v)=q_*+a_2 (v-u_2)^{m_2}+O((v-u_2)^{m_2+1}),
\]
with \(m_0,m_2\ge 1\), and with signs determined by whether \(q_*\) is a top or bottom endpoint for the corresponding outer interval.

In the equality/touching case, the two outer runs approach \(q_*\) from **opposite sides of value space exclusion**: one interval ends at \(q_*\) from below and the other begins at \(q_*\) from above, or vice versa, depending on orientation. Equivalently, in a punctured neighborhood of \(q_*\), the sign conditions for membership in the two image intervals are incompatible.

So there is no solution to
\[
\widehat Q(u)=\widehat Q(v)=q
\]
for any \(q\neq q_*\) sufficiently close to \(q_*\).

This shows more than isolation of the touched value. It shows **one-sided separation** in value space. The two outer image intervals meet with boundary contact only; they do not even admit a same-side perturbation.

That is exactly the right sterility statement for branch theory.

A clean way to package it is:

### Boundary-touching sterility lemma
Let \(f_0,f_2\) be continuous strictly monotone maps on intervals \(J_0,J_2\). If their images meet only in a common endpoint value
\[
f_0(J_0)\cap f_2(J_2)=\{q_*\},
\]
then the equal-value relation
\[
\{(u,v)\in J_0\times J_2:\ f_0(u)=f_2(v)\}
\]
is locally zero-dimensional near the pair corresponding to \(q_*\). In particular it contains no local one-parameter branch through that point.

Apply this with \(f_j=\widehat Q|_{J_j}\).

That gives the quotient-level closure of the equality case.

Now let me push the upstairs bookkeeping a bit more carefully.

Upstairs, repeated pairs are of two kinds:

- trivial involutive pairs \(t\leftrightarrow -t\), coming from the quotient map \(u=t^2\);
- nontrivial quotient coincidences between distinct quotient points.

The equality case we are discussing concerns the second kind. Since downstairs the equal-value relation between the two outer runs is locally zero-dimensional, lifting by \(u=t^2\) can at most produce finitely many isolated upstairs pairs near the touched configuration. It cannot create a new local branch, because the lift of a zero-dimensional germ is still zero-dimensional.

So the lift does not reopen the obstruction.

That removes the main caveat from the previous round.

This gives, I think, the correct theorem candidate:

### Touching-is-sterile theorem
On a quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch with at most three sign-runs of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), if the distance-two lobe criterion is in the equality case
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2,
\]
then the corresponding distance-two repeated-value set downstairs is a boundary-touching zero-dimensional germ. Its lift upstairs under \(u=t^2\) is still zero-dimensional. Hence equality cannot support a genuine local repeated-pair branch.

If this is right, then the three-run fixed-shear lane is effectively reduced to the strict-inequality case
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
because the equality case is now sterile.

That leaves a very sharp live target:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Exclude the strict three-lobe obstruction }B_1>B_0+B_2\text{ on the actual RH quotient-visible transport branch.}
}
\]

So the endgame in this lane is now even cleaner:

- quartic-sextic rung inert;
- equality case sterile;
- only strict three-lobe excess remains.

That is the sharpest continuation I see right now.
## User (2026-04-21 20:14:03)

continue your math attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:14:12)

_Thought for 8s_

The only live point in the quotient-visible fixed-shear three-run model now really is the **strict excess case**
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
\]
So the next attack should be to understand what this strict inequality would force geometrically on the actual quotient transport branch, and whether that is compatible with the RH package.

I think there is a useful reduction here.

---

## 1. Strict excess means a genuine open reentry interval downstairs

Work downstairs in \(u=t^2\) with quotient-visible scalar
\[
\widehat Q(u),\qquad \widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
and suppose we are in the three-run case with runs \(J_0,J_1,J_2\).

Let
\[
I_j:=\widehat Q(J_j)
\]
be the image intervals. The three-lobe criterion says:

- if
  \[
  B_1<B_0+B_2,
  \]
  there is no distance-two overlap;
- if
  \[
  B_1=B_0+B_2,
  \]
  there is only boundary touching;
- if
  \[
  B_1>B_0+B_2,
  \]
  then
  \[
  \operatorname{Int}(I_0)\cap \operatorname{Int}(I_2)\neq\varnothing.
  \]

So strict excess is equivalent to the existence of an **open value interval**
\[
\mathcal I \subset I_0\cap I_2
\]
such that for every \(q\in\mathcal I\) there are unique points
\[
u_0(q)\in J_0,\qquad u_2(q)\in J_2
\]
with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0(q))=\widehat Q(u_2(q))=q.
\]

Thus the distance-two repeated-pair set is an honest one-parameter branch downstairs.

So the real question is now:

> can the actual RH quotient-visible transport branch support such an open same-value interval between the two outer runs?

That is the sharp live problem.

---

## 2. Strict excess creates a nontrivial transport correspondence

Because \(\widehat Q\) is strictly monotone on each outer run, we get inverse maps
\[
\phi_0:\mathcal I\to J_0,\qquad
\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_2
\]
and hence a transport correspondence
\[
q \longmapsto (\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q)).
\]

Equivalently, after eliminating \(q\), we get a real analytic bijection
\[
T:J_0'\to J_2',\qquad \widehat Q(T(u))=\widehat Q(u)
\]
between subintervals \(J_0'\subset J_0\), \(J_2'\subset J_2\).

Differentiating gives
\[
\widehat Q'(T(u))\,T'(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
hence
\[
T'(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(T(u))}.
\]

Since the two outer runs have the same sign of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), this shows \(T\) is orientation-preserving.

This is the first useful gain: strict excess is not merely an inequality in amplitudes. It creates a genuine analytic transport involution-like correspondence between two separated quotient arcs.

---

## 3. The middle run becomes a barrier

Now add the middle run \(J_1\). Its image interval \(I_1\) contains the whole value drop/rise between the outer lobes, and strict excess says precisely that the middle excursion is larger than the two outer excursions combined.

That means every \(q\in\mathcal I\) is represented:

- once on \(J_0\),
- once on \(J_2\),

and, depending on orientation, typically not on the middle run at all in the distance-two comparison. So the middle run acts as a **barrier run** whose total excursion separates the two outer branches but still leaves an overlapping outer value window.

This suggests a variational interpretation of the strict excess:
the middle run has “overshot” enough to bring the two outer monotone graphs back into common value range.

So if strict excess is to be excluded, it should be by some **transport convexity / variation budget** statement, not by a local zero-count argument.

That is a useful conceptual shift.

---

## 4. Reformulating strict excess as a budget identity

Choose endpoints so that
\[
B_j = \int_{J_j} |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]
Then
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
becomes
\[
\int_{J_1} |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du
>
\int_{J_0} |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du
+
\int_{J_2} |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]

So the strict three-lobe obstruction is exactly an **\(L^1\)-dominance** of the middle run over the two outer runs.

That is probably the right quantitative form.

It says the middle lobe carries more total transport variation than the two neighboring outer lobes together.

So the next natural attack is:

> can the actual RH quotient transport law allow a sign-run of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) whose \(L^1\)-mass dominates the two adjacent runs combined?

That sounds much more like the right global transport question.

---

## 5. Why quartic–sextic inertness matters here

The updated paper already marks the quartic–sextic rung as inert for this lobe-amplitude obstruction because
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0.
\]
So the strict excess case cannot be born infinitesimally at the base rung. It would have to arise only after genuine transport evolution along the quotient branch. This means the obstruction is not local; it is a **global run-amplitude phenomenon**. That fits perfectly with the budget view above. turn12file1

So any proof against strict excess should probably use some monotonicity or variation principle along the branch, not a local Taylor argument.

---

## 6. A sharper theorem target

I think the next serious theorem should be something like:

### Run-budget theorem
On the actual quotient-visible exact fixed-shear transport branch arising from the RH package, consecutive sign-run amplitudes of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) satisfy
\[
B_1\le B_0+B_2.
\]

This is exactly the missing inequality.

A more flexible version would be:

### Weak run-budget theorem
The strict inequality
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
cannot occur; equality is possible only as sterile touching.

That would close the three-run model completely.

---

## 7. What kind of structure could prove this?

The generator audit matters here. The paper now says there is no second primitive **pointwise** highest-new generator beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\), and the fixed-shear residue is more likely a **nonlocal transport** issue than a new pointwise field. So the run-budget theorem should probably not be sought as an algebraic identity in a local jet. It should come from a nonlocal transport invariant or variation inequality. turn12file1

That suggests three plausible directions.

First, a **variation-diminishing** property for the transport law of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), which would directly bound central run mass by adjacent masses.

Second, a **comparison principle** for successive extrema of \(\widehat Q\), turning the lobe amplitudes into a monotone sequence or a discrete convexity sequence.

Third, an **endpoint-gap monotonicity transplanted downstairs**: if the same endpoint-gap monotonicity that was useful in the one-pair affine geometry has an exact fixed-shear quotient analogue, it might force the outer-run same-value correspondence to be one-shot or sterile rather than open.

The third is especially attractive because it matches the overall paper direction: monotone gap functions were the key reduction on the affine side, and the fixed-shear lane is now also a no-hidden-reset problem.

---

## 8. A concrete new object to attack

Given the transport correspondence \(T\) from section 2, define the quotient gap
\[
H(u):=T(u)-u
\]
on the overlapping subinterval \(J_0'\subset J_0\).

Then
\[
H'(u)=T'(u)-1
=
\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(T(u))}-1.
\]

So if one could show that along the actual RH quotient transport branch the ratio
\[
\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(T(u))}
\]
has a definite sign pattern or monotonicity forcing \(H\) to be one-shot, that would rule out reentry.

This is the exact fixed-shear analogue of an endpoint-gap function.

So the next local-global bridge is:

> strict three-lobe excess induces a quotient same-value correspondence \(T\), and the right object to study is the gap \(H(u)=T(u)-u\).

That feels much sharper than working only with the amplitudes \(B_j\).

---

## 9. Strongest new structural finding

The strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Strict three-lobe excess is equivalent to the existence of an open analytic same-value transport correspondence }T\text{ between the two outer runs.}
}
\]

So the final mixed fixed-shear problem is not just an inequality in three numbers. It is the existence of a nontrivial quotient transport map \(T\) whose gap function should be attacked directly.

That is a real sharpening of the lane.

---

## 10. Best next attack

So the next theorem I would attack is:

### Quotient-gap monotonicity theorem
Assume strict three-lobe excess on a quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, and let
\[
T:J_0'\to J_2'
\]
be the induced same-value transport map
\[
\widehat Q(T(u))=\widehat Q(u).
\]
Then the gap
\[
H(u)=T(u)-u
\]
is monotone or one-shot in a way incompatible with an open repeated-pair branch on the actual RH transport branch.

A weaker but still decisive version is just:

### No open same-value transport theorem
The actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear transport branch cannot support an open analytic same-value correspondence between separated runs.

If that lands, the strict inequality case dies, and the whole three-run quotient-visible model is closed.

That is the sharpest continuation I see now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:18:19)

_Thought for 1m 47s_

I think there is a genuinely sharper reduction now.

The strict three-lobe obstruction
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
does not just give an open same-value correspondence between the two outer runs. It actually forces a **same-value / same-slope pair** downstairs on the quotient branch.

That is a much stronger object to attack.

---

## 1. Setup on the quotient-visible fixed-shear branch

Work downstairs in the quotient variable
\[
u=t^2
\]
with
\[
\widehat Q(u),\qquad \widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]
Assume we are in the three-run case of the updated paper, with sign-runs \(J_0,J_1,J_2\) for \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), and with the outer runs carrying the same sign. The three-lobe criterion says that the only live overlap case is the strict excess case
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
while equality is only touching, and the quartic–sextic rung is inert because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\). turn12file1

Strict excess is equivalent to an open value interval
\[
\mathcal I \subset \widehat Q(J_0)\cap \widehat Q(J_2)
\]
on which there are unique inverse branches
\[
\phi_0:\mathcal I\to J_0,\qquad
\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_2
\]
satisfying
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]

So the strict excess case gives a genuine analytic same-value transport between the two outer runs.

---

## 2. Define the quotient-gap in \(u\)-space

Set
\[
G(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),\qquad q\in\mathcal I.
\]
Then \(G(q)>0\), and since
\[
\phi_j'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))}
\]
on each monotone run, we get
\[
G'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

This formula is the key.

---

## 3. Endpoint behavior forces an interior critical point of \(G\)

At one end of the overlap interval \(\mathcal I\), one of the two outer inverse branches approaches a critical endpoint of its run, so \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\to 0\) there, while the other branch stays at a noncritical point with \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\neq 0\). At the other end of \(\mathcal I\), the roles reverse.

Therefore \(G'(q)\) has opposite signs at the two ends of \(\mathcal I\). Concretely, in the up–down–up picture with \(\widehat{\mathcal D}>0\) on both outer runs, one gets
\[
G'(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{at one end of }\mathcal I,
\qquad
G'(q)\to +\infty
\quad\text{at the other end.}
\]

So by the intermediate value theorem there exists
\[
q_*\in \mathcal I
\]
such that
\[
G'(q_*)=0.
\]

By the formula for \(G'\), this is equivalent to
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q_*))
=
\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]

But by definition of \(\phi_0,\phi_2\),
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))
=
\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))
=
q_*.
\]

So we have proved:

\[
\boxed{
B_1>B_0+B_2
\ \Longrightarrow\
\exists\, u_0\in J_0,\ u_2\in J_2,\ u_0<u_2,
\text{ such that }
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\ 
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_0)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_2).
}
\]

This is the new theorem-level compression.

---

## 4. Reformulation as a self-intersection of the jet map

Define the quotient jet map
\[
\mathcal J(u):=\bigl(\widehat Q(u),\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\bigr).
\]

Then the result above says:

\[
\boxed{
B_1>B_0+B_2
\ \Longrightarrow\
\mathcal J \text{ has a self-intersection between the two outer runs.}
}
\]

That is much stronger than “there is an open same-value transport correspondence.” It says the strict excess case forces the first quotient jet to repeat.

So the final mixed fixed-shear problem can now be attacked as a **jet-injectivity problem**, not just an amplitude-inequality problem.

---

## 5. Why this is a real gain

This sharpens the lane in two ways.

First, equality/touching was already reduced to a zero-dimensional same-value germ and so is sterile. So the only live case was strict excess. The result above shows that strict excess cannot hide merely in value overlap; it must force a repeated pair already at the level of
\[
(\widehat Q,\widehat Q').
\]

Second, the updated paper already says there is no new second primitive pointwise highest-new generator beyond the current explicit package. So looking for a new local scalar was probably the wrong endgame. But injectivity or no-self-intersection of a **transport jet map** is a nonlocal statement, which fits exactly with the paper’s current conclusion that the fixed-shear residue is a nonlocal transport problem, not a new pointwise field problem. turn12file1

So this attack is aligned with the paper’s actual frontier.

---

## 6. A clean new theorem target

The right next theorem to try is now:

### Quotient jet no-self-intersection theorem
On the actual RH quotient-visible exact fixed-shear transport branch, the map
\[
u\longmapsto \bigl(\widehat Q(u),\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\bigr)
\]
has no self-intersection between separated sign-runs.

If that holds, then strict excess
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
is impossible.

Since equality is already sterile, that would close the three-run quotient-visible fixed-shear lane completely.

---

## 7. A slightly weaker but still decisive version

Even weaker would be enough:

### Outer-run jet separation theorem
For any two separated outer runs \(J_0,J_2\) in the quotient-visible fixed-shear model, there do not exist \(u_0\in J_0\), \(u_2\in J_2\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_0)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_2).
\]

This is exactly the obstruction produced by strict excess, so ruling it out is sufficient.

---

## 8. One more useful consequence

At the extremal point \(q_*\) where \(G'(q_*)=0\), the corresponding same-value transport map
\[
T(\phi_0(q))=\phi_2(q)
\]
satisfies
\[
T'(\phi_0(q_*))=1,
\]
because
\[
T'(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(T(u))}.
\]
So strict excess forces not only a same-value pair, but an interior point where the induced transport map has unit derivative.

Equivalently, the gap
\[
H(u):=T(u)-u
\]
has a critical point:
\[
H'(u_*)=0.
\]

So the live strict-excess case can also be phrased as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess forces an interior stationary point of the quotient same-value gap.}
}
\]

This is the exact fixed-shear analogue of the endpoint-gap story on the one-pair/global side.

That may be the most natural form to connect with the monotone-gap theorems elsewhere in the paper.

---

## 9. Bottom line

The sharpest continuation I see is:

- equality \(B_1=B_0+B_2\) is sterile;
- strict excess \(B_1>B_0+B_2\) forces a self-intersection of the quotient jet map
  \[
  u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)),
  \]
  equivalently an interior stationary point of the same-value transport gap.

So the final mixed fixed-shear task is no longer just “rule out a three-number inequality.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove no separated same-value / same-slope pair, or no interior stationary point of the quotient same-value gap, on the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear branch.}
}
\]

That is the strongest math reduction I can see right now.

_Thought for 1m 4s_

I think there is one more clean reduction.

The strict three-lobe obstruction
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
now boils down to the existence of an interior zero of a very explicit **reciprocal-slope gap** downstairs. That is a sharper object than the raw transport gap \(H(u)=T(u)-u\).

This fits the updated paper well: the one-pair/global side is now about monotone endpoint-gap functions, and the fixed-shear side should probably also be attacked by a monotone gap, but in quotient transport rather than affine incidence. turn15file1

---

## 1. Outer-run transport in value space

Work downstairs on the quotient-visible fixed-shear branch with
\[
\widehat Q(u),\qquad \widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]
In the only live three-run case, strict excess
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
gives an open overlap interval
\[
\mathcal I\subset \widehat Q(J_0)\cap \widehat Q(J_2)
\]
between the two outer runs, and hence inverse branches
\[
\phi_0:\mathcal I\to J_0,\qquad
\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_2
\]
with
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]

Define the \(u\)-gap
\[
G(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then
\[
G'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

So the strict excess case is governed not just by value overlap, but by the crossing behavior of the two reciprocal-slope profiles
\[
R_0(q):=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))},
\qquad
R_2(q):=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))}.
\]

---

## 2. Strict excess forces a reciprocal-slope crossing

At one end of \(\mathcal I\), one outer branch approaches a critical endpoint of its run, so the corresponding reciprocal slope blows up, while the other remains finite. At the other end, the roles reverse. Therefore
\[
R_2(q)-R_0(q)
\]
has opposite signs at the two ends of \(\mathcal I\). Hence there exists
\[
q_*\in \mathcal I
\]
such that
\[
R_2(q_*)=R_0(q_*).
\]

Equivalently,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q_*))
=
\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]
Together with
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*,
\]
this is exactly the same-value / same-slope pair I got last turn.

But phrased this way, the real point is:

\[
\boxed{
B_1>B_0+B_2
\ \Longrightarrow\
R_2-R_0 \text{ has an interior zero on }\mathcal I.
}
\]

So the final fixed-shear problem is now a zero problem for a quotient reciprocal-slope gap.

---

## 3. This is the exact fixed-shear analogue of endpoint-gap monotonicity

This is the most satisfying part.

On the updated one-pair/global side, the remaining burden is remote endpoint/branch incidence, encoded by monotone endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\). On the fixed-shear quotient side, the live strict-excess obstruction is now also encoded by a gap function:
\[
\Gamma(q):=R_2(q)-R_0(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

Strict excess is equivalent to \(\Gamma\) having an interior zero.

So the mixed fixed-shear lane has been reduced to the same kind of endgame as the nonmixed/global lane:

- there, exclude zeros of endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\);
- here, exclude interior zeros of the reciprocal-slope gap \(\Gamma\).

That is a real unification of the picture.

---

## 4. Derivative of the reciprocal-slope gap

Now differentiate \(\Gamma\). Since
\[
\phi_j'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))},
\]
we get
\[
R_j'(q)
=
-\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(\phi_j(q))}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))^3}.
\]
Therefore
\[
\Gamma'(q)
=
-\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(\phi_2(q))}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))^3}
+
\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))^3}.
\]

So a monotonicity theorem for \(\Gamma\) would follow from an ordering theorem for the quantity
\[
\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}
\]
between the two outer runs.

This is a clean new object.

---

## 5. Best new theorem target

The sharp next target is now:

### Reciprocal-slope gap monotonicity theorem
On the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear transport branch, for any two separated outer runs \(J_0,J_2\) and overlap interval \(\mathcal I\), the gap
\[
\Gamma(q)=
\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))}
\]
is one-sign or monotone on \(\mathcal I\).

If that holds, then \(\Gamma\) has no interior zero, so strict excess
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
is impossible.

A slightly weaker but still decisive version is:

### No reciprocal-slope crossing theorem
For separated outer runs in the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear model, the two reciprocal-slope profiles
\[
R_0(q),\qquad R_2(q)
\]
do not cross in the interior of their common value interval.

That is exactly equivalent to ruling out strict excess.

---

## 6. Why this is better than attacking \(B_1>B_0+B_2\) directly

This is a real improvement.

The amplitude inequality is an integrated \(L^1\) statement:
\[
B_j=\int_{J_j} |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]
That is a bit coarse.

The reciprocal-slope gap \(\Gamma\) is a **pointwise transport observable** along common value level \(q\). It directly measures whether the same-value correspondence is pulling the two runs together or apart in \(u\)-space.

So instead of trying to control three integrated amplitudes globally, we can try to prove that the actual RH transport law forbids a pointwise crossing of reciprocal slopes between separated runs. That is much more like the monotone-gap mechanisms already working elsewhere in the paper.

---

## 7. Strongest new structural finding

The strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The strict three-lobe obstruction is equivalent to an interior zero of the quotient reciprocal-slope gap }\Gamma.
}
\]

So the mixed fixed-shear endgame is no longer an abstract “three-number inequality.” It is a one-variable zero problem for a gap function, exactly parallel in spirit to the updated one-pair/global endgame.

That feels like the right final compression.

---

## 8. Honest status

I do **not** yet have a proof that \(\Gamma\) is monotone or one-sign on the actual RH branch.

But the attack has now reduced the live strict-excess case to a very narrow theorem:

- equality \(B_1=B_0+B_2\) is already sterile;
- strict excess \(B_1>B_0+B_2\) is equivalent to interior crossing of reciprocal-slope profiles;
- so the final fixed-shear task is to exclude that crossing.

That is the sharpest current attack I can see.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:27:39)

I’m pushing directly on the new gap observable \(\Gamma\) now. The goal is to replace the raw amplitude inequality \(B_1>B_0+B_2\) with an equivalent one-variable crossing problem that might admit the same kind of monotonicity closure as the endpoint-gap functions on the nonmixed side.

_Thought for 31s_

I think the next clean reduction is to replace the strict three-lobe inequality by a **jet-injectivity problem for a two-component quotient transport map**.

This is a real sharpening of the previous step.

---

## 1. From amplitude excess to reciprocal-slope crossing

On the quotient-visible fixed-shear branch, strict excess
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
gives an open overlap interval \(\mathcal I\) in value space and inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2
\]
on the two outer runs, with
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]

We defined the reciprocal-slope profiles
\[
R_j(q):=\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))},
\]
and the gap
\[
\Gamma(q):=R_2(q)-R_0(q).
\]

The previous reduction was:
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2 \Longrightarrow \Gamma \text{ has an interior zero.}
\]

Equivalently, there exist \(u_0\in J_0\), \(u_2\in J_2\) such that
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_0)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_2).
\]

So strict excess forces a self-intersection of the quotient jet map
\[
u\longmapsto \bigl(\widehat Q(u),\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\bigr).
\]

That was already a real gain.

---

## 2. A more natural jet map: \((\widehat Q,1/\widehat{\mathcal D})\)

But because the crossing is really phrased in terms of inverse branches and reciprocal slopes, the most natural object is not
\[
(\widehat Q,\widehat{\mathcal D}),
\]
but rather
\[
\mathcal K(u):=\Bigl(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}\Bigr).
\]

Then strict excess is equivalent to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The map }\mathcal K(u)=\Bigl(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}\Bigr)
\text{ has a self-intersection between the two outer runs.}
}
\]

Indeed, on the overlap interval \(\mathcal I\), the two outer branches are parametrized by the same value variable \(q\), and their second coordinates are exactly the reciprocal-slope profiles \(R_0(q)\) and \(R_2(q)\). A crossing of these profiles is exactly a self-intersection of \(\mathcal K\).

This is better than the raw amplitude inequality because it packages the problem as injectivity of a **two-component transport jet**.

---

## 3. Strict excess becomes a zero problem for a first-order transport invariant

Now differentiate the reciprocal-slope profiles with respect to \(q\):
\[
R_j'(q)=-\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(\phi_j(q))}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))^3}.
\]

So if we define the quotient transport curvature observable
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3},
\]
then
\[
R_j'(q)=-\Xi(\phi_j(q)).
\]

Therefore
\[
\Gamma'(q)
=
-\Xi(\phi_2(q))+\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
\]

This is the key formula.

It says the reciprocal-slope gap is monotone if the observable \(\Xi\) is ordered between the two outer runs.

So the strict-excess problem has now been compressed further:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Strict excess can occur only if the transport curvature observable }
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}
\text{ crosses between the two outer runs at equal }\widehat Q\text{-value.}
}
\]

That is a much sharper pointwise condition than the original amplitude inequality.

---

## 4. Equivalent formulations

There are now four equivalent ways to phrase the live strict-excess obstruction.

### (a) Amplitude form
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
\]

### (b) Same-value correspondence form
There is an open interval of values \(q\) with two separated preimages on the outer runs.

### (c) Reciprocal-slope crossing form
The gap
\[
\Gamma(q)=\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))}
\]
has an interior zero.

### (d) Jet self-intersection form
The map
\[
\mathcal K(u)=\Bigl(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}\Bigr)
\]
has a self-intersection between separated outer runs.

The last two are the most useful for attack.

---

## 5. Why this is closer to the nonmixed/global endgame

The updated paper says the one-pair/global lane has been compressed to zeros of monotone endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\). Here we now have an exact fixed-shear analogue:

- one-pair/global side: exclude zeros of an endpoint-gap;
- fixed-shear side: exclude zeros of a reciprocal-slope gap \(\Gamma\).

So both live endgames are now one-variable zero problems for gap functions.

That is a real unification of the overall proof picture.  

---

## 6. A better theorem target than raw monotonicity of \(\Gamma\)

Earlier I phrased the target as “prove \(\Gamma\) is monotone.” That is one way, but there is a more intrinsic statement now:

### Quotient reciprocal-slope jet separation theorem
For separated outer runs \(J_0,J_2\) on the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear branch, there do not exist \(u_0\in J_0\), \(u_2\in J_2\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),
\qquad
\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_0)}=\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_2)}.
\]

Equivalently, the transport jet map
\[
u\mapsto \Bigl(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}\Bigr)
\]
is injective across separated outer runs.

If this holds, strict excess is impossible.

This feels sharper than asking directly for monotonicity of \(\Gamma\), because it states exactly the geometric obstruction we need to exclude.

---

## 7. One more reduction: a same-value stationary-point criterion

There is also a clean dynamical interpretation.

Let
\[
T:J_0'\to J_2'
\]
be the same-value transport map induced by strict excess:
\[
\widehat Q(T(u))=\widehat Q(u).
\]
Then
\[
T'(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(T(u))}.
\]
So at a reciprocal-slope crossing,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(T(u)),
\]
hence
\[
T'(u)=1.
\]

Therefore strict excess forces an interior fixed-slope point of the same-value transport:
\[
\boxed{
B_1>B_0+B_2
\Longrightarrow
\exists\,u_* \text{ with } \widehat Q(T(u_*))=\widehat Q(u_*),\ T'(u_*)=1.
}
\]

Equivalently, for the same-value gap
\[
H(u):=T(u)-u,
\]
one has
\[
H'(u_*)=0.
\]

So the strict-excess obstruction can be rephrased either as:

- self-intersection of the jet map \((\widehat Q,1/\widehat{\mathcal D})\), or
- an interior stationary point of the same-value gap \(H\).

Both are strong nonlocal transport signatures.

---

## 8. Strongest new structural finding

The strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The live strict-excess case is exactly a self-intersection problem for the quotient reciprocal-slope jet }
u\mapsto\Bigl(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}\Bigr).
}
\]

That is sharper than the previous \((\widehat Q,\widehat{\mathcal D})\)-jet formulation, because it uses the natural value-space parametrization of the transport correspondence itself.

It also produces the clean derived observable
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3},
\]
whose equal-value ordering between the outer runs controls the sign of \(\Gamma'\).

So the final fixed-shear lane has now been reduced to a pointwise transport-jet separation problem.

---

## 9. Best next theorem target

The next theorem I would attack is:

### Reciprocal-slope jet injectivity theorem
On the actual RH quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, the map
\[
u\mapsto\Bigl(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}\Bigr)
\]
has no self-intersection between separated outer runs.

A slightly weaker but still decisive version is:

### Ordered transport-curvature theorem
At equal \(\widehat Q\)-value on separated outer runs, the observable
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}
\]
has a fixed ordering, so the reciprocal-slope gap \(\Gamma\) is one-sign and cannot vanish.

Either one would rule out strict excess. Since equality is already sterile, that would close the three-run fixed-shear quotient-visible lane.

That is the sharpest continuation I see now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:29:40)

_Thought for 24s_

I think there is one more real reduction, and it is better than just saying “strict excess gives a reciprocal-slope crossing.”

The sharp point is this:

on the quotient-visible fixed-shear branch, the live strict-excess case is governed by the reciprocal-slope profiles
\[
R_j(q):=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))},
\]
and these satisfy a scalar ODE in the value variable \(q\). So if the transport-curvature observable
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}
\]
is itself single-valued as a function of \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across the two outer runs, then strict excess is basically impossible.

That is a cleaner theorem target than raw jet injectivity.

---

## 1. The ODE for reciprocal-slope profiles

On an overlap interval
\[
\mathcal I\subset \widehat Q(J_0)\cap \widehat Q(J_2),
\]
let
\[
\phi_j:\mathcal I\to J_j
\]
be the inverse branches on the two outer runs, so
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_j(q))=q.
\]
Define
\[
R_j(q):=\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))}.
\]

Then, as already noted,
\[
R_j'(q)
=
-\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(\phi_j(q))}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_j(q))^3}
=
-\Xi(\phi_j(q)).
\]

So the pair \(R_0,R_2\) is governed by the same scalar first-order transport law, evaluated along two equal-\(\widehat Q\) lifts.

Now here is the key observation:

if there exists a scalar function \(F\) such that on the two outer runs
\[
\Xi(u)=F(\widehat Q(u)),
\]
then both reciprocal-slope profiles satisfy the **same ODE**
\[
R_j'(q)=-F(q).
\]

Hence their difference
\[
\Gamma(q):=R_2(q)-R_0(q)
\]
satisfies
\[
\Gamma'(q)=0.
\]
So \(\Gamma\) is constant on the whole overlap interval.

This is a big gain.

---

## 2. Why constant \(\Gamma\) is fatal to strict excess

Strict excess
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
forces an overlap interval \(\mathcal I\), and from the previous attack it also forces \(\Gamma\) to have an interior zero:
\[
\exists q_*\in\mathcal I\quad \Gamma(q_*)=0.
\]
If \(\Gamma\) is constant, then
\[
\Gamma\equiv 0
\quad\text{on }\mathcal I.
\]
So strict excess would force
\[
R_0(q)=R_2(q)
\quad\text{for every }q\in\mathcal I,
\]
equivalently
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_0(q))=\widehat{\mathcal D}(\phi_2(q))
\quad\text{for all }q\in\mathcal I.
\]

Then the \(u\)-gap
\[
G(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
satisfies
\[
G'(q)=R_2(q)-R_0(q)=0,
\]
so
\[
G(q)\equiv \text{constant}.
\]

Thus strict excess would force the two outer inverse branches to differ by a constant \(u\)-translation:
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+C.
\]

Equivalently, on subintervals of the two outer runs,
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u+C)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u).
\]

That is extremely rigid. For a separated pair of outer runs bounded by distinct critical points, a nontrivial translation symmetry of \(\widehat Q\) on an open interval is not compatible with the usual run-endpoint geometry unless the branch has a very special periodic-type structure, which the present RH transport picture does not suggest at all.

So the attack has reduced strict excess to an implausibly rigid symmetry statement.

---

## 3. A clean theorem schema

This gives the following theorem target.

### Single-valued transport-curvature theorem
On the actual RH quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, the observable
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}
\]
is single-valued as a function of \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across separated outer runs. Equivalently, there exists \(F\) such that
\[
\Xi(u)=F(\widehat Q(u))
\]
on the union of the two outer runs.

Then the reciprocal-slope profiles satisfy the same ODE
\[
R'(q)=-F(q),
\]
so their gap \(\Gamma\) is constant. Since strict excess would force an interior zero of \(\Gamma\), one gets \(\Gamma\equiv 0\), hence a constant \(u\)-gap and therefore an open translation symmetry of \((\widehat Q,\widehat{\mathcal D})\) between separated runs.

So to exclude strict excess it would then suffice to exclude this translation symmetry.

This is much sharper than a direct attack on the amplitude inequality.

---

## 4. Why this fits the current paper frontier

This matches the updated paper extremely well.

The generator audit says there is no second primitive **pointwise** highest-new generator beyond the current explicit package. So one should not expect a new local scalar to save the day.

But the statement
\[
\Xi(u)=F(\widehat Q(u))
\]
is not introducing a new generator. It is a **transport integrability statement**: the curvature observable of the reciprocal-slope transport depends only on the value coordinate downstairs.

That is exactly the kind of nonlocal transport structure the updated paper suggests is the real remaining issue on the fixed-shear side.

So this attack is aligned with the draft’s current state, not fighting it.

---

## 5. One more reduction: the no-translation version

There is also a slightly weaker and perhaps more natural theorem.

Instead of proving full single-valuedness of \(\Xi\) as a function of \(\widehat Q\), it would already be enough to prove:

### No constant-gap theorem
On separated outer runs of the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear branch, there is no open overlap interval on which
\[
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
\]
for a constant \(C\neq 0\).

Because, as above, strict excess plus single-valued \(\Xi\) forces exactly this constant-gap behavior.

So the endgame can be split into two narrower pieces:

1. prove \(\Xi\) is single-valued over \(\widehat Q\);
2. rule out constant-gap transport between separated runs.

Either one of these may be easier to attack directly than the original amplitude inequality.

---

## 6. Best new structural finding

The strongest new structural conclusion from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If }\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}
\text{ is single-valued over }q=\widehat Q(u),
\text{ then strict three-lobe excess forces a constant-gap translation symmetry between the two outer runs.}
}
\]

So the live mixed fixed-shear obstruction has now been compressed to a very rigid alternative:

- either the transport-curvature observable is not single-valued over \(\widehat Q\), which would itself be the first genuinely nontrivial transport obstruction;
- or it is single-valued, in which case strict excess forces an implausible translation symmetry.

That is a much sharper fork than before.

---

## 7. Honest status

I do **not** yet know how to prove
\[
\Xi(u)=F(\widehat Q(u))
\]
from the current paper, nor do I yet have a formal theorem excluding the constant-gap translation symmetry.

But this is now a very tight target. The strict three-run fixed-shear obstruction is no longer a vague global transport issue. It has been reduced to:

1. single-valuedness of the transport-curvature observable over the quotient value coordinate, and
2. exclusion of constant-gap same-value transport between separated runs.

That is the sharpest continuation I see right now.

I think there is one more real compression.

The last reduction showed:

- strict excess
  \[
  B_1>B_0+B_2

  gives an open same-value transport between the two outer runs;
- if
  \[
  \Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}

- hence the two outer inverse branches differ by a constant \(u\)-translation.

The next step is to push that constant-gap conclusion through the whole explicit pointwise package.

That gives a much sharper statement than just “there is a transport symmetry.”

## 1. Constant-gap transport forces equality of the full quotient jet

Assume we are in the strict-excess case and that \(\Xi\) is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\). Then, as argued last turn, there is a nonzero constant \(C\) and an open interval \(U\subset J_0\) such that
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\qquad (u\in U).
\]
Differentiating,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u+C)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u).
\]
Differentiating repeatedly,
\[
\widehat Q^{(k)}(u+C)=\widehat Q^{(k)}(u)
\qquad\text{for all }k\ge 0,\ u\in U.
\]

So strict excess would force not just same value and same slope, but equality of the **entire quotient jet**
\[
j^\infty\widehat Q(u+C)=j^\infty\widehat Q(u)
\]
on an open interval.

That is already extremely rigid.

---

## 2. Translation symmetry propagates to every explicit pointwise field built from \(\widehat Q\)

Now the paper update matters.

The derivative-geometry audit says the explicit pointwise fields currently in play stay in the differential algebra generated by the lower field and \(r=q^{(7)}\), and no second primitive pointwise highest-new generator has been found. In the fixed-shear quotient-visible model, the live downstairs object is \(\widehat Q\) and its transport derivative \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\), together with the explicit package derived from them. So if the full quotient jet of \(\widehat Q\) agrees under translation, every explicit pointwise field derived from that jet also agrees under translation. 

Concretely, under the constant-gap conclusion one gets on \(U\):
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u+C)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u),
\]
\[
\Xi(u+C)=\Xi(u),
\]
and similarly every explicit pointwise transport observable built differentially from \(\widehat Q\) matches at \(u\) and \(u+C\).

So the strict-excess case plus single-valued \(\Xi\) would force a **same-tower translation symmetry** of the entire currently explicit pointwise quotient-visible package between two separated outer runs.

That is a cleaner way to state the obstruction.

---

## 3. This converts the live strict-excess case into a same-tower symmetry problem

So the fixed-shear endgame can now be reformulated as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess }B_1>B_0+B_2
\ +\ 
\Xi=F(\widehat Q)
\ \Longrightarrow\
\text{a separated same-tower translation symmetry of the explicit quotient-visible pointwise package.}
}
\]

That is much sharper than the amplitude inequality and closer to the paper’s actual current frontier.

The update already says there is no new second primitive pointwise generator. So the real remaining burden cannot be “find one more pointwise scalar.” It has become:

> can the actual RH fixed-shear quotient transport branch support a separated same-tower translation symmetry of its explicit pointwise package?

That is a very concrete nonlocal transport question.

---

## 4. A new theorem target: no separated same-tower translation symmetry

This suggests the next clean theorem.

### Same-tower translation exclusion theorem
On the actual RH quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, there do not exist a nonzero constant \(C\) and an open interval \(U\) such that
\[
j^\infty\widehat Q(u+C)=j^\infty\widehat Q(u)
\qquad (u\in U),
\]
equivalently, such that the entire explicit pointwise quotient-visible package agrees at \(u\) and \(u+C\) on \(U\).

If this is proved, then under the additional transport-integrability hypothesis \(\Xi=F(\widehat Q)\), strict excess is impossible.

This is better than attacking the three-run inequality directly.

---

## 5. Why this is plausibly the right nonlocal obstruction

This fits the updated paper very closely.

The generator audit says the fixed-shear residue may still matter as a **nonlocal transport problem**, not as a new pointwise highest-new field. A separated same-tower translation symmetry is exactly such a nonlocal transport phenomenon: it does not create a new pointwise generator, but it does impose a strong global recurrence of the existing tower. 

So if the fixed-shear lane survives at all, this is exactly the kind of structure it should have to exploit.

That means the attack is now aligned with the actual ceiling established in the draft, not pushing against it.

---

## 6. A weaker version that may be enough

There is also a weaker theorem that would already be decisive.

### No local periodicity theorem
There is no nonzero constant \(C\) and open interval \(U\) on the quotient-visible fixed-shear branch such that
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\qquad (u\in U).
\]

Because once this is excluded, the stronger same-tower translation symmetry is automatically excluded too.

So the strict-excess lane can be split into two subtargets:

1. prove the transport-curvature observable is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\);
2. prove \(\widehat Q\) admits no nontrivial local translation symmetry across separated runs.

If both land, the strict three-lobe obstruction dies.

---

## 7. One more useful corollary

There is a nice corollary here.

If
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on an open interval, then every critical point in that interval translates to a critical point with the same full jet data. So the run-endpoint pattern itself repeats under translation. In a three-run model, that is already highly unstable: a true local translation symmetry tends to propagate the sign-run pattern, threatening to create additional repeated structure beyond the compressed three-run picture.

I would not claim that alone is a contradiction yet, but it does show that strict excess would force a kind of local periodicity incompatible with the “one exceptional three-run” feel of the current model.

So even qualitatively, the strict-excess case is now cornered into a very rigid recurrent pattern.

---

## 8. Best current frontier

I think the fixed-shear lane is now reduced to this fork:

### Route A
Prove
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u)}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)^3}
\]
is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\), and then exclude the resulting same-tower translation symmetry.

### Route B
Bypass \(\Xi\)-single-valuedness and directly prove injectivity of one of the transport jet maps
\[
u\mapsto \left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)}\right)
\quad\text{or}\quad
u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),\widehat{\mathcal D}(u))
\]
across separated outer runs.

Route A feels more integrated with the current paper, because it turns the live obstruction into exactly the kind of nonlocal transport symmetry the generator audit suggests is the real issue.

The sharpest continuation I see is:

\[
\boxed{

}
\]

- either show the transport-curvature observable is not single-valued over \(\widehat Q\), which would itself identify the first genuinely nontrivial transport obstruction;
- or, if it is single-valued, rule out the induced separated translation symmetry of the explicit pointwise package.

That is the strongest reduction I can see right now.
