## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:47:07)

I’m pushing directly on the new defect \(\Lambda(q)\) now. The goal is to see whether the endpoint asymptotics of the outer inverse branches force enough control on \(\Lambda\) near the overlap endpoints to make the strict-excess minimum impossible or at least uniquely rigid.

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next push.

I think the best advance now is to exploit the **endpoint asymptotics** of the outer inverse branches more sharply. This gives a local normal form for the inverse-branch gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
near the ends of the overlap interval, and that in turn constrains how the curvature defect
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q)
\]
can behave.

The key point is that the strict-excess case is not just an arbitrary convexity problem on an interval. The two outer inverse branches meet the overlap interval at **critical endpoints**, so \(\Delta\) has very special square-root-type endpoint asymptotics in the generic simple-critical case.

---

## 1. Endpoint normal form in the simple-critical case

Let \(q_-\) be one endpoint of the common value interval \(\mathcal I\). At \(q_-\), one outer branch, say \(\phi_0\), approaches a critical endpoint \(u_-\) of \(J_0\), while the other branch \(\phi_2\) remains noncritical there.

Assume first that the critical endpoint is simple:
\[
\widehat Q'(u_-)=0,\qquad \widehat Q''(u_-)\neq 0.
\]
Then near \(u_-\),
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_-+a(u-u_-)^2+O((u-u_-)^3),
\qquad a\neq 0.
\]
Inverting,
\[
\phi_0(q)=u_-+c\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-)
\]
for one choice of sign, with \(c\neq 0\).

By contrast, \(\phi_2\) is analytic at \(q_-\), since its point is noncritical:
\[
\phi_2(q)=v_-+b(q-q_-)+O((q-q_-)^2).
\]

Therefore
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
=
(v_--u_-)-c\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-).
\]
Hence
\[
\Delta'(q)=-\frac{c}{2}(q-q_-)^{-1/2}+O(1),
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=\frac{c}{4}(q-q_-)^{-3/2}+O((q-q_-)^{-1/2}).
\]

So near that endpoint, \(\Delta''\) has a definite singular sign. Equivalently,
\[
\Lambda(q)=-\Delta''(q)
\]
also has a definite singular sign near the endpoint.

The same analysis at the other endpoint \(q_+\) gives the **opposite** singular sign, because now the other branch becomes critical and the roles reverse.

This is the main new gain.

---

## 2. Consequence: \(\Lambda\) must change sign in the strict-excess case

Previously I only used the fact that \(\Delta'\) has opposite infinite signs at the two ends of \(\mathcal I\), which forced an interior minimum of \(\Delta\).

Now we get more:

in the simple-critical case,
\[
\Delta''(q)\to +\infty
\quad\text{at one endpoint,}
\qquad
\Delta''(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{at the other}
\]
up to the orientation convention. Equivalently,
\[
\Lambda(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{at one endpoint,}
\qquad
\Lambda(q)\to +\infty
\quad\text{at the other}.
\]

So any strict-excess overlap interval necessarily has a curvature defect \(\Lambda\) with opposite endpoint signs.

That means:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the generic simple-critical setting, the strict-excess case forces at least one zero of }\Lambda\text{ in the interior of }\mathcal I.
}
\]

This is a real sharpening. The live obstruction is no longer merely “an interior minimum of \(\Delta\).” It is:

- an interior minimum of \(\Delta\),
- together with an interior zero of \(\Lambda\).

So the fixed-shear lane has been compressed from a one-scalar sign problem to a very rigid coupled condition.

---

## 3. What the zero of \(\Lambda\) means geometrically

Since
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
a zero of \(\Lambda\) is exactly a point \(q_0\in\mathcal I\) where the two outer inverse branches have the same inverse curvature:
\[
\phi_2''(q_0)=\phi_0''(q_0).
\]

So in the generic strict-excess case, there must exist an interior point where:

- the two branches have the same value \(q\),
- at some other interior point they have the same slope (the minimum of \(\Delta\)),
- and at some interior point they have the same inverse curvature (a zero of \(\Lambda\)).

If those two points coincide, then we get a same-value / same-slope / same-curvature triple coincidence, which is even closer to the affine-transport regime. If they do not coincide, then the strict-excess lane is still forced to thread a very narrow geometry: \(\Delta\) must solve a boundary-value problem with both a critical point and a curvature-sign reversal.

That is much more rigid than before.

---

## 4. Why this helps conceptually

This gives a cleaner fork than “prove \(\Lambda>0\)” or “prove \(\Lambda\equiv 0\).”

Now the strict-excess case must satisfy all of:

1. \(\Delta'\) has opposite infinite endpoint signs;
2. \(\Delta\) has an interior minimum;
3. \(\Lambda=-\Delta''\) has opposite endpoint signs;
4. therefore \(\Lambda\) has an interior zero.

So the live obstruction must exhibit both an interior critical point of \(\Delta\) and an interior zero of \(\Lambda\).

This suggests two theorem targets:

- prove \(\Lambda\) cannot vanish in the interior of \(\mathcal I\);
- or prove that whenever \(\Lambda\) vanishes in the interior, the induced local geometry upgrades to the affine-transport contradiction.

Either would kill the strict-excess case.

That is more flexible than demanding a global sign law for \(\Lambda\).

---

## 5. A sharper local theorem target

The cleanest next theorem candidate is:

### No interior inverse-curvature matching theorem
On the actual RH quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, for any separated outer runs \(J_0,J_2\) with common value interval \(\mathcal I\), the defect
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
has no interior zero.

In the generic simple-critical setting, that would immediately rule out strict excess, because strict excess forces such a zero.

This is a stronger and probably more attackable target than a global sign theorem for \(\Lambda\).

---

## 6. A second theorem target: coincidence upgrade

There is another possible route.

Suppose \(q_0\in\mathcal I\) satisfies
\[
\Lambda(q_0)=0,
\]
so the two inverse branches have the same second derivative there. If simultaneously \(q_0\) is the minimum point of \(\Delta\), then
\[
\Delta'(q_0)=0,\qquad \Delta''(q_0)=0.
\]
That means the two outer branches agree at that \(q_0\) in value, slope, and inverse curvature. In terms of the original quotient transport data, this is a same-value / same-slope / same-\(\Xi\) coincidence.

That is precisely the threshold where the previous affine-transport route begins to look natural. So a plausible theorem would be:

### Coincidence upgrade theorem
If a same-value / same-slope pair on separated outer runs also satisfies
\[
\Xi(\phi_2(q_0))=\Xi(\phi_0(q_0)),
\]
then the same-value transport is locally affine, hence impossible by three-run compression.

This would not require \(\Lambda\equiv 0\) globally — only a sufficiently strong local coincidence at one point.

I do not yet have a proof of that, but it is a much sharper local statement than before.

---

## 7. Strongest new structural findings

The strongest new findings from this round are these.

First, in the generic simple-critical case, the inverse-branch gap \(\Delta\) has square-root endpoint asymptotics, so both \(\Delta'\) and \(\Delta''\) have explicit singular behavior.

Second, the curvature defect
\[
\Lambda=-\Delta''
\]
necessarily has opposite endpoint signs. So any strict-excess case forces not just an interior minimum of \(\Delta\), but also an interior zero of \(\Lambda\).

Third, this means the strict-excess fixed-shear obstruction is now a coupled two-stage condition:

- interior same-value / same-slope event,
- plus interior inverse-curvature matching somewhere.

That is a much narrower geometry than “open same-value transport.”

---

## 8. Honest limitations

This is still not closure.

I have not proved:

- that the relevant critical endpoints are always simple;
- that interior zeroes of \(\Lambda\) are impossible;
- or that a single interior zero of \(\Lambda\) upgrades automatically to affine same-value transport.

So this round gives a sharper necessary condition, not a contradiction.

But it is a real improvement, because the remaining fixed-shear obstruction is now forced into a much tighter endpoint-controlled shape.

---

## 9. Current best summary of the live fixed-shear lane

The live strict three-run fixed-shear case has now been reduced to this:

Let \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) be the outer inverse branches and
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then strict excess is equivalent to an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). In the generic simple-critical setting, the endpoint asymptotics force
\[
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \text{ at one end},\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \text{ at the other},
\]
and simultaneously
\[
\Lambda(q):=-\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
has opposite endpoint signs, hence an interior zero.

So the remaining obstruction must realize both:

- a same-value / same-slope pair, and
- an interior inverse-curvature match between the separated outer runs.

That is the sharpest continuation I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:56:29)

_Thought for 3m 4s_

I found an important correction and a sharper reduction.

A more careful endpoint computation shows that, in the generic **simple-critical** setting for the two outer runs, the curvature defect
\[
\Lambda(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q),
\qquad
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
does **not** have opposite endpoint signs. It is actually negative near **both** ends of the overlap interval. That changes the geometry of the live fixed-shear obstruction in an important way. 

Here is the computation.

---

## 1. Correct endpoint asymptotics of the inverse-branch gap

Work in the quotient-visible fixed-shear three-run model on the common value interval
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)
\]
of the two outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\).

At the left endpoint \(q_-\), one branch, say \(\phi_0\), approaches a critical endpoint \(u_-\) of its run, while \(\phi_2\) stays noncritical. In the generic simple-critical case
\[
\widehat Q'(u_-)=0,\qquad \widehat Q''(u_-)\neq 0,
\]
so locally
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_-+a(u-u_-)^2+O((u-u_-)^3),\qquad a\neq 0.
\]
Inverting gives
\[
\phi_0(q)=u_-+c_-\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-),
\]
while the noncritical branch is analytic:
\[
\phi_2(q)=v_-+b_-(q-q_-)+O((q-q_-)^2).
\]
Therefore
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
=
(v_--u_-)-c_-\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-).
\]
Hence
\[
\Delta'(q)=-\frac{c_-}{2}(q-q_-)^{-1/2}+O(1),
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=\frac{c_-}{4}(q-q_-)^{-3/2}+O((q-q_-)^{-1/2})>0
\]
near \(q_-\).

At the right endpoint \(q_+\), the roles reverse: \(\phi_2\) becomes critical and \(\phi_0\) stays noncritical. The same computation yields
\[
\Delta'(q)=+\frac{c_+}{2}(q_+-q)^{-1/2}+O(1),
\]
and again
\[
\Delta''(q)=\frac{c_+}{4}(q_+-q)^{-3/2}+O((q_+-q)^{-1/2})>0
\]
near \(q_+\).

So the correct endpoint sign picture is:

\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \text{ at }q_-,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \text{ at }q_+,
\qquad
\Delta''(q)>0 \text{ near both endpoints.}
}
\]

Equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda(q)=-\Delta''(q)<0
\text{ near both endpoints.}
}
\]

This corrects the earlier idea that \(\Lambda\) might have opposite endpoint signs.

---

## 2. Immediate consequence: the full integrability route \(\Lambda\equiv 0\) is generically impossible in the strict-excess case

Earlier I had a conditional closure route:

- if \(\Lambda\equiv 0\), then \(\Delta''=0\), so \(\Delta\) is affine;
- strict excess also forces an interior critical point of \(\Delta\), so \(\Delta\) would be constant;
- then one gets affine same-value transport and forbidden run-pattern replication.

That logic is still correct formally.

But the new endpoint asymptotics show that in the generic simple-critical strict-excess setting,
\[
\Lambda(q)<0
\]
near both ends of \(\mathcal I\). So
\[
\Lambda\equiv 0
\]
cannot happen on such an overlap interval.

This is a real structural finding:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the generic simple-critical strict-excess case, value-space integrability of }\Xi\text{ across the two outer runs is impossible.}
}
\]

So the “integrable \(\Xi\)” branch of the fork is not the generic endgame after all. The live fixed-shear obstruction is necessarily the **non-integrable** one.

That is important. It says the remaining fixed-shear issue is not “prove \(\Xi\) descends.” The generic strict-excess case itself already forces failure of that descent.

---

## 3. What strict excess now really means

The strict-excess case still forces
\[
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \text{ at one end},\qquad \Delta'(q)\to +\infty \text{ at the other},
\]
so \(\Delta\) has at least one interior minimum.

But now we know more: because \(\Delta''>0\) near both endpoints, the inverse-branch gap is **convex near both ends**. So any surviving strict-excess case must look like a one-dimensional “valley” function:
- steeply decreasing and convex near the left end;
- steeply increasing and convex near the right end;
- with at least one interior minimum.

So the remaining live geometry is not arbitrary. It is a very specific boundary-value problem for \(\Delta\).

That is a sharper normal form than before.

---

## 4. If one can prove \(\Lambda\le 0\) everywhere, the strict-excess lane collapses to a unique minimum regime

Now that the endpoint signs are corrected, the natural global sign target is not \(\Lambda>0\), but rather
\[
\Lambda(q)\le 0
\qquad (q\in\mathcal I).
\]
Since
\[
\Lambda=-\Delta'',
\]
this means
\[
\Delta''(q)\ge 0,
\]
so \(\Delta\) is convex on all of \(\mathcal I\).

Combined with the endpoint derivative asymptotics, convexity gives:

- \(\Delta'\) is monotone increasing;
- \(\Delta'\) crosses zero exactly once;
- hence \(\Delta\) has a **unique** interior minimum.

So under the sign law \(\Lambda\le 0\), the strict-excess lane is compressed to a single distinguished same-value / same-slope pair downstairs.

This is not closure, but it is a major simplification.

So the new theorem target becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda(q)\le 0 \text{ on outer-run overlap intervals.}
}
\]

That is weaker than full integrability and much more compatible with the endpoint asymptotics.

---

## 5. The unique-minimum point carries the exact remaining jet obstruction

Let \(q_*\) be the unique minimum point of \(\Delta\) under \(\Lambda\le 0\). Then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
so the corresponding points
\[
u_0=\phi_0(q_*),\qquad u_2=\phi_2(q_*)
\]
satisfy
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_0)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u_2).
\]

And since
\[
\Lambda(q_*)=\Xi(u_2)-\Xi(u_0)=-\Delta''(q_*)\le 0,
\]
we also get the ordered curvature inequality
\[
\Xi(u_2)\le \Xi(u_0).
\]

At a same-value / same-slope pair, equality of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) means that comparing \(\Xi\) is the same as comparing \(\widehat Q''\):
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
and \(\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)\neq 0\). So
\[
\Xi(u_2)\le \Xi(u_0)
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\widehat Q''(u_2)\le \widehat Q''(u_0).
\]

Thus, if \(\Lambda\le 0\), every surviving strict-excess case is reduced to a single separated pair with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\qquad
\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2),\qquad
\widehat Q''(u_2)\le \widehat Q''(u_0).
\]

That is a very concrete 2-jet ordering statement.

So the first genuine remaining fixed-shear obstruction can now be formulated as:

> a separated same-value / same-slope pair with a one-sided second-derivative ordering.

That is much sharper than the original lobe-amplitude inequality.

---

## 6. This gives a new theorem schema

I think the clean next theorem target is:

### Outer-run curvature-sign theorem
On the actual RH quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, for any overlap interval between two separated outer runs,
\[
\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))\le 0
\qquad(q\in\mathcal I).
\]

Then the strict-excess case reduces to a unique minimum of \(\Delta\), hence to a unique separated pair with equal \((\widehat Q,\widehat Q')\) and ordered \(\widehat Q''\).

A slightly weaker but still useful target is:

### No multiple-minimum theorem
The inverse-branch gap \(\Delta\) has at most one interior critical point on \(\mathcal I\).

That would also compress the strict-excess geometry to a unique same-value / same-slope pair.

---

## 7. Why this still fits the consolidated paper update

This revised picture is still well aligned with the consolidated update.

The generator audit says the fixed-shear residue is no longer about a new pointwise field. The scalar
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
is exactly a **nonlocal transport comparison** of the same explicit pointwise observable \(\Xi\) across two separated points connected by equal quotient value. So it is precisely the kind of surviving object one should expect after the pointwise audit ceiling. 

And just as on the nonmixed/global side the remaining burden is a one-variable gap-function zero problem, here the remaining burden is now a one-variable **convexity/sign problem** for the inverse-branch gap \(\Delta\), encoded by the sign of \(\Lambda\).

So the two residual fronts remain parallel in structure.

---

## 8. Strongest new structural findings

The strongest new findings from this round are:

First, in the generic simple-critical strict-excess case,
\[
\Lambda(q)<0
\]
near both endpoints of the overlap interval. So the earlier integrability route \(\Lambda\equiv 0\) is generically unavailable.

Second, the strict-excess lane is now best viewed as a boundary-value problem for a function \(\Delta\) that is convex near both ends and has at least one interior minimum.

Third, if one can prove the natural sign law \(\Lambda\le 0\) globally, then the strict-excess lane collapses to a **unique** same-value / same-slope pair with a one-sided curvature ordering
\[
\widehat Q''(u_2)\le \widehat Q''(u_0).
\]

That is the sharpest compression of the live obstruction I can see right now.

---

## 9. Honest status

This is not closure.

I have not proved the sign law
\[
\Lambda\le 0
\]
on the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear branch. And without that, the possibility of more complicated interior behavior of \(\Delta\) remains.

But the endpoint asymptotic correction is important because it changes the right target. The fixed-shear lane should no longer be attacked mainly through the “\(\Lambda\equiv 0\)” integrable route. The generic live target is now the sign/variation law of \(\Lambda\), most naturally \(\Lambda\le 0\), which would compress the obstruction to a unique same-value / same-slope / curvature-ordered pair.

So the sharpest current continuation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Determine the sign law of }
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\text{ on outer-run overlap intervals.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the right next fixed-shear attack.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:02:17)

I’m pushing directly on the sign law for \(\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))\) now. The aim is to see whether the geometry forces \(\Lambda\le 0\) as a transport-convexity statement, or whether the first remaining obstruction is even narrower than that.

_Thought for 12s_

Here is the next push.

I think the right move now is to stop treating \(\Lambda\) as an arbitrary comparison and instead derive the **ODE that governs it along equal-value transport**. That gives a much sharper picture of what a sign law for \(\Lambda\) would have to mean.

Recall the setup on the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch:

\[
\widehat Q'(u)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u),
\]
and on an overlap interval \(\mathcal I\) between the two outer runs we have inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]

Define the inverse-branch gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_2(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)))^3}
+
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_0(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q)))^3}.
\]
So
\[
\Lambda(q):=
\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
=
-\Delta''(q),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]

The new observation is that \(\Lambda\) itself has a transport derivative formula.

Differentiate once more. Writing
\[
\Upsilon(u):=\frac{\Xi'(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)}
=
\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q}(u),
\]
one gets
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\Xi(\phi_j(q))
=
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_j(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_j(q))}
=
\Upsilon(\phi_j(q)).
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda'(q)=\Upsilon(\phi_2(q))-\Upsilon(\phi_0(q)).
}
\]

So the defect \(\Lambda\) is governed by the same-value difference of the next value-derivative observable \(\Upsilon=d\Xi/d\widehat Q\).

This is a real gain. It says the sign law for \(\Lambda\) is not isolated; it is part of a transport hierarchy:
\[
\widehat Q
\rightsquigarrow
\widehat Q'
\rightsquigarrow
\Xi=\frac{d}{d\widehat Q}\!\left(\frac{-1}{\widehat Q'}\right)
\rightsquigarrow
\Upsilon=\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q}.
\]

That lets us sharpen the fork again.

## 1. If \(\Upsilon\) is single-valued over \(\widehat Q\), then \(\Lambda\) is constant

If there exists a scalar function \(H\) such that
\[
\Upsilon(u)=H(\widehat Q(u))
\]
across the two outer runs, then
\[
\Lambda'(q)=0.
\]
So \(\Lambda\) is constant on \(\mathcal I\).

Now from the corrected endpoint asymptotics in the generic simple-critical setting, we already know
\[
\Lambda(q)<0
\]
near both endpoints of \(\mathcal I\). Therefore if \(\Lambda\) is constant, it must be strictly negative everywhere:
\[
\Lambda(q)\equiv -c,\qquad c>0.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\Delta''(q)\equiv c>0.
\]

So under this weaker “next-step value-space integrability” of \(\Upsilon\), the inverse-branch gap is not affine; it is a **quadratic-convex** function:
\[
\Delta(q)=\frac{c}{2}q^2+aq+b
\]
on \(\mathcal I\), after shifting coordinates appropriately.

Then the strict-excess geometry is completely rigid:

- \(\Delta\) is strictly convex;
- \(\Delta'\) has the required opposite infinite endpoint signs;
- so \(\Delta\) has a unique interior minimum;
- and the whole obstruction is encoded by one quadratic-convex gap law.

This is much sharper than the earlier \(\Lambda\equiv 0\) affine route.

So the next natural conditional theorem is:

> If \(\Upsilon=d\Xi/d\widehat Q\) is single-valued over \(\widehat Q\) across separated outer runs, then any strict-excess overlap forces \(\Delta\) to be a strictly convex constant-curvature value-space gap.

That is a very rigid normal form.

## 2. Why this may be enough to contradict three-run geometry

A strictly convex \(\Delta\) with
\[
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \text{ at }q_-,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \text{ at }q_+
\]
has exactly one minimum, as already noted.

But now there is more structure: the curvature is constant in value space. So the same-value transport map between the two outer runs has constant second derivative in \(q\).

Equivalently, the reciprocal-slope gap
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}
\]
is affine in \(q\).

That means the difference of reciprocal slopes changes linearly across the whole overlap interval. In particular, its zero is unique and crosses transversely.

This is exactly the opposite of the previous affine same-value transport regime. There, the same-value gap \(\Delta\) itself was affine; here, the reciprocal-slope gap \(\Delta'\) is affine.

So the strict-excess obstruction would now be reduced to a unique, transverse same-value / same-slope pair with constant value-space curvature of the gap.

That is probably narrow enough to compare directly to the local collision-chart quadratic law on the fixed-shear corner, which also singles out a unique second-order transport profile.

I do not yet have the contradiction, but this is a much more rigid model than the raw inequality \(B_1>B_0+B_2\).

## 3. A more realistic hierarchy of closure targets

This suggests that the earlier target \(\Lambda\equiv 0\) may have been too rigid. The natural hierarchy is now:

### Level 1
\[
\Lambda(q)\le 0
\]
on overlap intervals.

Then \(\Delta\) is convex and the strict-excess obstruction collapses to a unique minimum regime.

### Level 2
\[
\Lambda'(q)=0
\]
equivalently \(\Upsilon\) single-valued over \(\widehat Q\).

Then \(\Delta\) has constant curvature in value space, giving a still more rigid unique-minimum model.

### Level 3
\[
\Lambda(q)\equiv 0.
\]
Then \(\Delta\) is affine and the old periodicity/run-replication contradiction applies.

So there are now three nested transport-regularity routes, and the weakest one already gives a major simplification. That is useful because it lowers the burden of what one needs to prove.

## 4. The first genuine obstruction has been pushed one rung higher

This is, I think, the main new structural finding.

Before this round, the first genuine remaining fixed-shear obstruction was the sign/variation of
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
\]

Now the hierarchy shows that if \(\Lambda\) itself is not controllable by sign, the next obstruction is not vague either. It is the value-space mismatch of
\[
\Upsilon(u)=\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q}(u).
\]

So the unresolved fixed-shear obstruction has been pushed one rung higher in the transport hierarchy:

- either \(\Lambda\) has a global sign law and we are in the unique-minimum regime;
- or \(\Lambda\) fails to have the right sign, and the next exact obstruction is the same-value mismatch of \(\Upsilon\).

This is a real compression, because it replaces a vague “nonlocal transport issue” by an explicit recursively defined chain of value-space defects.

## 5. A canonical defect tower

There is now a natural tower of same-value transport defects.

Start with
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
\[
\Delta''(q)=-\Lambda(q),
\qquad
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)),
\]
and
\[
\Lambda'(q)=\Upsilon(\phi_2(q))-\Upsilon(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
\Upsilon=\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q}.
\]

So the strict three-run fixed-shear obstruction is now governed by a **value-space defect tower**:
\[
\Delta,\ \Delta',\ \Lambda,\ \Lambda',\dots
\]
coming from successive value-derivatives of the quotient transport profile.

This is exactly the sort of nonlocal transport structure the consolidated update suggests should be the real remaining issue after the pointwise generator audit. 

## 6. Strongest new theorem targets

The best next theorem targets I see now are:

### Target A: curvature-sign theorem
Show
\[
\Lambda(q)\le 0
\]
on every outer-run overlap interval.

This would collapse the strict-excess case to a unique minimum.

### Target B: next-rung value-space integrability
Show
\[
\Upsilon(u)=\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q}(u)
\]
is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across separated outer runs.

Then \(\Lambda\) is constant, hence strictly negative, and \(\Delta\) has constant positive curvature.

### Target C: no constant-curvature strict-excess theorem
Show the constant-curvature gap model for \(\Delta\) is incompatible with the actual three-run quotient-visible fixed-shear branch.

This would close the strict-excess case under Target B.

This seems more realistic now than trying to jump directly to affine transport.

## 7. Bottom line

The sharpest continuation from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The fixed-shear strict-excess lane is governed by a value-space defect tower, and the next exact defect above }
\Lambda
\text{ is }
\Upsilon=\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q}.
}
\]

So the current picture is:

- equality \(B_1=B_0+B_2\) is sterile;
- strict excess \(B_1>B_0+B_2\) forces an inverse-branch gap \(\Delta\) with an interior minimum;
- generically \(\Lambda=-\Delta''<0\) near both endpoints;
- if one proves \(\Lambda\le 0\), the obstruction collapses to a unique minimum regime;
- if one proves \(\Upsilon\) is single-valued over \(\widehat Q\), the obstruction collapses further to a constant-curvature gap model.

That is the sharpest next layer of the math attack I can see.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:05:15)

_Thought for 45s_

I think there is a real improvement now, and it sharpens the fixed-shear lane one full rung further.

The important correction from the last round is this: in the generic simple-critical setting, the defect
\[
\Lambda(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q),
\qquad
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
is not just negative near both endpoints of the overlap interval \(\mathcal I\). Its derivative has opposite endpoint signs. So a strict-excess overlap forces not only an interior minimum of \(\Delta\), but also an interior **maximum** of \(\Lambda\). That pushes the remaining obstruction one rung higher in the value-space defect tower. This fits the consolidated fixed-shear picture very well. 

Here is the calculation.

---

## 1. Endpoint asymptotics, corrected and sharpened

Assume the two outer runs \(J_0,J_2\) are in the generic simple-critical setting at the two endpoints \(q_-,q_+\) of their common value interval \(\mathcal I\).

From the previous round, near the left endpoint \(q_-\),
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
=
\Delta_- - c_-\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-),
\]
with \(c_->0\), so
\[
\Delta'(q)=-\frac{c_-}{2}(q-q_-)^{-1/2}+O(1),
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=\frac{c_-}{4}(q-q_-)^{-3/2}+O((q-q_-)^{-1/2})>0.
\]
Hence
\[
\Lambda(q)=-\Delta''(q)
=
-\frac{c_-}{4}(q-q_-)^{-3/2}+O((q-q_-)^{-1/2})<0.
\]
Differentiating once more,
\[
\Lambda'(q)=\frac{3c_-}{8}(q-q_-)^{-5/2}+O((q-q_-)^{-3/2})>0.
\]

Near the right endpoint \(q_+\), one similarly gets
\[
\Delta(q)=\Delta_+ - c_+\sqrt{q_+-q}+O(q_+-q),
\]
so
\[
\Delta'(q)=+\frac{c_+}{2}(q_+-q)^{-1/2}+O(1),
\]
\[
\Delta''(q)=\frac{c_+}{4}(q_+-q)^{-3/2}+O((q_+-q)^{-1/2})>0,
\]
and therefore
\[
\Lambda(q)=-\Delta''(q)
=
-\frac{c_+}{4}(q_+-q)^{-3/2}+O((q_+-q)^{-1/2})<0,
\]
while
\[
\Lambda'(q)=
-\frac{3c_+}{8}(q_+-q)^{-5/2}+O((q_+-q)^{-3/2})<0.
\]

So the correct endpoint behavior is

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda(q)<0 \text{ near both endpoints,}
\qquad
\Lambda'(q)>0 \text{ near }q_-,
\qquad
\Lambda'(q)<0 \text{ near }q_+.
}
\]

That is much stronger than the previous endpoint sign information.

---

## 2. Immediate consequence: strict excess forces an interior maximum of \(\Lambda\)

Because \(\Lambda'\) is positive near the left endpoint and negative near the right endpoint, any strict-excess overlap interval forces at least one interior point \(q^\star\in\mathcal I\) with
\[
\Lambda'(q^\star)=0.
\]

Since \(\Lambda<0\) near both ends, this critical point is naturally an interior **maximum** of \(\Lambda\).

So the strict-excess case now forces two interior events:

1. an interior minimum \(q_*\) of \(\Delta\), where
   \[
   \Delta'(q_*)=0
   \]
   and hence
   \[
   \widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*)),
   \qquad
   \widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*));
   \]

2. an interior maximum \(q^\star\) of \(\Lambda\), where
   \[
   \Lambda'(q^\star)=0.
   \]

That is a significantly tighter necessary condition than anything we had before.

---

## 3. The next defect rung appears explicitly

Define
\[
\Upsilon(u):=\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q}(u)
=
\frac{\Xi'(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
Then, as already observed,
\[
\Lambda'(q)=\Upsilon(\phi_2(q))-\Upsilon(\phi_0(q)).
\]

So the interior maximum point \(q^\star\) satisfies
\[
\Upsilon(\phi_2(q^\star))=\Upsilon(\phi_0(q^\star)).
\]

Therefore the strict-excess lane forces not only a same-value / same-slope pair, but also somewhere an equal-\(\Upsilon\) pair at equal quotient value. In other words, the next defect in the tower must vanish somewhere.

So the value-space defect tower now looks like:

\[
\Delta'(q)
=
\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
\[
\Delta''(q)=-\Lambda(q),
\qquad
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)),
\]
\[
\Lambda'(q)=\Upsilon(\phi_2(q))-\Upsilon(\phi_0(q)).
\]

This means the strict-excess case forces:

- a zero of \(\Delta'\),
- and a zero of \(\Lambda'\).

That is a major sharpening.

---

## 4. A new immediate corollary: if \(\Upsilon\) is single-valued over \(\widehat Q\), strict excess is impossible

Earlier I had noted that if \(\Upsilon\) is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across the two outer runs, then \(\Lambda\) is constant. At the time, that looked like it led to a “constant-curvature gap” model.

The corrected endpoint asymptotics show something stronger and simpler:

if \(\Lambda\) is constant on \(\mathcal I\), it cannot satisfy
\[
\Lambda(q)\to -\infty \text{ near both endpoints}
\]
and certainly cannot have
\[
\Lambda'(q)>0 \text{ near }q_-,
\qquad
\Lambda'(q)<0 \text{ near }q_+.
\]

So in the generic simple-critical strict-excess case,

\[
\boxed{
\text{If }\Upsilon(u)=H(\widehat Q(u))\text{ across the two outer runs, then strict excess is impossible.}
}
\]

This is stronger than the old route through affine transport and run-pattern replication. It kills the strict-excess case immediately.

So the “next-rung value-space integrability” theorem is now a very strong closure target.

---

## 5. The fixed-shear fork is now cleaner

The remaining fixed-shear strict-excess obstruction has been sharpened to:

- \(\Delta\) must have an interior minimum;
- \(\Lambda\) must have an interior maximum;
- therefore \(\Upsilon(\phi_2)-\Upsilon(\phi_0)\) must vanish somewhere.

So the fork is no longer merely about \(\Lambda\). It is now:

### Route A
Prove \(\Upsilon\) is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across separated outer runs.

Then \(\Lambda'\equiv 0\), hence \(\Lambda\) is constant, which is incompatible with the endpoint asymptotics. So strict excess dies immediately.

### Route B
If \(\Upsilon\) is not single-valued, then the first genuine remaining fixed-shear obstruction is even more precise than before:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a separated equal-\(\widehat Q\) pair with a nontrivial mismatch of }
\Upsilon=\frac{d}{d\widehat Q}\!\left(\frac{\widehat Q''}{(\widehat Q')^3}\right).
}
\]

That is the next exact nonlocal transport obstruction.

This is tighter than the previous “\(\Lambda\)-sign law” framing.

---

## 6. A very sharp necessary condition for any surviving strict-excess case

Putting this together, any surviving strict-excess overlap in the generic simple-critical setting must realize all of the following on the same common value interval \(\mathcal I\):

- \(\Delta'(q)\) has opposite infinite endpoint signs, hence a zero;
- \(\Lambda(q)=-\Delta''(q)\) is negative near both endpoints;
- \(\Lambda'(q)\) has opposite endpoint signs, hence a zero.

So the obstruction must support both a zero of \(\Delta'\) and a zero of \(\Lambda'\). That is already a very narrow one-dimensional shape theorem.

Equivalently, the live strict-excess lane now requires:

> an equal-\((\widehat Q,\widehat Q')\) pair and an equal-\(\Upsilon\) pair across the separated outer runs.

This is a much stronger structural burden than the original amplitude inequality \(B_1>B_0+B_2\).

---

## 7. Best new theorem targets

The strongest next theorem targets I see are now:

### Target A: next-rung value-space integrability
Show that on separated outer runs of the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear branch,
\[
\Upsilon(u)=\frac{d}{d\widehat Q}\!\left(\frac{\widehat Q''}{(\widehat Q')^3}\right)
\]
is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\).

Then strict excess is impossible immediately by the endpoint asymptotics above.

### Target B: no interior maximum theorem for \(\Lambda\)
Show that the defect
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
cannot have an interior maximum on an overlap interval.

This would also kill strict excess, since strict excess forces such a maximum.

### Target C: no equal-\(\Upsilon\) pair theorem
Show that on separated outer runs there cannot exist \(u_0,u_2\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),
\qquad
\Upsilon(u_0)=\Upsilon(u_2).
\]
That would rule out the required zero of \(\Lambda'\).

Any of these would now be enough.

---

## 8. Strongest new structural findings

The strongest new findings from this round are:

First, the endpoint asymptotics force not only \(\Lambda<0\) near both ends, but also
\[
\Lambda'>0 \text{ near the left end,}
\qquad
\Lambda'<0 \text{ near the right end.}
\]

Second, therefore any strict-excess overlap forces an interior maximum of \(\Lambda\), not just a minimum of \(\Delta\).

Third, this pushes the remaining fixed-shear obstruction one rung higher: the decisive next observable is
\[
\Upsilon=\frac{d\Xi}{d\widehat Q},
\]
not just \(\Xi\) itself.

Fourth, if \(\Upsilon\) descends to value space across the two outer runs, the strict-excess case is killed immediately.

So the fixed-shear lane has now been reduced to an even sharper transport-defect fork than before.

---

## 9. Bottom line

The sharpest continuation from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the generic simple-critical fixed-shear strict-excess case, }
\Lambda
\text{ must have an interior maximum, hence }
\Upsilon(\phi_2)-\Upsilon(\phi_0)
\text{ must vanish somewhere.}
}
\]

So the next exact fixed-shear transport obstruction is no longer just the curvature mismatch
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)),
\]
but the next-rung value-space mismatch
\[
\Upsilon(\phi_2(q))-\Upsilon(\phi_0(q)).
\]

And if \(\Upsilon\) is single-valued over the quotient value coordinate, the strict three-run obstruction dies immediately.

That is the sharpest math attack I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:08:02)

_Thought for 1m 14s_

I think there is a genuinely better reduction now.

The fixed-shear strict-excess lane is not just controlled by one defect
\[
\Lambda(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
There is actually a whole **value-space defect tower**, and in the generic simple-critical setting its endpoint asymptotics are completely rigid. That gives a sharper necessary condition than anything I had before, and it fits the consolidated picture very well: the remaining issue is nonlocal quotient transport, not a new local field. 

Here is the structure.

---

## 1. Define the value-space transport tower

Let \(\phi\) be an inverse branch of \(\widehat Q\) on a monotone run, so
\[
\widehat Q(\phi(q))=q.
\]
Then
\[
\phi'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi(q))},
\qquad
\phi''(q)=-\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi(q)))^3}.
\]

Set
\[
\Xi_0(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
and recursively define
\[
\Xi_{m+1}(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}\frac{d}{du}\Xi_m(u)
=
\frac{d\Xi_m}{d\widehat Q}(u).
\]

Then by repeated differentiation of \(\phi''=-\Xi_0(\phi)\), one gets inductively
\[
\boxed{
\phi^{(m+2)}(q)=-\Xi_m(\phi(q))
\qquad (m\ge 0).
}
\]

Now on the common value interval \(\mathcal I\) of the two outer runs \(J_0,J_2\), let \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) be the inverse branches and define the gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then for each \(m\ge 0\),
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda_m(q):=\Xi_m(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_m(\phi_0(q))
=
-\Delta^{(m+2)}(q).
}
\]

So the fixed-shear strict-excess problem comes with an entire hierarchy of nonlocal transport defects
\[
\Lambda_0,\Lambda_1,\Lambda_2,\dots
\]
rather than just the first one \(\Lambda_0=\Lambda\).

That is the first new structural gain.

---

## 2. Endpoint asymptotics in the generic simple-critical case

Assume the two outer runs meet the common value interval \(\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)\) through simple critical endpoints. Then, as before, one inverse branch has square-root singularity at each end while the other remains analytic. This gives the local model
\[
\Delta(q)=A_- - c_-\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-)
\quad\text{near }q_-,
\]
and
\[
\Delta(q)=A_+ - c_+\sqrt{q_+-q}+O(q_+-q)
\quad\text{near }q_+,
\]
with \(c_\pm>0\).

Therefore, for every \(n\ge 1\),
\[
\Delta^{(n)}(q)\sim
(-1)^n\,\alpha_n\,(q-q_-)^{1/2-n}
\quad\text{near }q_-,
\]
and
\[
\Delta^{(n)}(q)\sim
+\alpha_n\,(q_+-q)^{1/2-n}
\quad\text{near }q_+,
\]
for explicit positive constants \(\alpha_n\).

Passing to
\[
\Lambda_m=-\Delta^{(m+2)},
\]
this yields the sign pattern

\[
\boxed{
\text{near }q_-:\quad
\operatorname{sgn}\Lambda_m = (-1)^{m+1},
\qquad
\text{near }q_+:\quad
\operatorname{sgn}\Lambda_m = -1.
}
\]

So:

- for even \(m\), \(\Lambda_m<0\) near **both** endpoints;
- for odd \(m\), \(\Lambda_m>0\) near the left endpoint and \(\Lambda_m<0\) near the right endpoint.

This is much sharper than the earlier one-rung picture.

---

## 3. Immediate consequence: every odd-rung defect must vanish somewhere

Since for odd \(m\) the defect \(\Lambda_m\) has opposite endpoint signs, the intermediate value theorem gives:

\[
\boxed{
\text{For every odd }m\ge 1,\ \Lambda_m(q)\text{ has at least one interior zero on }\mathcal I.
}
\]

The case \(m=1\) is exactly the previous observation that
\[
\Lambda_1(q)=\frac{d\Lambda_0}{dq}
\]
must vanish somewhere because \(\Lambda_0\) has an interior maximum.

But now this is part of a whole tower:

- strict excess forces an interior zero of \(\Delta'=-\Lambda_{-1}\) if you like,
- then an interior zero of \(\Lambda_1\),
- then an interior zero of \(\Lambda_3\),
- and so on.

This is a substantial strengthening of the necessary conditions.

---

## 4. A major new closure principle

Now we get a very clean theorem schema.

If for **any odd rung** \(m=2k+1\), the observable \(\Xi_m\) is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across the two outer runs, then
\[
\Lambda_m(q)\equiv 0.
\]
But the endpoint asymptotics above say that for odd \(m\),
\[
\Lambda_m>0 \text{ near }q_-,
\qquad
\Lambda_m<0 \text{ near }q_+.
\]
So \(\Lambda_m\equiv 0\) is impossible.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If any odd-rung value-space observable }\Xi_{2k+1}
\text{ descends to a single-valued function of }\widehat Q
\text{ across the two outer runs, then strict excess is impossible.}
}
\]

This is a stronger and cleaner closure route than the earlier \(\Upsilon\)-integrability observation, because it makes the pattern completely systematic.

The \(k=0\) case says:

- if
  \[
  \Xi_1(u)=\frac{d}{d\widehat Q}\left(\frac{\widehat Q''}{(\widehat Q')^3}\right)
  \]
  is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\),
- then strict three-lobe excess is impossible immediately.

That was already visible in the previous round, but now it sits inside a full hierarchy.

---

## 5. What survives if no odd-rung descent holds

This also sharpens the negative result.

If the strict-excess fixed-shear obstruction survives, then **none** of the odd-rung observables
\[
\Xi_1,\Xi_3,\Xi_5,\dots
\]
can descend to value space across the two outer runs.

So the first genuine remaining fixed-shear obstruction is not merely “\(\Xi\) is not single-valued over \(\widehat Q\).” It is stronger:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Every odd-rung value-space transport observable fails to descend across the separated outer runs.}
}
\]

That is an extremely rigid nonlocal obstruction.

It says the fixed-shear residue, if real, must survive through an infinite hierarchy of value-space mismatches, not just one low-order curvature mismatch.

That is the strongest negative finding of this round.

---

## 6. How this reframes the live fixed-shear endgame

The strict three-run fixed-shear case is now best understood as a one-dimensional boundary-value problem for \(\Delta\) whose derivative tower is constrained by the square-root endpoint asymptotics.

The tower says:

- \(\Delta'\) has opposite endpoint signs, so \(\Delta\) has an interior minimum;
- \(\Lambda_0=-\Delta''\) is negative near both ends;
- \(\Lambda_1=-\Delta'''\) has opposite endpoint signs, so \(\Lambda_0\) has an interior maximum;
- \(\Lambda_2=-\Delta^{(4)}\) is negative near both ends;
- \(\Lambda_3=-\Delta^{(5)}\) has opposite endpoint signs;
- and so on.

So any surviving strict-excess case must realize this entire alternating endpoint-sign structure in the value-space defect tower.

That is far more rigid than the original amplitude inequality
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
\]

---

## 7. Best theorem targets now

This suggests two especially sharp targets.

### Target A: odd-rung descent
Prove that for some odd \(m\),
\[
\Xi_m(u)=F_m(\widehat Q(u))
\]
across separated outer runs.

Then strict excess is impossible at once.

The first meaningful candidate is \(m=1\):
\[
\Xi_1=\frac{d}{d\widehat Q}\left(\frac{\widehat Q''}{(\widehat Q')^3}\right).
\]

### Target B: endpoint-sign incompatibility
Without proving full descent, prove that some odd-rung defect \(\Lambda_{2k+1}\) cannot have the forced opposite endpoint signs on an overlap interval.

That would also kill strict excess.

Target A is especially attractive because it aligns perfectly with the consolidated paper’s message: the remaining problem is transport/global/package-level, not one more pointwise field. A descent theorem for an odd-rung value-space observable is exactly the kind of nonlocal transport statement that could still be true after the generator audit. 

---

## 8. Honest status

I do **not** yet know how to prove descent for \(\Xi_1\) or any higher odd rung.

So this is not closure.

But the fixed-shear strict-excess lane has now been reduced to something much sharper:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If strict excess survives, then every odd-rung value-space transport observable }
\Xi_{2k+1}
\text{ must fail to descend across the two outer runs.}
}
\]

That is a very strong necessary condition.

It is the cleanest version yet of “the remaining obstruction is genuinely nonlocal transport.”

---

## 9. Bottom line

The best current summary is:

The fixed-shear strict-excess case is governed by the inverse-branch gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and its value-space defect tower
\[
\Lambda_m(q)=\Xi_m(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_m(\phi_0(q))
=
-\Delta^{(m+2)}(q),
\qquad
\Xi_{m+1}=\frac{d\Xi_m}{d\widehat Q}.
\]
In the generic simple-critical setting, odd-rung defects \(\Lambda_{2k+1}\) have opposite endpoint signs and therefore must vanish in the interior. Hence if any odd-rung observable \(\Xi_{2k+1}\) is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across the two outer runs, strict excess is impossible.

So the live fixed-shear obstruction has now been pushed into an **infinite odd-rung non-descent tower**. That is the sharpest continuation of the math attack I can see.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:10:32)

_Thought for 53s_

I think there is a genuinely sharper theorem now.

The whole odd-rung discussion can be upgraded to a single statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the generic simple-critical strict-excess fixed-shear case, descent of \emph{any} value-space tower observable } \Xi_m
\text{ across the two outer runs is impossible.}
}
\]

That is stronger than what I said before about odd rungs only.

---

## 1. The value-space tower

On a monotone quotient-visible branch, let \(\phi\) be an inverse branch of \(\widehat Q\), so
\[
\widehat Q(\phi(q))=q.
\]
Define
\[
\Xi_0(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\qquad
\Xi_{m+1}(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}\frac{d}{du}\Xi_m(u)
=\frac{d\Xi_m}{d\widehat Q}(u).
\]

For the two outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\), define the gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and the tower of same-value defects
\[
\Lambda_m(q):=\Xi_m(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_m(\phi_0(q)).
\]

As before, repeated differentiation gives the exact identity
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda_m(q)=-\Delta^{(m+2)}(q)
\qquad(m\ge 0).
}
\]

This is the right transport tower downstairs.

---

## 2. New key observation: descent of \(\Xi_m\) forces \(\Delta\) to be a polynomial

Suppose for some \(m\ge 0\) there exists a scalar function \(F_m\) such that across the two outer runs
\[
\Xi_m(u)=F_m(\widehat Q(u)).
\]
Then on the common value interval \(\mathcal I\),
\[
\Xi_m(\phi_2(q))=\Xi_m(\phi_0(q)),
\]
so
\[
\Lambda_m(q)\equiv 0.
\]
Using the identity above,
\[
\Delta^{(m+2)}(q)\equiv 0.
\]
Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\Delta(q)\text{ is a polynomial in }q\text{ of degree at most }m+1.
}
\]

This is already much stronger than the earlier “affine” or “constant-curvature” special cases.

- \(m=0\): \(\Delta''=0\), so \(\Delta\) is affine;
- \(m=1\): \(\Delta'''=0\), so \(\Delta\) is quadratic;
- in general: descent at rung \(m\) forces polynomial gap of degree \(\le m+1\).

So any rung descent would rigidify the same-value transport enormously.

---

## 3. Why polynomial \(\Delta\) is impossible in the strict-excess case

In the generic simple-critical strict-excess setting, the overlap interval is
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+),
\]
and the outer inverse branches have square-root endpoint behavior. Consequently,
\[
\Delta(q)=A_- - c_-\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-)
\quad\text{near }q_-,
\]
and
\[
\Delta(q)=A_+ - c_+\sqrt{q_+-q}+O(q_+-q)
\quad\text{near }q_+,
\]
with \(c_\pm>0\).

Hence
\[
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \text{ as }q\downarrow q_-,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \text{ as }q\uparrow q_+.
\]

But a polynomial on a bounded interval cannot have this square-root singular derivative behavior at finite endpoints.

Therefore \(\Delta\) cannot be a polynomial on \(\mathcal I\).

So we get the clean theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the generic simple-critical strict-excess case, no tower observable }\Xi_m
\text{ can descend to a single-valued function of }\widehat Q
\text{ across the two outer runs.}
}
\]

That is a real sharpening.

---

## 4. This subsumes all previous “integrability” routes

This one statement subsumes all the previous special reductions:

- If \(\Xi_0\) descends, then \(\Delta\) is affine. Impossible.
- If \(\Xi_1\) descends, then \(\Delta\) is quadratic. Impossible.
- If \(\Xi_2\) descends, then \(\Delta\) is cubic. Impossible.
- And so on.

So the earlier odd-rung hierarchy was too weak. The correct statement is:

> **Any** rung descent kills strict excess, because any rung descent polynomializes the inverse-branch gap.

That is much cleaner.

---

## 5. What this means for the remaining obstruction

This has an important consequence.

The surviving fixed-shear obstruction is not merely “\(\Xi_0\) fails to descend” or “\(\Xi_1\) fails to descend.” It is much stronger:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If strict excess survives, then the entire tower }
\Xi_0,\Xi_1,\Xi_2,\dots
\text{ is non-single-valued over }q=\widehat Q(u)
\text{ across the separated outer runs.}
}
\]

So the fixed-shear residual obstruction, if real, is not a low-order failure. It is an **all-rungs non-descent phenomenon**.

That is one of the strongest negative findings of the whole attack.

It also lines up perfectly with the consolidated update: the remaining issue is genuinely nonlocal transport, not one more pointwise generator or low-order scalar. 

---

## 6. A better way to state the live fixed-shear fork

The fixed-shear strict-excess fork is now:

### Route A
Prove descent of some tower observable \(\Xi_m\) to value space across separated outer runs.

Then strict excess is impossible immediately.

### Route B
No such descent holds for any \(m\ge 0\).

Then the fixed-shear obstruction is an infinite tower of non-descent defects, not just one isolated mismatch.

This is a much sharper formulation than before.

It says the mixed fixed-shear endgame is not “find the first obstruction.” The first obstruction is already forced at every rung unless the lane dies.

---

## 7. New structural finding: the live fixed-shear obstruction is necessarily infinite-order in value-space transport

This is the strongest structural conclusion from this round:

\[
\boxed{
\text{A surviving strict-excess fixed-shear obstruction must be infinite-order in the value-space transport tower.}
}
\]

Because any finite-rung descent would polynomialize \(\Delta\), contradicting the endpoint singular behavior.

That is a very strong statement. It says the live obstruction cannot be captured by any finite truncation of the value-space transport tower.

So the residual fixed-shear problem has been pushed beyond all finite transport-rung descent statements.

That is probably the clearest explanation yet of why the generator audit found no new finite pointwise closure and why the residual obstruction, if it survives, must be genuinely transport/global.

---

## 8. What remains mathematically live

This suggests two refined attack directions.

### Direction 1: prove finite-rung descent somewhere
Any theorem showing that one explicit \(\Xi_m\) descends to value space across separated outer runs would now be decisive.

This is probably the cleanest possible closure route.

### Direction 2: characterize the infinite non-descent tower
If no descent theorem is available, then the real remaining question is:

> can the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear branch support an all-rungs non-descent tower across separated outer runs?

That is now the exact form of the obstruction.

This is more precise than anything we had before.

---

## 9. Best current theorem target

The cleanest next theorem target is now:

### Finite-rung descent theorem
Show that for some finite \(m\ge 0\), the observable
\[
\Xi_m(u)
\]
is single-valued over \(q=\widehat Q(u)\) across the two outer runs of any strict-excess overlap.

Then strict excess is impossible.

A weaker but still useful version is:

### No all-rungs non-descent theorem
Show that the actual RH quotient-visible fixed-shear branch cannot support non-descent of \(\Xi_m\) across separated outer runs for every \(m\ge 0\).

That would also kill the strict-excess lane.

---

## 10. Bottom line

The sharpest continuation from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Strict three-run fixed-shear excess has now been reduced to an infinite non-descent tower problem.}
}
\]

More precisely:

- the inverse-branch gap \(\Delta=\phi_2-\phi_0\) has square-root endpoint behavior;
- if any tower observable \(\Xi_m\) descends to value space across the two outer runs, then \(\Delta\) becomes a polynomial, impossible;
- therefore any surviving strict-excess case must force non-descent of \(\Xi_m\) for every \(m\ge 0\).

That is the strongest and cleanest reduction I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:11:35)

I’m pushing on the new “infinite non-descent tower” fork now. The natural next step is to see whether all-rungs non-descent is even compatible with the finite explicit pointwise package the draft already tracks, or whether that infinite tower can be compressed back to finitely many transported fields.

_Thought for 1m 11s_

I found a cleaner reduction that simplifies the whole “infinite non-descent tower” story.

The key point is: the tower is not really a family of unrelated obstructions. It is the ordinary derivative tower of **one single value-space defect function**. So the strict-excess fixed-shear lane is governed by one scalar function, not by infinitely many independent defects.

That is a real structural gain.

---

## 1. Collapse the tower to one function

On the common value interval \(\mathcal I\) of the two outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\), define
\[
F_j(q):=\Xi_0(\phi_j(q)),
\qquad
\Xi_0(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
Then by definition
\[
\Lambda_0(q):=\Xi_0(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_0(\phi_0(q))
=
F_2(q)-F_0(q).
\]

Now recall the value-space transport tower
\[
\Xi_{m+1}(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}\frac{d}{du}\Xi_m(u)
=
\frac{d\Xi_m}{d\widehat Q}(u).
\]
Since \(\widehat Q(\phi_j(q))=q\), differentiation in \(q\) along an inverse branch is exactly differentiation with respect to \(\widehat Q\). So inductively,
\[
\frac{d^m}{dq^m}F_j(q)=\Xi_m(\phi_j(q)).
\]
Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda_m(q):=\Xi_m(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_m(\phi_0(q))
=
\frac{d^m}{dq^m}\Lambda_0(q).
}
\]

So the whole defect tower is just the ordinary derivative tower of the one scalar defect
\[
D(q):=\Lambda_0(q)=\Xi_0(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_0(\phi_0(q)).
\]

This is a big simplification.

---

## 2. Consequence: “all-rungs non-descent” is not a separate infinite obstruction

Previously I said that if strict excess survives, then every rung \(\Xi_m\) must fail to descend to value space.

That is still true, but now we can say something sharper.

If for some finite \(m\),
\[
\Xi_m(\phi_2(q))=\Xi_m(\phi_0(q))
\qquad\text{for all }q\in\mathcal I,
\]
then
\[
\Lambda_m(q)\equiv 0.
\]
By the identity above,
\[
D^{(m)}(q)\equiv 0.
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
D(q)\text{ is a polynomial in }q\text{ of degree at most }m-1.
}
\]

But in the generic simple-critical strict-excess setting, the endpoint asymptotics of \(D=\Lambda_0\) are singular:
\[
D(q)=\Lambda_0(q)\to -\infty
\]
at both ends of \(\mathcal I\), with explicit \((q-q_\pm)^{-3/2}\)-type blow-up. So \(D\) cannot be a polynomial.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the generic simple-critical strict-excess case, no finite-rung descent can occur.}
}
\]

That is now an immediate corollary of one scalar endpoint singularity, not an independent infinite hierarchy of obstructions.

So the “infinite non-descent tower” is better understood as:

> one non-polynomial defect \(D(q)\) whose every derivative is forced to stay nonzero somewhere.

That is cleaner and more useful.

---

## 3. The live fixed-shear obstruction is now exactly the shape of one function \(D(q)\)

So the strict-excess fixed-shear lane has been reduced to the analysis of one scalar function
\[
D(q):=\Xi_0(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_0(\phi_0(q)).
\]

All the earlier data are now encoded by derivatives of \(D\):

- \(D=\Lambda_0=-\Delta''\),
- \(D'=\Lambda_1\),
- \(D''=\Lambda_2\),
- and in general \(D^{(m)}=\Lambda_m\).

Also, the inverse-branch gap satisfies
\[
\Delta''(q)=-D(q).
\]

So the strict-excess case is not “a mysterious high transport tower.” It is a one-dimensional second-order boundary-value problem driven by the forcing term \(D(q)\).

That is the strongest simplification of the lane so far.

---

## 4. Endpoint geometry of \(D\)

In the generic simple-critical setting, we already computed:

\[
D(q)=\Lambda_0(q)<0
\quad\text{near both endpoints of }\mathcal I.
\]
And from the refined endpoint asymptotics,
\[
D'(q)=\Lambda_1(q)>0
\quad\text{near the left endpoint,}
\qquad
D'(q)=\Lambda_1(q)<0
\quad\text{near the right endpoint.}
\]

So \(D\) is negative and rising near the left end, negative and falling near the right end. Therefore \(D\) has at least one interior maximum.

This means the live strict-excess lane has the following shape data:

\[
\boxed{
D<0 \text{ near both ends of }\mathcal I,
\qquad
D'>0 \text{ near the left end},
\qquad
D'<0 \text{ near the right end},
}
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
D \text{ has an interior maximum.}
}
\]

Since
\[
\Delta''=-D,
\]
that is exactly the earlier statement that \(\Delta\) is convex near both ends and has an interior minimum. But phrased in terms of \(D\), it is cleaner: the obstruction is a negative singular forcing term with an interior peak.

---

## 5. A better way to frame the remaining theorem targets

This shifts the emphasis.

The main question is no longer “prove some \(\Xi_m\) descends.” That is sufficient but not necessary for understanding the lane.

The real question is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{What sign / convexity / oscillation laws can the value-space defect }D(q)
\text{ satisfy on the overlap interval?}
}
\]

Because once \(D\) is controlled, everything else follows by differentiation/integration.

So the best next theorem targets now look like this.

### Target A: sign law for \(D\)
Show
\[
D(q)\le 0
\qquad (q\in\mathcal I).
\]
Then
\[
\Delta''(q)\ge 0,
\]
so \(\Delta\) is convex and has a unique interior minimum. This collapses the strict-excess lane to a unique same-value / same-slope pair.

### Target B: unimodality of \(D\)
Show \(D\) has at most one critical point on \(\mathcal I\). Since endpoint asymptotics already force one interior maximum, this would imply a unique maximum, and again a very rigid shape theorem.

### Target C: finite-order ODE for \(D\)
Use the fact that the explicit pointwise package is finitely generated by the known tower to derive a finite-order differential equation in \(q\) satisfied by \(F_0\) and \(F_2\), hence by
\[
D=F_2-F_0.
\]
Then use the endpoint singular asymptotics of \(D\) to rule out the required interior shape.

This is, I think, now the most promising bridge to the generator audit.

---

## 6. Why this aligns well with the generator audit

The consolidated update said that no second primitive pointwise highest-new generator has appeared beyond the existing explicit package. That suggested the remaining fixed-shear issue should be nonlocal transport, not one more pointwise field. 

The reduction to the scalar defect \(D(q)\) fits that perfectly:

- \(D\) is not a new pointwise field;
- it is a nonlocal comparison of the same explicit pointwise observable \(\Xi_0\) across two separated points with equal quotient value;
- and every higher defect is just a derivative of \(D\).

So the residual mixed fixed-shear lane has now become exactly the kind of nonlocal transport problem the audit predicts.

That is a strong conceptual consistency check.

---

## 7. A useful conditional theorem

There is one clean conditional theorem worth recording.

### Conditional theorem
Assume the explicit quotient-visible transport package is finite-dimensional enough that the value-space profile
\[
F(q):=\Xi_0(\phi(q))
\]
along any monotone inverse branch satisfies a common finite-order scalar ODE in \(q\), with coefficients depending only on \(q\) (or only on \(\widehat Q=q\)).

Then the difference
\[
D(q)=F_2(q)-F_0(q)
\]
satisfies the same linear homogeneous ODE. Combined with the explicit endpoint singular asymptotics of \(D\), this gives a sharply constrained boundary-value problem for \(D\). Any theorem ruling out a negative function with those singular endpoints and the required interior maximum would kill strict excess.

I do not yet have the ODE, but this is now the right kind of theorem to look for if one wants to connect the finite generator audit to the fixed-shear transport lane.

---

## 8. Strongest new structural findings

The strongest new structural findings from this round are:

First, the entire fixed-shear defect tower collapses to the derivative tower of one scalar value-space defect
\[
D(q)=\Xi_0(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_0(\phi_0(q)).
\]

Second, finite-rung descent is equivalent to \(D\) being a polynomial, which is impossible in the generic simple-critical strict-excess setting because \(D\) has singular negative endpoint blow-up.

Third, the live strict-excess lane is therefore governed by the shape of a single non-polynomial forcing term \(D\) in the second-order relation
\[
\Delta''=-D.
\]

Fourth, the remaining obstruction is now best thought of as a one-dimensional boundary-value/oscillation problem for \(D\), not an infinite collection of separate transport mismatches.

That is a major clarification.

---

## 9. Bottom line

The sharpest continuation from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The strict three-run fixed-shear obstruction has now been reduced to the analysis of one scalar value-space defect }
D(q)=\Xi_0(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_0(\phi_0(q)).
}
\]

Everything else is derivative bookkeeping:
\[
D^{(m)}(q)=\Lambda_m(q).
\]
Any finite-rung descent would polynomialize \(D\), impossible. So the live issue is no longer “some transport tower obstruction.” It is the boundary-value shape of a single non-polynomial defect \(D\) with explicit endpoint singularities and an interior maximum.

That is the cleanest and strongest reduction I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:15:09)

_Thought for 53s_

I think there is a real improvement now: the remaining fixed-shear obstruction can be split into a **universal singular factor** and a **regular transport defect**. That makes the live problem much cleaner and connects it better to the finite explicit package in the paper. 

The starting point is the scalar value-space defect
\[
D(q):=\Xi_0(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_0(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
\Xi_0(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
on the common value interval \(\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)\) of the two outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\). We already have
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
\Delta''(q)=-D(q),
\]
and in the generic simple-critical setting,
\[
D(q)<0 \text{ near both endpoints,}
\qquad
D'(q)>0 \text{ near }q_-,
\qquad
D'(q)<0 \text{ near }q_+.
\]

What matters now is that these endpoint singularities are completely universal.

If \(q_-\) and \(q_+\) are simple-critical endpoints of the outer runs, then
\[
\phi_0(q)=u_-+c_-\sqrt{q-q_-}+O(q-q_-)
\quad\text{near }q_-,
\]
\[
\phi_2(q)=u_+-c_+\sqrt{q_+-q}+O(q_+-q)
\quad\text{near }q_+),
\]
and one gets
\[
D(q)\sim -\,A_-(q-q_-)^{-3/2}
\quad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-,
\]
\[
D(q)\sim -\,A_+(q_+-q)^{-3/2}
\quad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+,
\]
for positive constants \(A_\pm\).

So the singular behavior of \(D\) is not mysterious transport complexity. It is just the universal inverse-square-root endpoint geometry differentiated twice.

That means we should factor it out.

Define the **regularized fixed-shear defect**
\[
\boxed{
E(q):=(q-q_-)^{3/2}(q_+-q)^{3/2}\,D(q).
}
\]

Then in the generic simple-critical setting, \(E\) extends continuously to the closed interval \([q_-,q_+]\), and in fact
\[
E(q_-)<0,\qquad E(q_+)<0.
\]

This is a real gain. The live fixed-shear obstruction is no longer a singular function \(D\). It is a regular interior function \(E\), with the singular endpoint behavior already stripped off.

That gives a much sharper formulation of what remains:

- the singularity of \(D\) at the endpoints is universal and harmless;
- all genuinely nontrivial transport information is carried by the regularized defect \(E\).

So the strict-excess lane is now a one-dimensional boundary-value problem for a regular function \(E\), not for a singular one.

There is also a clean tower version. Earlier I defined
\[
\Xi_{m+1}=\frac{d\Xi_m}{d\widehat Q},
\qquad
\Lambda_m(q)=\Xi_m(\phi_2(q))-\Xi_m(\phi_0(q)),
\]
with
\[
\Lambda_m(q)=-\Delta^{(m+2)}(q).
\]
In the same generic simple-critical setting,
\[
\Lambda_m(q)\sim C_{m,-}(q-q_-)^{-m-3/2}
\quad\text{near }q_-,
\]
\[
\Lambda_m(q)\sim C_{m,+}(q_+-q)^{-m-3/2}
\quad\text{near }q_+,
\]
with explicit alternating sign pattern. So the regularized tower
\[
\boxed{
E_m(q):=(q-q_-)^{m+3/2}(q_+-q)^{m+3/2}\Lambda_m(q)
}
\]
extends continuously to \([q_-,q_+]\).

This means the whole “infinite non-descent tower” can be renormalized into a sequence of regular defect functions \(E_m\). The singular endpoint blow-up is no longer the hard part; the hard part is the interior sign and oscillation of the regularized defects.

That is a much better place to be.

It also gives a useful correction to my previous framing. Earlier I emphasized that any finite-rung descent would polynomialize the inverse-branch gap \(\Delta\), which is incompatible with its square-root endpoint behavior. That is still correct. But now we can say it more cleanly:

any finite-rung descent would force one of the regularized defects \(E_m\) to vanish identically, which is impossible because its endpoint values are already fixed and nonzero in the generic simple-critical case.

So the fixed-shear strict-excess obstruction, if it survives, is not just an infinite non-descent tower. It is an infinite non-descent tower **after universal singularity extraction**.

That is the strongest conceptual clarification of this round.

There is also a very useful integral reformulation. Since
\[
\Delta''(q)=-D(q),
\]
once a basepoint \(q_0\in\mathcal I\) is chosen,
\[
\Delta(q)=\Delta(q_0)+\Delta'(q_0)(q-q_0)-\int_{q_0}^q (q-s)D(s)\,ds.
\]
Substituting \(D(s)=E(s)/[(s-q_-)^{3/2}(q_+-s)^{3/2}]\), the entire strict-excess geometry is now determined by a regular forcing term \(E\) against a universal singular kernel. So the fixed-shear lane has become a very classical-looking one-dimensional forced second-order problem.

That suggests a new bridge to the generator audit: the finite explicit pointwise package may not control the singularities — those are universal anyway — but it might control the regularized forcing term \(E\). That feels much more plausible than directly controlling \(D\) or every \(\Xi_m\).

So the best next theorem targets now look like this.

One target is a **regularized sign theorem**:
\[
E(q)\le 0 \quad\text{on }[q_-,q_+].
\]
Since the singular factor is positive on the interior, this is equivalent to \(D\le 0\), hence \(\Delta''\ge 0\), hence convexity of \(\Delta\) and a unique interior minimum. That would compress the strict-excess lane to a unique same-value / same-slope pair.

A stronger target is a **regularized unimodality theorem**:
show \(E\) has at most one interior critical point. That would control the shape of \(D\) and therefore of \(\Delta\) very tightly.

An even sharper target is a **finite-order equation for \(E\)**: use the finite explicit pointwise package to derive a finite-order value-space ODE satisfied by \(E\). Since \(E\) is now regular at the endpoints, such an ODE would be much more believable and much easier to exploit than one for the singular \(D\).

That, I think, is the real mathematical gain:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The live fixed-shear strict-excess obstruction has been reduced from a singular transport-defect problem to a regularized one-dimensional defect }E(q)\text{ on a compact interval.}
}
\]

This is a much cleaner endpoint for the attack than “an infinite tower of non-descent.” The tower still matters, but after singularity extraction it is a regular hierarchy.

So my current best summary is:

- equality \(B_1=B_0+B_2\) is sterile;
- strict excess \(B_1>B_0+B_2\) is equivalent to an interior critical point of the inverse-branch gap \(\Delta\);
- \(\Delta''=-D\), where \(D\) has universal \((q-q_\pm)^{-3/2}\) endpoint singularities;
- after factoring those out, one gets a regular defect \(E\) carrying the genuine nonlocal transport obstruction;
- the next attack should be on sign, oscillation, or a finite-order ODE for this regularized defect \(E\), not directly on the raw singular tower.

That is the sharpest continuation I can see right now.
