_Thought for 49s_

Yes. There is a sharper attack now, and it improves the strict fixed-shear lane a lot.

I also need to clean up one sign convention from the previous round. If I define
\[
J(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
K(q):=J(\phi_2(q))-J(\phi_0(q)),
\]
then
\[
K(q)=\Delta'(q),
\qquad
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and the correct derivative is
\[
\boxed{
K'(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
}
\]
So the sign is the reverse of how I wrote it once earlier. The geometry below uses the corrected version.

What follows is the sharpest continuation I see.

---

## 1. The strict-excess witness is a zero-average chord for \(H\)

The update note explicitly suggested attacking sign/comparison laws for
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\]
or \(\Xi\) across same-value chords.【181†source】

That is exactly right, because
\[
J'(u)=-H(u),
\]
so
\[
K(q)=J(\phi_2(q))-J(\phi_0(q))
=
-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
K(q_*)=0
\iff
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H(u)\,du=0.
}
\]

Since strict excess is equivalent to the existence of some \(q_*\) with \(K(q_*)=0\), every strict-excess witness is a **zero-average same-value chord** for the scalar field \(H\).

That is already better than phrasing it only as “same-value / same-slope.” It says the strict-excess lane is a chord-average cancellation problem for one scalar field.

---

## 2. Immediate consequence: every strict-excess witness forces a zero of \(H\) on the chord

Because \(H\) is continuous on the open outer-run chord (the outer runs are away from zeros of \(\widehat Q'\)), a zero average of \(H\) over the chord implies that \(H\) cannot keep one strict sign there.

So by the intermediate value theorem:

\[
\boxed{
K(q_*)=0
\Longrightarrow
\exists\,\xi_*\in(\phi_0(q_*),\phi_2(q_*))
\text{ with }
H(\xi_*)=0.
}
\]

Since \(\widehat Q'(\xi_*)\neq 0\) on the outer runs and their interior chords, this is equivalent to
\[
\widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0.
\]

So every strict-excess witness produces an **inflection-point witness on the interior of the same-value chord**.

This is a real new gain.

It means the strict fixed-shear lane is not just about outer-shell transport. It necessarily threads through interior zeros of \(\widehat Q''\) along those chords.

---

## 3. The live strict-excess lane is now a two-point/one-point coupled witness

At a strict-excess witness \(q_*\), we therefore get three linked points:

- the two outer same-value points
  \[
  u_0=\phi_0(q_*),\qquad u_2=\phi_2(q_*),
  \]
  with
  \[
  \widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2)=q_*,
  \qquad
  \widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)
  \]
  because \(K(q_*)=0\);

- and an interior chord point
  \[
  \xi_*\in(u_0,u_2)
  \]
  with
  \[
  \widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0.
  \]

So the strict-excess case has been reduced to a very rigid configuration:

\[
\boxed{
u_0<u_1<u_2,\quad
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\quad
\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2),\quad
\widehat Q''(u_1)=0.
}
\]

This is much sharper than the original lobe-amplitude inequality.

It says a strict-excess witness is a **same-value / same-slope outer pair bracketing an inflection point**.

---

## 4. There is also an exact weighted mean-value form

Because
\[
K(q)=J(\phi_2(q))-J(\phi_0(q)),
\]
the ordinary mean value theorem gives
\[
K(q)=J'(\eta_q)\,(\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q))
\]
for some
\[
\eta_q\in(\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q)).
\]
Since \(J'=-H\), this becomes
\[
\boxed{
K(q)=-H(\eta_q)\,\Delta(q).
}
\]

This is a very strong identity-in-form. Since \(\Delta(q)>0\) on a genuine outer overlap interval, the sign of \(K(q)\) is exactly the opposite of the sign of \(H\) at some interior chord point \(\eta_q\).

So strict excess is equivalent to the statement that the chord-average witness crosses through an interior zero of \(H\).

This makes the right target much clearer:

> prove that \(H\) cannot change sign on same-value outer chords.

If that holds, strict excess dies immediately.

---

## 5. This yields the cleanest direct closure target so far

The sharpest theorem target I see now is:

### Same-value chord sign theorem
For every same-value chord between the two outer runs in the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model,
\[
H(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\]
has one sign on the chord interior.

Then
\[
\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du
\]
can never vanish, so \(K(q)\neq 0\) for all \(q\), hence strict excess is impossible.

This is cleaner than attacking the regularized defect \(E\) directly, and it is more tightly aligned with the updated note’s recommendation to study \(H\) or \(\Xi\) on same-value chords.【181†source】

---

## 6. A slightly weaker but still useful theorem target

Even if a full one-sign theorem for \(H\) is too much, we now have a weaker target that still gives major compression:

### One-crossing theorem for \(H\) on same-value chords
Show that along every same-value outer chord, \(H\) has at most one zero and crosses with a fixed orientation.

Then the chord-mass function
\[
K(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du
\]
has controlled sign variation, and strict excess would reduce to at most one witness.

So the fixed-shear lane would collapse to a unique same-value / same-slope / inflection-point configuration.

That is probably enough to hand back to the scalar-local side or to the package-level side as a single canonical witness.

---

## 7. This also clarifies the role of the regularized defect \(E\)

The \(E\)-formalism is still correct, but now it is clearly downstream.

Indeed,
\[
K'(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\]
and after stripping off the universal endpoint singularity of \(K'\), one gets the regularized defect \(E\).

So the natural order of attack is now:

1. attack the zero set of \(K\) via chord-sign properties of \(H\);
2. only if needed, study \(K'\) and its regularization \(E\).

That is a cleaner hierarchy than trying to start from \(E\) itself.

---

## 8. Strongest new structural finding

The strongest new finding from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{A strict-excess fixed-shear witness is exactly a zero-average same-value chord for }
H=\widehat Q''/(\widehat Q')^2,
\text{ hence necessarily brackets an interior zero of } \widehat Q''.
}
\]

So the strict fixed-shear lane is no longer best seen as a raw lobe-amplitude problem, nor even primarily as a defect-tower problem. It is a **same-value chord sign problem** for one scalar field \(H\).

That is the cleanest transport formulation I have reached.

---

## 9. Best next theorem targets

The most promising next targets are now:

### Target 1
Prove a one-sign theorem for
\[
H(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\]
on every same-value outer chord.

This would kill strict excess immediately.

### Target 2
Prove a one-crossing theorem for \(H\) on same-value outer chords.

This would reduce strict excess to at most one canonical witness.

### Target 3
Prove a closure law
\[
\widehat Q''(u)=G(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u))
\]
on the outer shells. Then the sign of \(H\) on same-value chords becomes a planar comparison problem, which is exactly the kind of transport-closure theorem the updated note points toward.【181†source】

Of these, Target 1 is the sharpest direct kill.

---

## 10. Bottom line

So the sharpened attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Replace the strict-excess lobe inequality by the same-value chord mass }
K(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} \frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}\,du,
}

and try to prove that this mass can never vanish, either because \(H\) has one sign on same-value outer chords, or because its sign variation is too rigid to support a zero.

That is the clearest and strongest remaining theorem-level attack I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:41:09)

_Thought for 1m 8s_

Yes — there is a sharper attack now, and it fits the updated note very well.

The best move is to stop working with only the first chord-mass
\[
K(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}
\]
and instead use the full **reciprocal-slope tower** that the new note explicitly points toward.【181†source】

This gives a much cleaner hierarchy of obstructions.

---

## 1. Put the strict-excess lane into the \(J_n\)-tower

Define
\[
J_1(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u)
\quad (n\ge 1).
\]
This is exactly the reciprocal-slope tower from the note.【181†source】

On the common value interval \(\mathcal I\) of the two outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\), define the outer-run defects
\[
K_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]

Then the key identity is:

\[
\boxed{
\frac{d}{dq}K_n(q)=K_{n+1}(q).
}
\]

Indeed,
\[
\frac{d}{dq}J_n(\phi_j(q))
=
J_n'(\phi_j(q))\,\phi_j'(q)
=
J_n'(\phi_j(q))\,J_1(\phi_j(q))
=
J_{n+1}(\phi_j(q)).
\]

So the whole fixed-shear transport tower along same-value chords is just the ordinary \(q\)-derivative tower of one scalar function \(K_1\).

That is the first big simplification.

---

## 2. What strict excess means in the tower

Strict excess
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
is equivalent to an interior zero of
\[
K_1(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

So the strict-excess witness is:

\[
\boxed{
K_1(q_*)=0.
}
\]

At such a point,
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=q_*,
\qquad
\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*)).
\]

So \(K_1=0\) is exactly the same-value / same-slope outer witness already isolated in the new note.【181†source】

Now the higher tower says more:

- if \(K_2(q_*)=0\) too, then at the same equal-\(\widehat Q\) pair one also has
  \[
  J_2(\phi_2(q_*))=J_2(\phi_0(q_*)),
  \]
  i.e. the first even-jet witness matches;
- if \(K_3(q_*)=0\), then the next rung matches too;
- and so on.

So the strict-excess lane is now an explicit **finite jet-separation** problem on the outer runs.

That is much sharper than the raw lobe inequality.

---

## 3. Rolle gives a finite witness-elimination scheme

Because
\[
K_{n+1}=K_n',
\]
any multiplicity of strict-excess witnesses forces higher-rung coincidences.

Concretely:

- if \(K_1\) has two distinct zeros on \(\mathcal I\), then \(K_2\) vanishes somewhere between them;
- if \(K_1\) has three distinct zeros, then \(K_3\) vanishes somewhere;
- more generally, if \(K_1\) has \(m+1\) distinct zeros, then \(K_{m+1}\) vanishes somewhere on \(\mathcal I\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Multiple strict-excess witnesses force higher-rung equal-jet coincidences in the }J_n\text{-tower.}
}
\]

This is a real improvement. It means the live lane is not a diffuse transport family; it is a finite zero-count problem for one scalar function and its derivatives.

---

## 4. New theorem targets that are now enough

This gives three very concrete attack targets.

### Target A: finite-rung chord separation
Show that for some finite \(N\),
\[
u\longmapsto \bigl(\widehat Q(u),J_1(u),\dots,J_N(u)\bigr)
\]
separates points on the relevant outer runs.

Then no point of the overlap interval can satisfy
\[
K_1=K_2=\cdots=K_N=0,
\]
and repeated Rolle collapses the possible witness structure dramatically.

This is exactly in the spirit of the note’s “same-value / same-slope / even-jet witness” sharpening.【181†source】

### Target B: sign theorem for one higher rung
Show that some \(K_n\) has fixed sign on \(\mathcal I\).

Then the previous rung \(K_{n-1}\) is monotone. For example:

- if \(K_2\) has one sign, then \(K_1\) is monotone, so strict excess gives at most one witness;
- if \(K_3\) has one sign, then \(K_2\) is monotone, so \(K_1\) can have at most two zeros;
- and so on.

So even weak sign control on a finite rung gives a strong witness-count bound.

### Target C: branch-independent closure law
Prove
\[
\widehat Q''(u)=G(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u))
\]
on the relevant outer shells.

Then \(J_2,J_3,\dots\) all become recursive functions of finitely many lower variables, and the tower closes to a planar or finite-dimensional comparison problem. This is exactly the kind of transport-closure target recommended in the new note.【181†source】

---

## 5. Why this sharpens the attack more than the \(E\)-formalism

The regularized defect \(E\) is still useful, but it is now clearly downstream.

The real live object is \(K_1\):

\[
K_1(q)=\Delta'(q),
\qquad
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

The \(E\)-formalism studies \(K_1'\) after universal endpoint regularization. That is good for convexity/shape analysis. But the tower formulation is better for **finite witness elimination**, because it turns the lane into:

\[
K_1=0,\quad K_2=K_1',\quad K_3=K_2',\ \dots
\]

So the strict-excess problem is now a direct calculus problem on one scalar function \(K_1\), with outer-shell jet data encoded by the \(J_n\).

That is cleaner and better aligned with the new draft note.

---

## 6. Best immediate attack from here

The best next attack is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Try to prove a finite-rung sign/separation theorem for the }J_n\text{-tower on the outer runs.}
}
\]

The most realistic first version is probably:

> prove \(K_2\) has fixed sign on \(\mathcal I\).

Because that would already imply \(K_1\) is monotone, hence strict excess gives at most one same-value / same-slope witness.

That would be a major reduction.

A stronger version is:

> prove \((\widehat Q,J_1,J_2)\) already separates points on the outer runs.

That would kill the same-value / same-slope / first-even-jet witness directly.

---

## 7. Strongest new structural finding

The strongest new finding from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The strict three-run fixed-shear lane is now a finite witness-elimination problem in the reciprocal-slope tower }J_n,
\text{ with }K_{n+1}=K_n'.
}
\]

So the remaining transport problem is no longer best viewed as “a hard global amplitude inequality.” It is a one-variable zero-count problem for \(K_1\), controlled by finite-rung jet separation on the outer shells.

That is, I think, the sharpest theorem-level attack now available.

Here’s a brief note you can share with all agents:

---

Team — one important draft correction: there was a sign reversal in the downstream strict three-run fixed-shear narrative, and it has now been corrected.

The correct trichotomy is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1 \quad\text{open outer overlap,}
]
[
B_0+B_2=B_1 \quad\text{touching,}
]
[
B_1>B_0+B_2 \quad\text{separation.}
]

So the inverse-branch-gap / (\Delta) / (\Xi) transport setup belongs only to the actual overlap regime
[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
]
not to the separation regime
[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
]

In particular, any argument that starts from outer inverse branches (\phi_0,\phi_2), an overlap interval (\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2), or a gap function
[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
]
should now be understood as conditional on genuine outer overlap. The transport identities themselves may still be fine once overlap exists; what was wrong was the regime identification.

Practical consequence: if you are using the strict three-run lane, please make sure you are not treating
[
B_1>B_0+B_2
]
as the “live overlap” case. That is the separation case. The live overlap case is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
]

This does not damage the broader transport endgame, but it does mean the (\Delta/\Xi) story must be phrased as an overlap-regime analysis, not as something triggered by the opposite inequality.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:44:59)

_Thought for 37s_

Good catch. That correction is important, and it **does** change how I should phrase the transport attack.

The right correction is:

\[
B_0+B_2>B_1 \quad\text{open outer overlap,}
\]
\[
B_0+B_2=B_1 \quad\text{touching,}
\]
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2 \quad\text{separation.}
\]

So my earlier \(\Delta/\Xi/K\)-story was attached to the wrong inequality. The machinery itself is still fine, but it belongs to the **overlap** regime, not the separation regime.【180†source】

That actually sharpens the attack rather than damaging it.

---

## Corrected picture

The inverse-branch setup
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2,\qquad
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
only makes sense on a genuine common value interval
\[
\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\neq\varnothing,
\]
so it belongs only to
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]

Thus:

- **separation**
  \[
  B_1>B_0+B_2
  \]
  is **not** a transport-via-overlap case at all;
- **touching**
  \[
  B_0+B_2=B_1
  \]
  remains the sterile boundary case;
- the real transport lane is the **open overlap**
  \[
  B_0+B_2>B_1.
  \]

So the corrected fixed-shear endgame is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{analyze and kill the genuine overlap regime }B_0+B_2>B_1.
}
\]

That is the right regime identification now.【180†source】

---

## What survives from the previous attack

Quite a lot survives, after reindexing to the overlap regime.

Assume
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so there is an open overlap interval \(\mathcal I\subset \Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\), and define inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]

Then define
\[
J_1(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
K_1(q):=J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q)).
\]

This is still the right first transport observable. In fact,
\[
K_1(q)=\Delta'(q).
\]

And the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u),
\qquad
K_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q))
\]
still satisfies
\[
\boxed{
K_{n+1}(q)=\frac{d}{dq}K_n(q).
}
\]

So the whole finite witness-elimination framework is still valid — but now as an **overlap-regime** analysis, not a strict-excess analysis.

That is the first sharpened point.

---

## First corrected theorem target: overlap forces a same-value / same-slope witness

Now that we are in the correct regime, the next thing to check is the endpoint behavior of \(K_1\) on the overlap interval.

At one end of \(\mathcal I\), one outer inverse branch approaches a critical endpoint of its run, so
\[
J_1=\frac1{\widehat Q'}
\]
blows up there, while the other branch stays finite. At the other end, the roles reverse.

Therefore \(K_1\) has opposite endpoint signs, so by continuity:

\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\exists\,q_*\in\mathcal I
\text{ such that }
K_1(q_*)=0.
}
\]

Equivalently, in the actual overlap regime there is always a same-value / same-slope outer witness:
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*)),
\qquad
\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]

This is now the correct transport-theoretic consequence of open overlap.

That is a real theorem target, and it is much cleaner than my earlier misattached “strict excess gives a witness” story.

---

## Second corrected theorem target: finite-rung elimination on the actual overlap lane

Once you have \(K_1(q_*)=0\), the higher tower becomes relevant.

Since
\[
K_{n+1}=K_n',
\]
you can now attack the genuine live overlap lane by any of the following:

- prove \(K_2\) has fixed sign on \(\mathcal I\), so \(K_1\) is monotone and the witness is unique;
- prove \((\widehat Q,J_1,J_2)\) separates points on the outer runs, which kills same-value / same-slope / first-even-jet coincidence;
- more generally, prove finite-rung separation for
  \[
  u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),J_1(u),\dots,J_N(u)).
  \]

This is now the cleanest hard attack on the real live overlap regime.

So the corrected sharpened target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Kill the open overlap regime }B_0+B_2>B_1
\text{ by finite-rung separation in the reciprocal-slope tower.}
}
\]

That is much sharper than just “analyze overlap.”

---

## Third corrected theorem target: chord-sign law for \(H\)

The \(H\)-mass formulation also survives and is probably still the cleanest direct route.

Define
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}.
\]
Then
\[
J_1'(u)=-H(u),
\]
so on an overlap chord,
\[
K_1(q)=J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q))
=
-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du.
\]

Thus the open overlap regime yields a same-value / same-slope witness exactly when the chord mass vanishes:
\[
K_1(q_*)=0
\iff
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H(u)\,du=0.
\]

So the clean direct closure target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove that }H=\widehat Q''/(\widehat Q')^2
\text{ has one sign on every same-value outer chord.}
}
\]

If that holds, the chord integral cannot vanish, so the overlap witness cannot exist, and therefore the open overlap regime \(B_0+B_2>B_1\) is impossible.

This is probably the single best corrected theorem target after the sign fix, and it matches the note exactly.【181†source】

---

## What becomes of the separation regime?

The separation regime
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
is no longer part of the \(\Delta/\phi_0/\phi_2\) transport story. It should now be treated as the **non-overlap** case, not as the live transport case.

So one thing I would explicitly discard from my previous attack is any sentence of the form:

> “strict excess produces inverse branches / same-value transport / \(\Delta\)-gap / \(\Xi\)-fork.”

That was the wrong regime.

The corrected statement is:

> **open overlap** produces inverse branches / same-value transport / \(\Delta\)-gap / \(\Xi\)-fork.

That is the key cleanup.

---

## Sharpened next attack

So the corrected attack is now:

1. stay entirely inside the genuine overlap regime
   \[
   B_0+B_2>B_1;
   \]
2. define outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) and the reciprocal-slope tower \(J_n\);
3. use endpoint behavior to force a same-value / same-slope witness \(K_1=0\);
4. attack that witness by either:
   - a one-sign theorem for
     \[
     H=\widehat Q''/(\widehat Q')^2
     \]
     on same-value outer chords, or
   - finite-rung tower separation \((\widehat Q,J_1,J_2,\dots)\) on the outer runs.

That is, I think, the cleanest sharpened attack after the correction.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:47:29)

_Thought for 51s_

With the corrected regime in place, the clean next attack is to work entirely in the genuine overlap case
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
and push the reciprocal-slope tower one rung further. That gives a sharper witness structure than I had before, and it fits the updated notes well.【180†source】【181†source】

Let \(\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)\subset \Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\) be the common value interval of the two outer runs, with inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]
Define
\[
J_1(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
and the same-value transport defects
\[
K_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]
Then, exactly as before,
\[
K_{n+1}(q)=\frac{d}{dq}K_n(q).
\]

The first useful fact is that \(K_1\) has the right endpoint behavior for a real witness. Near one end of \(\mathcal I\), one outer inverse branch approaches a simple critical endpoint, so \(J_1=1/\widehat Q'\) blows up there while the other branch stays finite; near the other end the roles reverse. Thus
\[
K_1(q)\to -\infty \text{ at one end of }\mathcal I,
\qquad
K_1(q)\to +\infty \text{ at the other}.
\]
So by continuity there exists at least one
\[
q_*\in\mathcal I
\]
with
\[
K_1(q_*)=0.
\]
This is the corrected overlap-regime theorem candidate: open overlap forces a same-value / same-slope outer witness, not strict excess.

Now rewrite \(K_1\) in the scalar field
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}.
\]
Since
\[
J_1'(u)=-H(u),
\]
one has
\[
K_1(q)=J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q))
=
-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du.
\]
So a witness \(K_1(q_*)=0\) is exactly a zero-average same-value chord for \(H\). By continuity, every such witness forces an interior point
\[
\xi_*\in(\phi_0(q_*),\phi_2(q_*))
\]
with
\[
H(\xi_*)=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
\widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0,
\]
since \(\widehat Q'\neq 0\) on the interior of the outer runs. So every genuine overlap witness is a same-value / same-slope outer pair bracketing an inflection point. That is already a much tighter geometric package than the raw overlap inequality.

The next rung is where the real sharpening happens. Define
\[
K_2(q)=K_1'(q)=J_2(\phi_2(q))-J_2(\phi_0(q)),
\]
with
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)J_1'(u)=-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So
\[
K_2(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
with the corrected sign convention.

In the generic simple-critical setting, \(K_2\) has a very useful endpoint behavior: it blows up positively at both ends of \(\mathcal I\). The reason is simple. Near the left endpoint,
\[
K_1(q)\sim -c_-(q-q_-)^{-1/2},
\]
so
\[
K_2(q)=K_1'(q)\sim +\frac{c_-}{2}(q-q_-)^{-3/2}>0.
\]
Near the right endpoint,
\[
K_1(q)\sim +c_+(q_+-q)^{-1/2},
\]
so again
\[
K_2(q)=K_1'(q)\sim +\frac{c_+}{2}(q_+-q)^{-3/2}>0.
\]
Thus
\[
\boxed{
K_2(q)>0 \text{ near both endpoints of the overlap interval.}
}
\]

That is the new theorem-level gain.

It means that the only way the overlap lane can have more than the forced witness structure is if \(K_2\) develops interior negativity. So the real live question is no longer “does overlap produce a witness?” It does. The question is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }K_2(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))\text{ ever become negative in the interior?}
}
\]

This reframes the overlap lane in a very sharp way.

If one proves
\[
K_2(q)\ge 0 \qquad (q\in\mathcal I),
\]
then \(K_1\) is monotone increasing on \(\mathcal I\). Since \(K_1\to -\infty\) at one end and \(+\infty\) at the other, it follows that \(K_1\) has exactly one zero. So open overlap collapses to a unique same-value / same-slope witness. That is already a major reduction.

And if one can prove the stronger statement that \(H\) itself has one sign on every same-value outer chord, then
\[
K_1(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)}H(u)\,du
\]
can never vanish, so open overlap is impossible outright. That remains the cleanest direct kill target and matches the updated note exactly.【181†source】

So the sharpened attack is now:

First, stay only in the corrected overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]

Second, use \(K_1\) to force a same-value / same-slope witness and hence an interior inflection point on the chord.

Third, attack \(K_2\). Since \(K_2>0\) near both endpoints, any surviving overlap complexity must come from an interior sign reversal of \(K_2\). So the strongest next theorem target is
\[
K_2(q)\ge 0 \text{ on }\mathcal I.
\]

That would make \(K_1\) monotone and reduce the whole overlap lane to a unique witness. If one can go one step further and prove one-sign behavior for \(H\) on same-value outer chords, the overlap lane dies completely.

So the cleanest continuation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Attack the sign of }K_2(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\text{ on the genuine overlap interval.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the sharpest theorem-level attack now available after the sign correction.

_Thought for 34s_

With the corrected regime in place, the clean next attack is to push the **same-value chord tower** one rung further and isolate exactly what would have to happen if the overlap lane were still alive.

We are now only in the genuine overlap case
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so there is a common value interval \(\mathcal I\subset \Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\) for the two outer runs, inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q,
\]
and the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\qquad
K_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]
As before,
\[
K_{n+1}=K_n'.
\]
This is now the right transport hierarchy for the live overlap lane, not for the separation lane.【180†source】【181†source】

The first rung \(K_1\) is the same-value / same-slope defect:
\[
K_1(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
Endpoint behavior forces
\[
K_1\to -\infty \text{ at one end of }\mathcal I,\qquad
K_1\to +\infty \text{ at the other},
\]
so there is at least one witness \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) with
\[
K_1(q_*)=0.
\]
That gives the outer same-value / same-slope pair.

The new useful point is what happens one rung up. Define
\[
K_2(q)=K_1'(q)=J_2(\phi_2(q))-J_2(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
J_2(u)=-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}=-\Xi(u).
\]
So, with the sign convention fixed,
\[
K_2(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

In the generic simple-critical setting, \(K_2\) has a decisive endpoint feature:
\[
K_2(q)>0
\quad\text{near both endpoints of }\mathcal I.
\]
That follows directly from differentiating the endpoint asymptotics of \(K_1\). This is the first real positive structural fact for the corrected overlap lane.

So now the live question is no longer whether there is a witness. There is. The question is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }K_2\text{ ever become negative in the interior of }\mathcal I?
}
\]

If the answer is no, then \(K_2\ge 0\) on \(\mathcal I\), hence \(K_1\) is monotone increasing, and because \(K_1\) goes from \(-\infty\) to \(+\infty\), it has exactly one zero. So the whole overlap lane collapses to a **unique** same-value / same-slope witness.

That already would be a major simplification.

Now suppose, for contradiction, that \(K_2\) does become negative somewhere. Since it is positive near both endpoints, it must have at least two distinct zeros
\[
a<b \quad\text{in }\mathcal I,
\]
and a negative interior minimum at some \(c\in(a,b)\). At that minimum,
\[
K_2(c)<0,
\qquad
K_3(c)=K_2'(c)=0.
\]

So any surviving non-monotone overlap automatically produces a more rigid higher-rung witness:
\[
\boxed{
K_3(c)=0 \text{ and } K_2(c)<0.
}
\]

In terms of the \(J_n\)-tower, this means there is a same-value pair on the outer runs with equal \(J_3\) but strictly ordered \(J_2\). Equivalently, there is a same-value chord where the first even-jet mismatch is negative but the next odd-rung defect already vanishes.

That is a much sharper obstruction than just “overlap exists.”

So the overlap lane has now split very cleanly:

- either \(K_2\ge 0\) on \(\mathcal I\), and we are in the **unique witness** regime;
- or \(K_2\) changes sign, and then there is an interior point with
  \[
  K_3=0,\qquad K_2<0,
  \]
  i.e. an explicit higher-rung witness in the reciprocal-slope tower.

This gives the best next theorem target I can see:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove }K_2(q)\ge 0\text{ on every genuine overlap interval } \mathcal I.
}
\]

That would make \(K_1\) monotone and collapse the overlap lane to one canonical witness.

A slightly weaker but still very useful target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove }K_3(q)\neq 0\text{ whenever }K_2(q)<0.
}
\]

That would rule out any interior negative dip of \(K_2\), and hence also imply \(K_2\ge 0\).

There is also a very clean integral reformulation. Since
\[
J_2'(u)=\frac{1}{J_1(u)}\,J_3(u)=\widehat Q'(u)\,J_3(u),
\]
one has
\[
K_2(q)=\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} J_2'(u)\,du
=
\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} \widehat Q'(u)\,J_3(u)\,du.
\]
So if one can prove a one-sign theorem for \(J_3\) on same-value outer chords, then \(K_2\) has one sign and we are done. This matches the updated note’s recommendation to attack the \(\Xi\)-lane through sign/monotonicity/transport-mass theorems or through separation of \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) on the outer run.【181†source】

So the sharpened attack is now:

1. work only in the corrected overlap regime \(B_0+B_2>B_1\);
2. force the first witness \(K_1=0\);
3. attack the sign of \(K_2\);
4. if \(K_2\) could dip negative, it would force a higher-rung witness \(K_3=0\) with \(K_2<0\);
5. therefore the key theorem to prove is either \(K_2\ge 0\) directly, or a sign/separation law for \(J_3\) that implies it.

That is the cleanest and sharpest continuation I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:52:54)

_Thought for 59s_

Here is the sharpened attack after the regime correction.

The right live regime is now the genuine outer overlap case
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
and the \(\phi_0,\phi_2,\Delta\)-transport setup belongs only there, not to the separation case.【180†source】 The updated note also explicitly recommends the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
as the right framework for the same-value / same-slope / even-jet witnesses.【181†source】 I think that is exactly the right place to push.

Let \(\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)\subset \Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\) be the common value interval of the two outer runs, with inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,\qquad \widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]
Define the same-value transport defects
\[
K_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]
Then the exact identity
\[
K_{n+1}(q)=\frac{d}{dq}K_n(q)
\]
still holds. So the overlap lane is a one-variable derivative tower.

The first thing to record is the full endpoint asymptotic pattern of this tower in the generic simple-critical setting. Near the left endpoint \(q_-\), one outer inverse branch approaches a simple critical endpoint, so
\[
J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q))\sim -c_-(q-q_-)^{-1/2},
\]
while near the right endpoint
\[
J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q))\sim +c_+(q_+-q)^{-1/2},
\]
with \(c_\pm>0\). Differentiating repeatedly gives the clean universal pattern
\[
K_{2m}(q)>0 \text{ near both endpoints},
\]
\[
K_{2m+1}(q)<0 \text{ near }q_-,
\qquad
K_{2m+1}(q)>0 \text{ near }q_+,
\]
for every \(m\ge 0\), after fixing the natural orientation of the outer runs. In particular:
\[
K_1:\ -\infty \to +\infty,\qquad
K_2>0 \text{ near both ends},\qquad
K_3<0 \text{ near the left and } >0 \text{ near the right}.
\]

This is the first new solid structural gain. It means the corrected overlap regime has a completely rigid tower-endpoint signature.

A first immediate consequence is that the open overlap lane necessarily contains at least one same-value / same-slope witness, because
\[
K_1(q)\to -\infty \text{ at }q_-,
\qquad
K_1(q)\to +\infty \text{ at }q_+,
\]
so there is some \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) with
\[
K_1(q_*)=0.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*)),
\qquad
\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]
So the corrected overlap regime always forces a same-value / same-slope outer witness. That is now the right interpretation of the open overlap case.

A second consequence is that any attempt to kill the overlap lane by a finite-rung descent theorem has to fail in the generic simple-critical setting. If for some \(N\),
\[
J_N(\phi_2(q))=J_N(\phi_0(q))
\quad\text{for all }q\in\mathcal I,
\]
then
\[
K_N\equiv 0,
\]
which is impossible because the endpoint asymptotics above show that no \(K_N\) can vanish identically: even rungs are positive near both ends, odd rungs have opposite endpoint signs. So the corrected analog of my earlier “no finite-rung descent” statement is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the generic simple-critical overlap regime, no finite-rung reciprocal-slope observable }J_N
\text{ descends to a single-valued function of }q=\widehat Q
\text{ across the two outer runs.}
}
\]

That is a real negative finding, and it is now attached to the correct regime. It also matches the spirit of the package audit: the live obstruction is genuinely transport-level, not one more finite local closure. 【181†source】

The next sharpening is the one that matters for closure. Since
\[
K_2=K_1'
\]
is positive near both ends, the only way the overlap lane can remain complicated is if \(K_2\) dips negative somewhere in the interior. But if that happens, then because it is positive near both ends it must have at least two zeros, and hence \(K_3=K_2'\) must vanish at an interior point where \(K_2\) is negative. In other words, any non-monotone overlap necessarily produces a stronger higher-rung witness:
\[
K_3(q^\dagger)=0,\qquad K_2(q^\dagger)<0
\]
for some interior \(q^\dagger\in\mathcal I\).

So the corrected overlap lane has split very cleanly:

- either \(K_2\ge 0\) on \(\mathcal I\), in which case \(K_1\) is monotone increasing and there is exactly one same-value / same-slope witness;
- or \(K_2\) dips negative, and then the overlap lane automatically produces a higher-rung witness with \(K_3=0\) and \(K_2<0\).

That means the best current theorem target is no longer “prove overlap impossible immediately.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove }K_2(q)\ge 0\text{ on every genuine overlap interval.}
}
\]

If that lands, then \(K_1\) is monotone, and the whole overlap lane reduces to a unique outer same-value / same-slope witness. That would be a major simplification.

There is also a very useful integral reformulation. Since
\[
J_1'(u)=-H(u),\qquad H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2},
\]
we have
\[
K_1(q)=J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q))
=
-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du.
\]
So a witness \(K_1(q_*)=0\) is exactly a zero-average same-value chord for the scalar field \(H\). By continuity, every such witness brackets an interior point \(\xi_*\in(\phi_0(q_*),\phi_2(q_*))\) with
\[
H(\xi_*)=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
\widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0,
\]
because \(\widehat Q'\neq 0\) on the outer-run interiors. So every overlap witness is a same-value / same-slope outer pair bracketing an inflection point.

This gives the cleanest direct closure route still available: prove that \(H\) has one sign on every same-value outer chord. Then the chord integral \(K_1\) can never vanish, so the overlap regime dies immediately. This is, in my view, still the strongest direct kill target, and it matches the updated note exactly.【181†source】

So the sharpened attack is now:

stay only in the corrected overlap regime \(B_0+B_2>B_1\); use the reciprocal-slope tower \(J_n\) and its defects \(K_n\); exploit the universal endpoint signature \(K_{2m}>0\) near both ends and \(K_{2m+1}\) of opposite signs; force the first witness \(K_1=0\); and then attack the sign of \(K_2\). If \(K_2\ge 0\), the overlap lane collapses to a unique witness. If one can prove the stronger one-sign theorem for
\[
H=\frac{\widehat Q''}{(\widehat Q')^2}
\]
on same-value outer chords, then the overlap lane dies outright.

That is the cleanest and strongest continuation I can see after the regime correction.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:53:54)

I’m pushing directly on the strongest live target: show the corrected overlap lane cannot sustain \(K_2<0\) in the interior. The cleanest route is to turn \(K_2\) into a same-value chord integral and see whether the outer-shell geometry forces its sign.

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next attack.

I think the best move now is to attack \(K_2\) exactly the same way \(K_1\) was attacked: rewrite it as a **same-value chord mass** for a new scalar field. That makes the overlap lane much more rigid.

We are in the corrected live regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
with outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q,
\]
and reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\qquad
K_n(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]
The corrected endpoint pattern is that \(K_1\) has opposite endpoint signs, while \(K_2\) is positive near both ends.【180†source】【181†source】

The first rung already had the chord-mass form
\[
K_1(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_1(u)\,du,
\qquad
H_1(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}.
\]
So \(K_1(q_*)=0\) means the first chord mass vanishes.

Now do the same one rung higher. Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)J_1'(u)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
we have
\[
J_2'(u)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}\,\partial_u J_2(u)=\widehat Q'(u)\,J_3(u).
\]
So define the second chord field
\[
H_2(u):=-J_2'(u).
\]
Then
\[
K_2(q)=J_2(\phi_2(q))-J_2(\phi_0(q))
=
-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_2(u)\,du.
\]

This is the first new theorem-level reduction:

\[
\boxed{
K_2 \text{ is the signed chord mass of }H_2=-J_2'.
}
\]

So the overlap lane has become a two-stage chord problem:

- \(K_1=0\) means zero first chord mass;
- \(K_2\ge 0\) means nonnegative second chord mass;
- \(K_2<0\) would mean a negative second chord mass.

That is much sharper than carrying \(K_2\) abstractly.

Now suppose we are at an actual overlap witness \(q_*\) with
\[
K_1(q_*)=0.
\]
Then
\[
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H_1(u)\,du=0.
\]
So by continuity there exists an interior point \(\xi_1\in(\phi_0(q_*),\phi_2(q_*))\) with
\[
H_1(\xi_1)=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
\widehat Q''(\xi_1)=0.
\]
This was the earlier inflection-point witness.

Now add the sign of \(K_2\).

If \(K_2(q_*)<0\), then
\[
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H_2(u)\,du>0.
\]
So a negative \(K_2\) at a witness forces the second chord field \(H_2\) to have positive average over the same-value / same-slope chord. Thus any surviving non-monotone overlap must support a chord on which:

1. the first chord mass vanishes,
2. the second chord mass is strictly positive.

That is already a very rigid coupled witness.

Let me make that more concrete. Because
\[
J_2(u)=-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
we compute
\[
H_2(u)=-J_2'(u)
=
-\partial_u\!\left(-\frac{\widehat Q''}{(\widehat Q')^3}\right)
=
\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\,\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}.
\]
So
\[
\boxed{
H_2(u)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\,\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}.
}
\]

This is useful because the second term is nonpositive after the minus sign in \(K_2\)-mass language: it penalizes oscillation of \(\widehat Q''\). So \(K_2<0\) can only happen if the \(\widehat Q'''\)-term dominates that quadratic penalty on the whole same-value chord.

That suggests the strongest direct target now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove }H_2(u)\le 0\text{ on every same-value outer chord.}
}
\]

If that holds, then
\[
K_2(q)\ge 0
\]
for every overlap \(q\), so \(K_1\) is monotone increasing, and the whole overlap regime collapses to exactly one same-value / same-slope witness.

This is the cleanest next theorem I can see.

There is also a weaker but still useful version. Since a \(K_1\)-witness already brackets a point where \(\widehat Q''=0\), at such a point the quadratic penalty term in \(H_2\) vanishes, and
\[
H_2(\xi_1)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(\xi_1)}{(\widehat Q'(\xi_1))^3}.
\]
So if one can prove that on any same-value outer chord, every zero of \(\widehat Q''\) has
\[
\frac{\widehat Q'''(\xi)}{(\widehat Q'(\xi))^3}\le 0,
\]
then a neighborhood argument pushes toward \(H_2\le 0\) on the relevant chord, and again \(K_2\ge 0\).

That gives a more local-looking closure target:

\[
\boxed{
\text{At any outer-chord inflection witness, } \widehat Q'''/(\widehat Q')^3 \le 0.
}
\]

This would be enough to constrain the sign of the second chord mass near the only place where the first chord mass can vanish.

So the sharpened picture is now:

- open overlap \(B_0+B_2>B_1\) forces \(K_1=0\) somewhere;
- any \(K_1=0\) witness brackets an inflection point \(\widehat Q''=0\);
- the only way overlap can remain complicated is if \(K_2<0\) somewhere;
- but \(K_2<0\) means the second chord field
  \[
  H_2=\frac{\widehat Q'''}{(\widehat Q')^3}-3\frac{(\widehat Q'')^2}{(\widehat Q')^4}
  \]
  has positive average on the same-value chord.

So the cleanest next attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Attack the sign of }H_2\text{ on same-value outer chords.}
}
\]

If \(H_2\le 0\) on those chords, then \(K_2\ge 0\), \(K_1\) is monotone, and the overlap lane reduces to a unique witness. That is the sharpest theorem-level continuation I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:56:48)

_Thought for 1m 3s_

Here is the sharpest next attack I can see.

We are now firmly in the **correct** live regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so we have a genuine common value interval \(\mathcal I\subset \Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\), outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q,
\]
and the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),\qquad
K_n(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]
This is exactly the framework the update note now recommends for the live overlap lane.【180†source】【181†source】

The first witness is \(K_1=0\). The next job is to kill the possibility that \(K_2\) dips negative. The cleanest way to do that is to express \(K_2\) as a chord mass of a more rigid scalar field.

---

## 1. Rewrite \(K_2\) as a second chord mass

We already have
\[
K_1(q)=J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q))
=
-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_1(u)\,du,
\qquad
H_1(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}.
\]

Now
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\,J_1'(u)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
Differentiate:
\[
J_2'(u)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
+
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}.
\]
So define
\[
H_2(u):=-J_2'(u)
=
\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}.
\]

Then
\[
\boxed{
K_2(q)=J_2(\phi_2(q))-J_2(\phi_0(q))
=
-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_2(u)\,du.
}
\]

So \(K_2\) is the signed chord mass of the scalar field \(H_2\).

This is already better than treating \(K_2\) abstractly.

---

## 2. What a non-monotone overlap would force

We know \(K_2>0\) near both ends of \(\mathcal I\). So the only way the overlap lane stays complicated is if there is some interior point where
\[
K_2(q)<0.
\]

But if \(K_2(q)<0\), then
\[
\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_2(u)\,du>0.
\]
So every negative dip of \(K_2\) forces a same-value outer chord whose **second chord mass is positive**.

At a \(K_1\)-witness \(q_*\), we also have
\[
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H_1(u)\,du=0.
\]
Therefore every genuinely bad overlap witness would have to support a chord on which:

- the first chord mass vanishes;
- the second chord mass is either zero or positive.

That is a very rigid two-level witness.

---

## 3. Inflection-point refinement

At any \(K_1\)-witness \(q_*\), there exists an interior point \(\xi_*\in(\phi_0(q_*),\phi_2(q_*))\) with
\[
H_1(\xi_*)=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
\widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0,
\]
because the first chord mass is zero.

At such an inflection point, the \(H_2\)-field simplifies to
\[
H_2(\xi_*)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(\xi_*)}{(\widehat Q'(\xi_*))^3}.
\]

So the sign of the second chord mass is controlled, at the natural witness point, by the sign of the normalized third derivative at an interior inflection point.

This gives a much sharper closure target:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If one can prove } \frac{\widehat Q'''(\xi)}{(\widehat Q'(\xi))^3}\le 0
\text{ at every outer-chord inflection witness, then }K_2\ge 0.
}
\]

That is because the quadratic penalty term
\[
-3\frac{(\widehat Q'')^2}{(\widehat Q')^4}
\]
in \(H_2\) is already nonpositive everywhere. So the only way \(H_2\) can become positive is if the \(\widehat Q'''\)-term dominates.

This isolates the exact source of trouble.

---

## 4. The cleanest theorem target now

The strongest next target I see is:

### Same-value outer-chord second-mass sign theorem
For every same-value outer chord in the overlap regime,
\[
H_2(u)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}
\le 0
\qquad\text{along the chord interior.}
\]

Then
\[
K_2(q)\ge 0
\]
for all \(q\in\mathcal I\), hence \(K_1\) is monotone increasing. Since \(K_1\to-\infty\) at one end and \(+\infty\) at the other, there is exactly one \(K_1\)-witness.

That would collapse the whole overlap lane to a **unique same-value / same-slope / inflection-point witness**.

That is already a major reduction, even if it does not by itself kill the overlap lane.

---

## 5. A slightly weaker but probably more attackable target

A weaker theorem that would still be very valuable is:

### Inflection-point sign theorem
At every same-value outer-chord witness \(q_*\), if \(\xi_*\) is an interior point with \(\widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0\), then
\[
\frac{\widehat Q'''(\xi_*)}{(\widehat Q'(\xi_*))^3}\le 0.
\]

This would force \(H_2\) to be nonpositive at the natural witness point and is likely enough, together with continuity and the nonpositive quadratic term, to prevent \(K_2\) from dipping negative.

This theorem is much more local-looking and may be easier to connect to the scalar-local or package-level sides.

---

## 6. Why this is better than the earlier \(E\)-formalism

The regularized defect \(E\) is still fine, but it is now clearly **downstream**.

The natural hierarchy is:

- \(K_1\): first chord mass, whose zero is the overlap witness;
- \(K_2\): second chord mass, whose sign controls uniqueness;
- \(H_2\): pointwise scalar field whose chord integral gives \(K_2\).

So the real question is no longer “what is the sign of the regularized second derivative defect?” It is more directly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the second chord mass }K_2\text{ ever be negative, and if so, what sign of }\widehat Q'''/(\widehat Q')^3\text{ forces it?}
}
\]

That is a cleaner, more localizable question.

---

## 7. Strongest new structural finding

The strongest new thing from this attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The overlap lane is now a two-level chord-mass problem: }
K_1=0 \text{ gives the same-value / same-slope witness, and }
K_2=-\int H_2
\text{ is controlled by a normalized third-derivative field with a built-in nonpositive quadratic penalty.}
}
\]

In plain terms: if the overlap lane survives, it is because the third-derivative term beats the curvature-squared penalty on a same-value outer chord. That is a very sharp statement.

---

## 8. Best next step

So the next math attack I would actually run is:

1. stay entirely in the corrected overlap regime \(B_0+B_2>B_1\);
2. force the witness \(K_1=0\);
3. study the sign of
   \[
   H_2(u)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
   -
   3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}
   \]
   on the corresponding same-value outer chord;
4. try to prove \(H_2\le 0\) on such chords, or at least at their interior inflection witnesses.

If that lands, then \(K_2\ge 0\), \(K_1\) is monotone, and the overlap lane collapses to one canonical witness.

That is the sharpest continuation I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 02:01:06)

_Thought for 8s_

Here is the next real attack.

The sharpest move now is to push the corrected overlap lane one rung further **without** overclaiming. We are in the genuine overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2
\]
exist on a common value interval \(\mathcal I\), and the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),\qquad
K_n(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q))
\]
satisfies
\[
K_{n+1}=K_n'.
\]
This is now the right transport framework for the live lane, exactly as the corrected note says. 【180†source】【181†source】

The first witness is
\[
K_1(q)=0,
\]
that is,
\[
\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
equivalently a same-value / same-slope outer pair. The second rung is
\[
K_2(q)=J_2(\phi_2(q))-J_2(\phi_0(q))
=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
We already know \(K_2>0\) near both ends of \(\mathcal I\), so the only way the overlap lane stays complicated is if \(K_2\) dips negative somewhere in the interior.

The cleanest thing to do is to turn \(K_2\) into a chord-mass, exactly as we did for \(K_1\).

Define
\[
H_1(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2},
\qquad
H_2(u):=-J_2'(u)
=
\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}.
\]
Then
\[
K_1(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_1(u)\,du,
\qquad
K_2(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_2(u)\,du.
\]

So the overlap lane is now a two-level chord-mass problem:

- \(K_1=0\) means the first chord mass vanishes;
- \(K_2<0\) means the second chord mass is strictly positive.

That already forces a rigid coupled witness.

Now attack it directly.

## 1. Every \(K_1\)-witness brackets an inflection point

If
\[
K_1(q_*)=0,
\]
then
\[
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H_1(u)\,du=0.
\]
Since \(H_1\) is continuous on the chord interior, there exists
\[
\xi_*\in(\phi_0(q_*),\phi_2(q_*))
\]
with
\[
H_1(\xi_*)=0.
\]
Because \(\widehat Q'\neq 0\) on the outer-run interiors,
\[
H_1(\xi_*)=0 \iff \widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0.
\]

So every overlap witness is automatically a same-value / same-slope outer pair bracketing an interior inflection point. This is already a much stronger structure than the raw overlap inequality.

## 2. If \(K_2<0\) at a \(K_1\)-witness, the third derivative must win against the quadratic penalty

At such an inflection point \(\xi_*\), the \(H_2\)-field simplifies to
\[
H_2(\xi_*)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(\xi_*)}{(\widehat Q'(\xi_*))^3},
\]
because the quadratic penalty term vanishes when \(\widehat Q''(\xi_*)=0\).

Now if at the same chord we also had
\[
K_2(q_*)<0,
\]
then
\[
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H_2(u)\,du>0.
\]
Since
\[
H_2(u)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4},
\]
this says the positive contribution from the normalized third derivative must dominate the everywhere nonpositive quadratic penalty over the entire same-value chord.

So a negative dip of \(K_2\) is not a mild event. It forces a chord on which the third-derivative term beats the curvature-squared penalty strongly enough to make the average positive.

That is the first new no-go style sharpening.

## 3. A \(K_2\)-dip forces a higher-rung witness \(K_3=0\)

Because \(K_2>0\) near both ends of \(\mathcal I\), if \(K_2\) ever becomes negative then it has at least two zeros and an interior negative minimum. Therefore there exists some
\[
q^\dagger\in\mathcal I
\]
with
\[
K_2(q^\dagger)<0,\qquad K_3(q^\dagger)=0.
\]

Since
\[
K_3=K_2',
\]
this means any non-monotone overlap automatically upgrades to a stronger same-value outer witness one rung higher in the reciprocal-slope tower. In terms of pointwise fields, \(K_3=0\) says the third-rung reciprocal-slope observable matches across the two outer same-value points, while \(K_2<0\) says the second-rung defect is still strictly ordered.

So the live overlap lane now splits cleanly:

- either \(K_2\ge 0\) on \(\mathcal I\), in which case \(K_1\) is monotone and there is exactly one outer witness;
- or \(K_2\) dips negative, and then there is an interior point with
  \[
  K_3=0,\qquad K_2<0.
  \]

This is the sharpest witness dichotomy we have so far.

## 4. Best concrete theorem targets now

This gives three clean theorem-level attacks.

First:

\[
\boxed{
K_2(q)\ge 0 \text{ on every genuine overlap interval } \mathcal I.
}
\]

If this holds, then \(K_1\) is monotone increasing, so the overlap lane collapses to a unique same-value / same-slope witness.

Second, weaker but still decisive:

\[
\boxed{
H_2(u)\le 0 \text{ on every same-value outer chord.}
}
\]

Then
\[
K_2(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_2(u)\,du \ge 0,
\]
so again \(K_1\) is monotone and there is a unique witness.

Third, even more local:

\[
\boxed{
\frac{\widehat Q'''(\xi)}{(\widehat Q'(\xi))^3}\le 0
\text{ at every outer-chord inflection witness } \widehat Q''(\xi)=0.
}
\]

This would at least block the only place where the positive part of \(H_2\) can emerge without paying the quadratic penalty, and it is probably the most plausible local theorem to try to extract next.

## 5. Honest status

I do **not** yet have a proof of any of those sign theorems.

So we still do **not** have a finished theorem in hand.

But the live overlap lane is now much tighter than before. It is no longer just “an overlap interval exists.” It must support:

- a same-value / same-slope witness \(K_1=0\),
- hence an interior inflection point on the chord,
- and if the lane is not already monotone-unique, then a higher-rung witness \(K_3=0\) with \(K_2<0\).

That is a real reduction.

So the sharpest continuation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Attack the sign of }H_2(u)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4}
\text{ on same-value outer chords.}
}
\]

That is the strongest theorem-level attack I can see right now.

_Thought for 1m 48s_

Here is the sharpest attack I can make now.

The right move is to stop looking at \(K_2\) only as an integral or a transport derivative, and instead use the exact **Hermite contact factorization** that every \(K_1\)-witness carries. This packages the overlap lane much more tightly than the raw \(H_2\)-formula.

We are in the corrected live regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so there is a common value interval \(\mathcal I\subset \Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\), outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q,
\]
and the reciprocal-slope defects
\[
K_n(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}=J_1\partial_u J_n.
\]
A witness is a point \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) with
\[
K_1(q_*)=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*,
\qquad
\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]

Let me write
\[
u_0:=\phi_0(q_*),\qquad u_2:=\phi_2(q_*),\qquad d:=u_2-u_0>0,
\qquad p:=\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)\neq 0.
\]

Now define the affine interpolant
\[
L(u):=q_*+p(u-u_0).
\]
Then the remainder
\[
R(u):=\widehat Q(u)-L(u)
\]
satisfies
\[
R(u_0)=R'(u_0)=0,
\qquad
R(u_2)=R'(u_2)=0.
\]

Because \(R\) is analytic, this gives the exact factorization
\[
\boxed{
\widehat Q(u)=q_*+p(u-u_0)+(u-u_0)^2(u-u_2)^2\,S(u),
}
\]
for an analytic function \(S\) on the chord.

This is the key new reduction.

It says every same-value / same-slope outer witness is encoded by a single analytic coefficient \(S\), after stripping off the universal double-endpoint contact. So the overlap lane is no longer best viewed as “some transport issue” or even mainly as an \(H_2\)-sign problem. It is a **quartic contact coefficient problem**.

That already sharpens the geometry a lot.

Now the first useful consequence is at the endpoints. Differentiating the factorization gives
\[
\widehat Q''(u_0)=2d^2\,S(u_0),
\qquad
\widehat Q''(u_2)=2d^2\,S(u_2).
\]
Therefore
\[
K_2(q_*)=\Xi(u_0)-\Xi(u_2)
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(u_0)-\widehat Q''(u_2)}{p^3}
=
\frac{2d^2}{p^3}\bigl(S(u_0)-S(u_2)\bigr).
\]

So the sign of \(K_2\) at a witness is **exactly** the sign of the endpoint asymmetry of \(S\). In particular,

\[
\boxed{
K_2(q_*)<0
\iff
S(u_0)<S(u_2)
\quad\text{(assuming }p>0\text{; reversed if }p<0\text{).}
}
\]

This is much sharper than the earlier integral phrasing. A negative dip of \(K_2\) is not some diffuse chord phenomenon. It is simply endpoint asymmetry of the quartic coefficient \(S\).

That is the first real theorem-level gain from this round.

There is a second gain. Because
\[
R(u)=(u-u_0)^2(u-u_2)^2S(u),
\]
repeated Rolle now becomes very transparent. Since \(R\) has double zeros at both endpoints, there are at least two interior zeros of \(R''\), hence at least two interior points where
\[
\widehat Q''(u)=0,
\]
and at least one interior point where
\[
\widehat Q'''(u)=0.
\]
So every overlap witness actually carries a stronger local package than I had stated before:

\[
\boxed{
u_0<u^-<u^0<u^+<u_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q''(u^-)=\widehat Q''(u^+)=0,
\qquad
\widehat Q'''(u^0)=0.
}
\]

So a same-value / same-slope witness is not just bracketing one inflection point. It forces a little Hermite-Rolle package inside the chord.

That is a significant refinement.

Now let me connect this back to the earlier \(H_2\)-attack. I had
\[
H_2(u)=\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
-
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4},
\qquad
K_2(q)=-\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H_2(u)\,du.
\]
The quartic factorization now explains why this formula was only half the story. The true remaining issue is not simply sign of \(H_2\) on the chord. It is whether the analytic coefficient \(S\) can have endpoint asymmetry of the forbidden sign.

So the cleanest next theorem target becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove a sign/monotonicity law for the witness coefficient }S
\text{ along an outer same-value / same-slope chord.}
}
\]

There are two concrete versions of this.

First, a **coherence theorem**:
if \(S(u_0)\ge S(u_2)\) always, then \(K_2(q_*)\ge 0\) at every witness. Since \(K_2>0\) near both ends of \(\mathcal I\), this would force \(K_1\) to be monotone increasing and collapse the overlap lane to a unique witness.

Second, an even stronger **rigidity theorem**:
if \(S(u_0)=S(u_2)\) at a witness, then perhaps the quartic factorization becomes symmetric enough to force higher-rung agreement, maybe even the translated-shell replication branch mentioned in the note.【181†source】 I do not have that proof, but the factorization shows that \(S\) is the right scalar to analyze if one wants such a rigidity statement.

So the overlap lane has now been sharpened to this:

- open overlap forces a same-value / same-slope witness;
- every such witness admits an exact quartic Hermite factorization
  \[
  \widehat Q(u)=q_*+p(u-u_0)+(u-u_0)^2(u-u_2)^2S(u);
  \]
- the next transport defect \(K_2\) is exactly endpoint asymmetry of \(S\);
- the chord also contains two inflection witnesses and one third-derivative witness.

That is a much tighter witness package than anything I had before.

The strongest new structural finding is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The live overlap lane has been reduced to a quartic-contact witness problem, with }K_2\text{ measuring the endpoint asymmetry of the analytic coefficient }S.
}
\]

This is a real sharpening. It turns the transport lane from a vague “same-value chord issue” into a concrete local analytic factorization problem along the chord.

So the sharpest continuation from here is:

attack the sign or monotonicity of \(S\) along outer same-value / same-slope chords. If we can show
\[
S(u_0)\ge S(u_2)
\]
at every witness, then \(K_2\ge 0\), \(K_1\) is monotone, and the entire overlap lane collapses to one canonical witness. That is, I think, the strongest attack currently available.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 02:06:37)

I’m pushing directly on the new quartic-contact coefficient \(S\) now. The goal is to stop treating it as an abstract witness scalar and derive explicit endpoint formulas and integral identities that force what a negative \(K_2\) witness would actually have to look like.

_Thought for 25s_

Here is the next attack.

The quartic Hermite factorization is strong enough that we can now compute the witness data explicitly and see exactly what a bad \(K_2\)-dip would force.

We are in the corrected overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
and at a same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\) we write
\[
u_0:=\phi_0(q_*),\qquad u_2:=\phi_2(q_*),\qquad d:=u_2-u_0>0,
\qquad p:=\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)\neq 0.
\]
Then with
\[
t:=u-u_0,
\]
the exact quartic Hermite factorization is
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_*+pt+t^2(t-d)^2S(t),
\]
where \(S\) is analytic on the chord. This is now the right local normal form for the overlap witness.

The key is that \(K_2\) and the next transport data can be written explicitly in terms of endpoint jets of \(S\).

Start from
\[
F(t):=t^2(t-d)^2=t^4-2dt^3+d^2t^2.
\]
Then
\[
F''(0)=2d^2,\qquad F'''(0)=-12d,\qquad
F''(d)=2d^2,\qquad F'''(d)=12d.
\]
Using \(\widehat Q=q_*+pt+FS\), we get the endpoint formulas
\[
\widehat Q''(u_0)=2d^2S(0),\qquad \widehat Q''(u_2)=2d^2S(d),
\]
and
\[
\widehat Q'''(u_0)=-12d\,S(0)+6d^2S'(0),
\]
\[
\widehat Q'''(u_2)=12d\,S(d)+6d^2S'(d).
\]

So the first nontrivial transport defect at the witness is
\[
K_2(q_*)=\Xi(u_0)-\Xi(u_2)
=\frac{\widehat Q''(u_0)-\widehat Q''(u_2)}{p^3}
=
\frac{2d^2}{p^3}\bigl(S(0)-S(d)\bigr).
\]

That formula is exact. It means:

\[
\boxed{
K_2(q_*)<0
\iff
S(0)<S(d)
\quad\text{if }p>0
}
\]
and the inequality reverses if \(p<0\). So a negative \(K_2\)-dip is **exactly** endpoint growth of the quartic coefficient along the chord.

That is already much more precise than the earlier \(H_2\)-average language.

Now push one rung further.

Since
\[
J_2(u)=-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
at a witness where \(\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)=p\), differentiation gives
\[
K_3(q_*)=J_3(u_2)-J_3(u_0)
=
\frac{1}{p}\Bigl(J_2'(u_2)-J_2'(u_0)\Bigr).
\]
Using
\[
J_2'(u)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q'''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
+
3\frac{(\widehat Q''(u))^2}{(\widehat Q'(u))^4},
\]
we obtain after substitution the exact endpoint expression
\[
K_3(q_*)=
\frac{12d}{p^4}\bigl(S(0)+S(d)\bigr)
+
\frac{6d^2}{p^4}\bigl(S'(d)-S'(0)\bigr)
+
\frac{12d^4}{p^5}\bigl(S(d)^2-S(0)^2\bigr).
\]

This is messy, but conceptually very useful. It says:

- \(K_2\) only sees the endpoint **difference** \(S(0)-S(d)\);
- \(K_3\) sees the endpoint **sum** \(S(0)+S(d)\), the endpoint derivative difference \(S'(d)-S'(0)\), and a quadratic correction in the same endpoint asymmetry.

So if the overlap lane is non-monotone — meaning \(K_2<0\) somewhere and then \(K_3=0\) at an interior extremum of \(K_2\) — the quartic coefficient \(S\) must satisfy a very specific endpoint constraint. It is no longer just “some transport issue.” It is an explicit compatibility equation on the endpoint jets of \(S\).

That is the first major new sharpening.

There is a second one.

Because
\[
R(t):=\widehat Q(u)-q_*-pt = t^2(t-d)^2S(t),
\]
the chord witness forces not only double contact at the endpoints, but also Rolle structure inside the chord. In particular, \(R''\) has at least two zeros in \((0,d)\), and \(R'''\) has at least one zero. Since
\[
R''=\widehat Q'' ,\qquad R'''=\widehat Q''',
\]
every witness chord contains
- at least two interior zeros of \(\widehat Q''\),
- at least one interior zero of \(\widehat Q'''\).

So if the overlap lane survives, it is already forcing a same-value / same-slope outer pair together with a two-inflection / one-third-derivative witness package in the interior. That is much stronger than the earlier “one inflection point” statement.

This leads to a very concrete no-go strategy.

A negative \(K_2\)-dip means \(S(d)>S(0)\) (assuming \(p>0\)). But an interior extremum of \(K_2\) gives \(K_3=0\), hence the explicit identity above. So any genuinely bad overlap would require endpoint growth of \(S\) together with the endpoint-jet balance
\[
\frac{12d}{p^4}\bigl(S(0)+S(d)\bigr)
+
\frac{6d^2}{p^4}\bigl(S'(d)-S'(0)\bigr)
+
\frac{12d^4}{p^5}\bigl(S(d)^2-S(0)^2\bigr)=0.
\]

That is now the exact compatibility equation for a non-monotone witness.

So the sharpest theorem-level targets become:

First, a **witness coefficient monotonicity theorem**:
\[
S(0)\ge S(d)
\]
at every same-value / same-slope outer witness. That would imply \(K_2\ge 0\), hence \(K_1\) monotone, hence the whole overlap lane collapses to a unique witness.

Second, a weaker but still decisive **endpoint-jet incompatibility theorem**:
show that if \(S(d)>S(0)\), then the \(K_3=0\) compatibility equation above cannot hold. That would rule out every negative dip of \(K_2\), and again force \(K_2\ge 0\).

Third, a still more local variant:
if \(S\) has endpoint growth of the bad sign, then the required interior Rolle package for \(\widehat Q'',\widehat Q'''\) is incompatible with the endpoint derivatives \(S'(0),S'(d)\). That would also block non-monotone overlap.

So the overlap lane has now been reduced to an explicit endpoint-jet problem for one analytic function \(S\). That is the strongest reduction I have so far.

The biggest new structural finding is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{At a same-value / same-slope overlap witness, }K_2\text{ and }K_3\text{ are explicit endpoint-jet invariants of the quartic coefficient }S.
}
\]

So the live overlap lane is no longer best viewed as a diffuse transport family. It is a quartic-contact witness with a concrete endpoint-jet compatibility law. That is much more attackable.

The sharpest continuation is now:

- use the exact formulas
  \[
  K_2=\frac{2d^2}{p^3}\bigl(S(0)-S(d)\bigr),
  \]
  \[
  K_3=
  \frac{12d}{p^4}\bigl(S(0)+S(d)\bigr)
  +
  \frac{6d^2}{p^4}\bigl(S'(d)-S'(0)\bigr)
  +
  \frac{12d^4}{p^5}\bigl(S(d)^2-S(0)^2\bigr),
  \]
- and try to prove either \(S(0)\ge S(d)\) always, or that \(S(d)>S(0)\) is incompatible with the \(K_3=0\) relation forced by an interior negative dip of \(K_2\).

That is the strongest math attack I can see right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 02:11:12)

_Thought for 2m 1s_

Here is a sharper attack.

The quartic-contact factorization is strong enough that we can now eliminate the auxiliary \(S\)-language and express the next defect **directly** as an integral of \(\widehat Q'''\) over the witness chord. That is cleaner and gives the most concrete remaining target I’ve seen.

We are in the corrected live regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so on the common value interval \(\mathcal I\) of the two outer runs we have inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to J_0,J_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]
A witness is a point \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) with
\[
K_1(q_*)=0,
\]
equivalently a same-value / same-slope outer pair
\[
u_0:=\phi_0(q_*),\qquad u_2:=\phi_2(q_*),\qquad
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2)=q_*,
\qquad
\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)=:p\neq 0.
\]

As before, write \(d:=u_2-u_0>0\), \(t:=u-u_0\), and factor
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_*+pt+t^2(t-d)^2S(t).
\]
From this we already had
\[
K_2(q_*)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u_0)-\widehat Q''(u_2)}{p^3}
=\frac{2d^2}{p^3}\bigl(S(0)-S(d)\bigr).
\]

The important new step is that this can be rewritten with no \(S\) at all:

\[
\widehat Q''(u_2)-\widehat Q''(u_0)=\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'''(u)\,du,
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
K_2(q_*)=-\frac{1}{p^3}\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'''(u)\,du.
}
\]

This is exact.

So a negative \(K_2\)-dip is **exactly** the statement that the total third-derivative mass on the witness chord has the “wrong” sign. Assuming \(p>0\),
\[
K_2(q_*)<0
\iff
\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'''(u)\,du>0.
\]

That is much sharper than the earlier \(H_2\)-integral formulation. It says the first nontrivial overlap defect is nothing but the signed chord integral of \(\widehat Q'''\).

There is an even better refinement. Because
\[
2d^2S(0)=\widehat Q''(u_0),\qquad
2d^2S(d)=\widehat Q''(u_2),
\]
integration by parts gives the weighted moment formulas
\[
\boxed{
2d^3S(d)=\int_{u_0}^{u_2}(u-u_0)\,\widehat Q'''(u)\,du,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
-2d^3S(0)=\int_{u_0}^{u_2}(u_2-u)\,\widehat Q'''(u)\,du.
}
\]

So the endpoint coefficient asymmetry is literally the first moment asymmetry of \(\widehat Q'''\) along the chord. In particular,
\[
S(d)-S(0)=\frac{1}{2d^2}\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'''(u)\,du.
\]

This is a real improvement. It means a bad overlap witness does **not** just require “some transport issue.” It requires that \(\widehat Q'''\), along the witness chord, has enough positive total mass to bias the quartic coefficient toward the right endpoint.

Now combine this with the Rolle package already forced by a witness. Since
\[
R(u):=\widehat Q(u)-q_*-p(u-u_0)
\]
has double zeros at \(u_0\) and \(u_2\), we know:

- \(\widehat Q''=R''\) has at least two interior zeros on \((u_0,u_2)\),
- \(\widehat Q'''=R'''\) has at least one interior zero on \((u_0,u_2)\).

So any bad \(K_2\)-witness must satisfy both:

1. \(\widehat Q'''\) changes sign on the chord, because it has an interior zero;
2. nevertheless its **total mass**
   \[
   \int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'''(u)\,du
   \]
   has the sign needed to make \(K_2<0\).

That is now the sharpest concrete obstruction I can state:

\[
\boxed{
\text{A bad overlap witness is exactly a same-value / same-slope outer chord on which }\widehat Q''' \text{ has an interior zero but still carries the wrong signed total mass.}
}
\]

This suggests the best next theorem target is no longer “prove \(H_2\le 0\)” in full generality. The more direct target is:

\[
\boxed{
\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'''(u)\,du \le 0
\quad\text{for every same-value / same-slope outer witness chord }[u_0,u_2].
}
\]

If that holds, then \(K_2(q_*)\ge 0\) at every witness. Since \(K_2>0\) near both ends of \(\mathcal I\), it follows that \(K_1\) is monotone increasing, so the overlap lane collapses to exactly one witness.

There is also a weaker but probably more attackable version:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove a one-sign or one-crossing theorem for }\widehat Q''' \text{ on every witness chord.}
}
\]

Because once \(\widehat Q'''\) has only one sign change and a controlled sign orientation, its total mass becomes sign-constrained. That would also force \(K_2\ge 0\).

So the sharpened attack is now this:

- stay in the corrected overlap regime \(B_0+B_2>B_1\);
- use \(K_1=0\) to isolate a same-value / same-slope outer witness chord;
- replace the abstract \(K_2\)-defect by the exact signed mass formula
  \[
  K_2=-p^{-3}\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'''(u)\,du;
  \]
- try to prove that this signed third-derivative mass cannot be positive on such a chord.

That is the cleanest and strongest continuation I can see right now.
